Mistaken Gender in Purchasing a Slave
Ruling on buying a slave with mistaken gender

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules a sale invalid when the slave's gender is mistaken, distinguish between qiyas and istihsan in this case, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, mistaking a slave's gender invalidates the sale (istihsan), unlike animals where only an option is given.

This lesson examines a specific dispute within the Hanafi school: what happens when someone buys a slave believing them to be one gender, but discovers the opposite. While analogical reasoning (qiyas) on animals would grant the buyer an option to rescind, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) applies juristic preference (istihsan) and declares the sale void. Understanding this case illustrates how istihsan overrides qiyas when human purposes differ fundamentally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the dispute is between the view of Zufar (sale valid with khiyar) and the majority of Hanafi imams (sale invalid).
The majority includes Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Understand the qiyas-based argument
Examine the analogy with animals: purchasing a ram that turns out to be a ewe gives the buyer an option (khiyar) because gender is a description.
Zufar extends this analogy to human slaves.
3
Analyze the istihsan counter-argument
See why the Hanafi majority considers human genders as different types (jinsān), not mere descriptions, due to vastly different legal and social purposes.
Examples include marriage, inheritance, and testimony rules.
4
Apply the ruling to hire (ijara)
Note that the same principle applies to contracts of hire: invalid conditions render the contract void.
This is mentioned at the end of the passage.
5
Conclude with the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is that the sale is invalid (batil) by istihsan; Zufar's view is not followed.
Al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir confirms this tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمة تبين إلخ أي لو اشترى شخصا على أنه أمة فتبين أنه عبد أو على أنه عبد، فإذا هي أمة لم يجز البيع استحساناً، والقياس أنه يجوز، وهو قول زفر؛ لأنه اختلاف الوصف؛ إذ الذكورة والأنوثة وصف في الحيوان، وهو يوجب الخيار لا الفساد كما في البهائم، فإنه لو اشترى كبشاً مثلا فإذا هو نعجة أو بالعكس لا يفسد البيع، وإنما يثبت الخيار للمشتري؛ لفوات الوصف المرغوب فيه وجه الاستحسان أن الذكر والأنثى من بني آدم جنسان مختلفان؛ لتفاحش التفاوت في المقاصد والأغراض، فلا يصح البيع، بخلاف البهائم، والإجارة كالبيع فإنها تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة رمز الحقائق: ۳۷/۲] (فتح)
And a female slave that turns out to be male, etc.: meaning if a person buys a person thinking it is a female slave, but it turns out to be a male slave, or he buys thinking it is a male slave but it turns out to be a female, the sale is not permissible by istihsan. By qiyas, it would be permissible, which is the view of Zufar; because it is a difference in description; since masculinity and femininity are descriptions in animals, which would give an option (khiyar) not invalidity, as in animals. For example, if someone buys a ram but finds it is a ewe, or vice versa, the sale is not invalidated; rather, the buyer gets the option due to loss of a desired description. The basis of istihsan is that the male and female of the human species are different genders due to the vast difference in objectives and purposes, so the sale is not valid, unlike animals. And hire (ijara) is like sale; it becomes void due to invalid conditions. (Fath)
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific case: a person buys a slave believing the slave to be of one gender, but discovers the opposite gender. The author, from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, presents two positions. By qiyas (analogy) with animals, the sale would be valid but the buyer would have an option (khiyar) due to the missing desired attribute, because in animals gender is considered a description (wasf), not a difference in essence. This is the view of Zufar. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the sale is invalid altogether because in humans, the genders differ so greatly in purposes and objectives that they are considered different types (jinsān mukhtalifān). The text also notes that the same principle applies to contracts of hire (ijāra): they are invalidated by corrupt conditions.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: setting aside qiyas for a more suitable ruling based on a stronger evidence or need.
خيار (khiyar): Option or right to rescind a contract due to a defect or missing attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if someone buys a slave thinking it is male but finds it is female, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid and the buyer has the option to keep or return
B) The sale is invalid (batil)
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer agrees
D) The sale is valid but the price must be adjusted

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale of a slave with mistaken gender is invalid due to istihsan, because human genders are considered different types with vastly different purposes.

Buying Back a Sold Item at a Lower Price Before Full Payment
Ruling on buying back sold item at lower price before full payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buying back a sold item at a lower price before full payment, identify the hadith evidence, and explain why the Shafi'i school differs.

Core Idea
Buying back a credit-sold item at a lower price before full payment is haram because it is disguised riba.

This lesson examines a common practice: a seller who has not yet received full payment buys back the item from the buyer at a discount. The Hanafi school strictly prohibits this, citing a hadith of Aisha and a clear qiyas to riba. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, permits it. We will dissect the evidence and understand the legal reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and its timing
Clarify the scenario: A sells an item on credit, then before B pays the full price, A buys it back from B for less than the original price. The key is the timing: before full payment.
The ruling changes if the price is fully paid first; then it is permissible by consensus.
2
State the Hanafi ruling and its basis
The Hanafi school rules this transaction invalid (haram). The proof is the hadith of Aisha: she severely warned Zayd ibn Arqam who engaged in such a deal, threatening that Allah would void his Hajj and jihad. This indicates the contract is void.
The severity of the threat shows it is not merely disliked but prohibited.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i permits it, analogizing to buying with a garment of lesser value. The Hanafi response: the reduction here is effectively a discount on the debt for early payment, which is riba. The value of the sold item is fixed by the original price, so any reduction before full payment is riba.
The debate hinges on whether this is a new independent sale or a disguised settlement of the debt.
4
Understand the conditions for permissibility
If the buyer repurchases at the same price or higher, it is permissible by consensus because there is no reduction. Similarly, if the buyer has already paid the full price, a buyback at a lower price is a new sale and permissible.
Consensus exists in these two cases, indicating the prohibition is specific to the reduction before payment.
5
Apply the ruling to modern contexts
Identify modern equivalents: credit sales where the seller offers a discount for early payment, or buy-back arrangements in financing. These often fall under the same prohibition if structured as a sale with a reduction before full payment.
Care must be taken to distinguish genuine sales from hidden interest-bearing loans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشراء ما باع إلخ: أي لم يجز شراء ما باع بأقل من الثمن الأول قبل أن يقبض البائع كل الثمن مطلقاً، سواء كان شراه لنفسه أو شراه من لا تصح شهادته له، وسواء باع بنفسه أو باع وكيله وقال الشافعي: يجوز كما لو اشتراه بثوب قيمته أقل مما باعه ولنا قول عائشة الاهلها وهو أن امرأة دخلت على عائشة فقالت: إني اشتريت من زيد بن أرقم جارية إلى العطاء بثمان مائة درهم ثم بعتها منه بست مائة درهم قبل محل الأجل، فقالت عائشة: بئس ما شريت و بئس ما اشتريت أبلغي زيد بن أرقم أن الله تعالى أبطل حجه وجهاده مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إن لم يتب عن هذه، فأتاها زيد بن أرقم معتذراً، فتلت قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ جَاءَهُ مَوْعِظَةٌ مِنْ رَبِّهِ فَانْتَهَى فَلَهُ مَا سَلَفَ ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٧٥) فهذا الوعيد الشديد دليل على فساد العقد، ولأنهما اصطلحا على أن مالية المبيع مقدرة بثمن معين، فإذا اشتراه بأقل منه مع بقاء هذا التقدير يكون رباً، وأما إذا اشتراه بمثل الثمن الأول أو بالزيادة، فيجوز إجماعاً؛ لعدم الربا، فإن الربح هنا يحصل للمشتري، والمبيع قد دخل بضمانه، وكذا لو اشترى بالأقل بعد نقد الثمن يجوز إجماعاً.
And buying what one sold, etc.: meaning it is not permissible to buy what one sold for less than the first price before the seller receives the full price absolutely, whether he buys it for himself or buys it from someone whose testimony is not valid for him, and whether he sold it himself or his agent sold it. Al-Shafi'i said: it is permissible, just as if he bought it with a garment whose value is less than the price he sold it for. Our evidence: the saying of Aisha رضي الله عنها, which is that a woman entered upon Aisha and said: I bought a slave girl from Zayd ibn Arqam for eight hundred dirhams on credit until the stipend, then I sold it back to him for six hundred dirhams before the due date. So Aisha said: Evil is what you bought, and evil is what you sold! Inform Zayd ibn Arqam that Allah has rendered void his Hajj and jihad with the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم if he does not repent from this. So Zayd ibn Arqam came to her apologizing, and she recited the verse: 'Then whoever comes to him an admonition from his Lord and desists, he shall have what has passed.' (2:275) This severe threat indicates the invalidity of the contract. Also because they agreed that the value of the sold item is estimated at a specific price, so if he buys it for less while this estimation remains, it becomes riba. As for buying it for the same first price or more, it is permissible by consensus, because there is no riba; the profit goes to the buyer, and the sold item has entered his liability. Likewise, if he buys it for less after payment of the price, it is permissible by consensus.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a person who sells an item on credit and then buys it back from the buyer before receiving full payment, at a lower price. The Hanafi school deems this transaction invalid because it resembles riba. The proof is a story narrated from Aisha: a woman bought a slave from Zayd ibn Arqam on deferred payment, then sold it back to him at a lower price before the due date. Aisha condemned this severely, citing that it voids his Hajj and jihad unless he repents. This indicates the contract is invalid. The rationale is that the value of the item is fixed by the original price, so buying it back at a lower price while the debt remains is like riba. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees and permits it, analogizing to buying with a garment of lesser value. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the threat from Aisha and the principle that a reduction on a debt in exchange for early payment is riba. If the purchase is at the same price or higher, it is permitted. Also, if the buyer pays the full price first and then sells it back at a lower price, it is permissible by consensus.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; any excess in exchange of same type of countervalue without proper compensation.
بيع العينة (bay' al-'aynah): A type of sale where one sells an item on credit then buys it back at a lower price for cash, resembling a loan with interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, if A sells a watch to B for $100 on credit, and before B pays, A buys it back from B for $80, the transaction is:
A) Permissible because both parties agree
B) Invalid because it involves riba
C) Permissible if A and B are not related
D) Permissible after three days

Key Takeaway
Buying back a credit-sold item at a lower price before full payment is haram in the Hanafi school because it is a disguised form of riba.

The Sale and Use of Camel Wool in Hanafi Law
A ruling on the permissibility of benefiting from items whose sale is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the sale of camel wool is invalid but its use is permissible, and explain the basis for this distinction in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Camel wool cannot be sold but can be used—invalidity of sale does not forbid benefit.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi tradition regarding camel wool. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists it among invalid sales, yet the commentary clarifies that benefiting from it is allowed. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping Hanafi principles of property (māl) and contractual validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling camel wool. According to the matn, it is an invalid sale.
The matn originally lists 'its wool' among items whose sale is invalid.
2
Identify the exception on benefit
The commentary explicitly adds that benefiting from the wool is permissible.
The phrase 'ويجوز الانتفاع' indicates permissibility of use.
3
Understand the rationale
In Hanafi law, camel wool is not considered māl mutaqawwim (valuable property) required for a valid sale, but its material use is not prohibited.
This distinction is consistent with Hanafi rulings on similar items like pork and alcohol.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووبرها: أي الإبل ويجوز الانتفاع بهذه الأشياء أيضاً وكذا بشعرها.
And its wool: i.e., of camels, and it is permissible to benefit from these things as well, and similarly from its hair.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary. The matn lists 'its wool' as one of the invalid sales. The commentary specifies that it refers to camel wool, and clarifies that although the sale is invalid, using the wool and hair for benefit is permissible. This is based on the Hanafi principle that items which are not māl mutaqawwim cannot be sold but their use is not prohibited.

Key Terms
وبرها (wabarihā): The wool or hair of a camel, mentioned in context of invalid sale.
مال (māl): Property or wealth that is considered legally valuable for contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling camel wool according to the commentary?
A) Permissible and beneficial
B) Invalid but benefiting is permissible
C) Invalid and not permissible to benefit
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Selling camel wool is invalid, but using it is permissible—a key distinction between contract invalidity and benefit permissibility.

Ruling on Selling the Upper Part of a Collapsed Building
Explains the invalidity of selling airspace rights due to non-property status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the airspace right of a collapsed building and distinguish it from water rights.

Core Idea
The right to build above is a right (haqq), not property (mal), so its sale is invalid.

In this passage, al-Haskafi explains why selling the upper part of a collapsed building is invalid. The key distinction is between property (māl) and mere rights. While water rights are considered property and can be sold, the right of elevation is not, leading to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being sold? The airspace right (haqq al-ta'li) after the building collapses. The ruling: the sale is invalid (fasid/batil).
2
Locate the legal reason (talil)
Why is it invalid? Because haqq al-ta'li is not considered property (mal) in Hanafi law, and the subject matter of a sale must be property.
3
Contrast with water rights
Water rights (sharb) are considered property because they are a tangible share of water that can be possessed and guaranteed. Hence their sale is valid, unlike airspace rights.
4
Understand the condition for validity
A sale contract requires the subject matter to be property (mal) capable of possession. If the subject matter is only a right, the contract is void from the outset or becomes void if the building collapses before delivery.
5
Apply to a scenario
Imagine the owner sells the upper part while the building is intact, then the building collapses before delivery. The sale becomes void because the subject matter (the physical building) no longer exists, leaving only the right which is not property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلو سقط : [ أي ولم يجز أيضاً بيع علو؛ لأن حق التعلي ليس بمال ومحل البيع مال (عيني)] علو الدار بالضم والكسر ضد السفل، أي إذا كان علو لأحد وسفل لآخر فسقطا أو سقط العلو وبقي السفل فباع صاحب العلو موضع العلو لم يجز، فإن سقط العلو بعد البيع قبل القبض بطل البيع؛ لأن له حق التعلي لا غير وهو ليس بمال ومحل البيع المال، وهو ما يمكن إحرازه، وإنما يصح بيعه قبل الانهدام باعتبار البناء القائم ولم يبق، بخلاف الشرب الذي من الماء حيث يصح بيعه تبعاً للأرض باتفاق الروايات، ومقصوداً في رواية، وهو اختيار مشايخ بلخ؛ لأنه نصيب من الماء وهو مال، ولهذا يضمن بالإتلاف، حتى لو سقى به رجل أرضه يضمن قيمته رمز الحقائق: ۳۷/۲] (فتح، شرح الوقاية)
And the upper part of a collapsed building: meaning, it is not permissible to sell the upper part because the right of elevation is not property, and the object of sale is property. The upper part of a house (pronounced with damm or kasr) is opposite of the lower part. So if there is an upper part belonging to one person and a lower part to another, and they collapse, or the upper part collapses while the lower remains, then if the owner of the upper part sells the place of the upper part, it is not permissible. If the upper part collapses after the sale but before delivery, the sale becomes void because he only has the right of elevation, which is not property, and the object of sale is property, which is something that can be possessed. It was only valid before collapse due to the existing building, which no longer remains. This is unlike the right to irrigation water, which can be sold as a subsidiary to land by agreement of all narrations, and even as a primary object in some narrations, and this is the choice of the scholars of Balkh because it is a share of water which is property; therefore, it is guaranteed if destroyed, so if a man irrigates his land with it, he must guarantee its value.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's phrase 'علو سقط'. The commentary explains that the right to build above a property (haqq al-ta'li) is not considered property (māl) in Hanafi fiqh, and since a sale contract requires the object to be property, selling this right alone is invalid. If the building collapses before delivery, the sale becomes void because the subject matter is gone. An exception is made for water rights (sharb), which are considered property and can be sold either with the land or independently according to some narrations. This distinction is because water is a tangible share and is compensable if destroyed. The reference to Rami al-Haqa'iq and Fath al-Qadir shows the standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
علو (‘uluw): The upper floor or part of a building.
حق التعلي (haqq al-ta‘lī): The right to build an upper story or to use the airspace above a property.
شرب (sharb): The right to use water for irrigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling the upper part of a collapsed building invalid?
A) Because the building is destroyed
B) Because the right of elevation is not property
C) Because it causes dispute
D) Because it is riba

Key Takeaway
The right of elevation (haqq al-ta'li) is not property, so its independent sale is invalid; unlike water rights which are property and can be sold.

Selling Watercourses and Pathways
Ruling on sale of watercourse and pathway with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings on selling a watercourse (invalid) and a pathway (valid), and state the conditions and intra-madhhab dispute regarding the sale of the right of passage.

Core Idea
Gharar voids the sale of watercourses; specification validates the sale of pathways.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq tackles a common property transaction issue: can you sell a watercourse or a right of passage? The Hanafi school distinguishes between physical property and mere rights, with important implications for contract validity. Understanding this ruling helps avoid voidable agreements due to uncertainty (gharar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objects
Note the text addresses two separate items: a watercourse (masīl) and a pathway (ṭarīq). The ruling differs for each.
The watercourse is a channel for water flow; the pathway is a physical right of way.
2
Determine the ruling for each
The text states a watercourse cannot be sold or gifted, while a pathway can be sold or gifted.
The ruling for watercourse is fasid (invalid); for pathway it is sahih (valid) with conditions.
3
Understand the reason for invalidity of watercourse
The watercourse is majhūl (unknown) because its capacity to hold water is not known, leading to gharar (uncertainty) which invalidates the sale.
Gharar in the object of sale is prohibited.
4
Understand the conditions for pathway
A pathway sale is valid whether or not its boundaries are specified. If unspecified, the width defaults to the largest door of the adjoining house.
Specification or estimability removes gharar.
5
Distinguish between selling the pathway and selling the right alone
Selling the right of passage (haqq al-murūr) along with the land is valid by consensus. Selling the right alone is disputed: some Hanafi narrations prohibit it because rights cannot be sold individually.
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that selling rights alone is not permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسيل: أي لا يجوز بيع مسيل الماء ولا هبته؛ لأن المسيل مجهول؛ إذ لا يعلم قدر ما يشغله الماء، ويجوز بيع الطريق وهبته سواء حد أو لا، وإذا لم يبين يقدر بعرض باب الدار العظمى، وصح بيع حق المرور تبعاً للأرض بالإجماع، ووحده في رواية ابن سماعة، وفي رواية "الزيادات": لا يجوز؛ لأنه حق من الحقوق، وبيع الحقوق بالانفراد لا يجوز.
And the watercourse: meaning, it is not permissible to sell a watercourse nor give it as a gift, because the watercourse is unknown (majhūl) since one does not know the amount of water it can hold. And it is permissible to sell a pathway and give it as a gift, whether it is specified or not. If it is not specified, it is estimated by the width of the largest door of the house. And the sale of the right of passage is valid as a subsidiary to the land by consensus. But selling it alone is in one narration from Ibn Sami'a not permissible, and in the narration of al-Ziyadat also not permissible because it is a right among rights, and selling rights individually is not permissible.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: watercourses and pathways. A watercourse (masīl) is the channel through which water flows. Its sale is invalid because the capacity is unknown (majhūl), leading to uncertainty (gharar) prohibited in sales. A pathway (ṭarīq) or right of passage is saleable because it can be specified. If not specified, the default width is the largest door of the house. However, selling the right of passage alone (without the land) is disputed: the majority allow it as a subsidiary, but some narrations from Ibn Sami'a and al-Ziyadat prohibit it because it is a mere right. The relied-upon position is that selling rights in isolation is not allowed, but selling the pathway itself (as a physical space) is allowed.

Key Terms
مسيل (masīl): A watercourse or channel for water flow.
طريق (ṭarīq): A pathway or right of way.
حق المرور (haqq al-murūr): The right of passage over someone else's land.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling a watercourse invalid?
A) Because water is not property
B) Because it is unknown how much water it can hold
C) Because it belongs to the public
D) Because it is riba

Key Takeaway
A watercourse is not saleable due to unknown capacity; a pathway is saleable, and the right of passage is only transferable with the land.

10 min
Buying Back for Less Before Full Payment: Ruling and Exceptions
Prohibits buying back sold goods for less before full payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on buying back sold goods for less before full payment, identify the evidence and its wujūh al-dalālah, and apply the exceptions (same price, after payment, physical defect, different currency).

Core Idea
Buying back what you sold for less before full payment is prohibited as it constitutes ribā.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqā’iq examines a transaction that might appear innocent but hides a common form of ribā: the sale-and-buyback at a discount. The Hanafi school, citing a powerful hadith from ‘Ā’ishah, rules such a sale invalid (fāsid). Beyond the core prohibition, the text outlines several exceptions and extensions that test your understanding of the underlying ‘illah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core act and its ruling
Buying back an item one sold for less than the original price before the full price is paid. The Hanafi ruling: not permissible (fāsid).
This is the central ruling of the passage.
2
Locate the primary evidence
The hadith of Zayd ibn Arqam as reported by ‘Ā’ishah (ra). The severe threat ('nullifies Hajj and jihad') indicates the contract is invalid and a major sin.
The evidence is a Sunnah ahad, zanni in transmission but qat'i in indication.
3
Understand the ‘illah (effective cause)
The buyer and seller had agreed on a specific valuation (the original price). Buying back for less while that valuation stands creates an excess without compensation, i.e., ribā.
The ‘illah is the continuing valuation of the item at the original price.
4
Apply the exceptions
Permissible exceptions: (a) buying for the same or more, (b) after full payment, (c) if the item has physically deteriorated, (d) using a different currency. Market price decline alone does not permit the discount.
These exceptions confirm that the prohibition is tied to the original price agreement.
5
Compare opposing views
Al-Shāfi‘ī permits it, analogizing to barter. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it only if buying from free individuals (not slaves/mukatab) whose testimony is not accepted.
The Hanafi majority view (Imam Abu Hanifa) is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشراء ما باع إلخ: أي لم يجز شراء ما باع بأقل من الثمن الأول قبل أن يقبض البائع كل الثمن مطلقاً، سواء كان شراه لنفسه أو شراه من لا تصح شهادته له، وسواء باع بنفسه أو باع وكيله وقال الشافعي: يجوز كما لو اشتراه بثوب قيمته أقل مما باعه ولنا قول عائشة الاهلها وهو أن امرأة دخلت على عائشة فقالت: إني اشتريت من زيد بن أرقم جارية إلى العطاء بثمان مائة درهم ثم بعتها منه بست مائة درهم قبل محل الأجل، فقالت عائشة هما: بئس ما شريت و بئس ما اشتريت أبلغي زيد بن أرقم أن الله تعالى أبطل حجه وجهاده مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إن لم يتب عن هذه، فأتاها زيد بن أرقم معتذراً، فتلت قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ جَاءَهُ مَوْعِظَةٌ مِنْ رَبِّهِ فَانْتَهَى فَلَهُ مَا سَلَفَ ﴾ (البقرة: ٢٧٥) فهذا الوعيد الشديد دليل على فساد العقد، ولأنهما اصطلحا على أن مالية المبيع مقدرة بثمن معين، فإذا اشتراه بأقل منه مع بقاء هذا التقدير يكون رباً، وأما إذا اشتراه بمثل الثمن الأول أو بالزيادة، فيجوز إجماعاً؛ لعدم الربا، فإن الربح هنا يحصل للمشتري، والمبيع قد دخل بضمانه، وكذا لو اشترى بالأقل بعد نقد الثمن يجوز إجماعاً. بالأقل قبل النقد، وشراء من لا تجوز شهادته له كالولد والوالد والعبد والمكاتب بمنزلة شراء البائع بنفسه، وقال أبو ومحمد: يجوز في غير العبد والمكاتب وشراء الوكيل وبيعه كبيعه بنفسه، وكذا لو اشترى من وارث مشتريه بأقل مما اشترى به المورث لم يجز؛ لقيام الوارث مقام المورث، ثم شرط عدم الجواز أن يكون الشراء من مشتريه أو من وارثه، لا من الموهوب له أو الموصى له، وأن يكون الثمنان متحدين جنساً، فإذا اشتراه بجنس آخر غير الثمن الأول يجوز، وإن كان الثمن الثاني أقل، والدنانير جنس الدراهم، وأما إذا تعيب المبيع ذاتاً في يد المشتري، فباعه البائع بأقل من الثمن جاز، ولو انتقصت قيمته بتغير السعر لا بنقصان الذات لم يجز شراؤه بالأقل.
And buying what one sold etc.: meaning, it is not permissible to buy what one sold for less than the first price before the seller receives the full price absolutely, whether he buys it for himself or buys it from someone whose testimony is not valid for him, and whether he sold it himself or his agent sold it. Al-Shafi'i said: it is permissible, just as if he bought it with a garment whose value is less than what he sold it for. Our evidence is the statement of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that a woman came to Aisha and said: I bought a slave-girl from Zayd ibn Arqam on credit until the stipend for eight hundred dirhams, then I sold her back to him for six hundred dirhams before the term. Aisha said: 'How bad is what you bought and how bad is what you sold! Inform Zayd ibn Arqam that Allah has nullified his Hajj and jihad with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) if he does not repent from this.' So Zayd ibn Arqam came to her apologizing, and she recited the verse: 'But whoever is warned by his Lord and desists, then what has passed is for him' (2:275). This severe threat is evidence of the invalidity of the contract. And also because they agreed that the monetary value of the sold item is determined by a specific price, so if he buys it for less while this determination remains, it is riba. But if he buys it for the same price or more, it is permissible by consensus because there is no riba, as the profit goes to the buyer and the item has entered his guarantee. Similarly, if he buys it for less after paying the price, it is permissible by consensus. [And] with less before payment [it is not permissible]. And buying from someone whose testimony is not valid for him, like a child, parent, slave, or mukatab, is like the seller buying himself. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: it is permissible except for the slave and mukatab. And the sale of an agent is like his own sale. Similarly, if one buys from the heir of his buyer for less than what the deceased bought it for, it is not permissible because the heir stands in place of the deceased. Then the condition for non-permissibility is that the purchase is from his buyer or his heir, not from a donee or legatee, and that the two prices are of the same type. If he buys it with a different type than the first price, it is permissible even if the second price is less, and dinars are a type of dirhams. But if the sold item has physically deteriorated in the buyer's hand, and the seller buys it back for less, it is permissible. But if its value has only decreased due to market change, not physical deterioration, then buying it for less is not permissible.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This extensive passage covers the ruling on buying back what one sold for less than the original price before full payment. The Hanafi position is that it is absolutely forbidden because it resembles riba. The evidence is the strong hadith of Aisha regarding Zayd ibn Arqam. The reasoning is that the parties agreed on a valuation, so buying back for less while that valuation is still in effect constitutes riba. Exceptions include: buying for the same or more, buying after full payment, buying with a different currency, and cases where the item has physically depreciated. The ruling also extends to buying through intermediaries whose testimony is not accepted, and to heirs. There is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad make an exception for free individuals (not slaves or mukatabs). The reliance is on the majority view.

Key Terms
ربا (ribā): Usury or interest, specifically in sales, surplus without compensation.
بيع (bay‘): Sale contract.
ثمن (thaman): Price.
فاسد (fāsid): A contract that is legally defective but can be rectified; not void but invalid as is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, when is it permissible to buy back an item you sold for less than the original price?
A) Before payment if you use a different currency
B) After full payment
C) Anytime with mutual consent
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Buying back sold goods for less before full payment is prohibited (fāsid) because it constitutes ribā; only after full payment or due to physical defect/currency change is a discount permissible.

The Principle of Non-Spreading of Invalidity in a Mixed Sale
Ruling on a mixed sale with one invalid item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mixed sale where one item is invalid and explain why the invalidity does not spread to the valid part.

Core Idea
Invalidity does not spread when it is weak due to ikhtilaf.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a valid and an invalid sale are combined, the invalidity does not automatically taint the entire contract. This lesson examines a specific case where a buyer sells back an item along with an additional item before paying the original price. The sale is valid for the added item but invalid for the original because of a price imbalance. However, the invalidity is confined to that part, because the matter is subject to scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf). Understanding this principle helps prevent the misconception that any defect voids the whole transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the passage and note the transaction: a buyer purchases a slave girl for 1000, then sells her back to the original seller along with another slave girl for 1000 before paying the first price.
Focus on the timing: the first price remains unpaid.
2
Pinpoint the valid and invalid parts
According to the text, the sale is valid for the added slave girl (the one not originally purchased from the seller) and invalid for the original slave girl.
The invalidity arises because some of the price must be allocated to the added item, making the buyer effectively repurchase the original for less than he sold it — a form of riba.
3
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The text states 'it is weak in it, being a matter of ijtihad' because Imam al-Shafi'i permits the entire transaction. This weakness prevents the invalidity from spreading (lā yashī‘u al-fasād).
The principle: fasād does not spread when the ruling is subject to legitimate ijtihad.
4
Note the Shafi'i counter-position and Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position isolates the invalidity; the muftā bihi is that the valid part stands alone. The Shafi'ī view considers the whole sale permissible.
The tarjīḥ is based on the weakness of the proof for spreading the invalidity in the presence of ikhtilāf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح فيما ضم إليه: أي إلى المشترى - بفتح الراء - بأن اشترى مثلاً جارية بألف وقبضها، ثم باعها المشتري وضم الأخرى معها بألف من البائع قبل نقد الثمن الأول، جاز البيع في التي لم يشترها من البائع، ويفسد في الأخرى؛ لأنه لابد أن يجعل بعض الثمن بمقابلة التي لم يشترها منه، فيكون مشترياً للأخرى بأقل مما باع، وهو فاسد، ولا يشيع الفساد؛ لأنه ضعيف فيها، لكونه مجتهداً فيه؛ لأن الشافعي يقول بجوازه، حتى لو قضى القاضي بجوازه صح.
And it is valid in what is added to it: i.e., to the purchased item (with fatha on the ra). For example, if one buys a slave girl for a thousand and takes possession, then the buyer sells her and adds another (slave girl) with her for a thousand from the seller before paying the first price, then the sale is valid for the one that was not purchased from the seller, and invalid for the other; because some of the price must be allocated to the one he did not purchase from him, so he becomes a buyer of the other for less than what he sold, which is invalid. And the invalidity does not spread because it is weak in it, being a matter of ijtihad, since al-Shafi'i allows it, so if a judge rules on its validity, it is valid.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a buyer sells back an item along with an additional item to the original seller before the first price is paid. The sale is valid for the additional item (which was not originally sold) but invalid for the original item because it results in buying back for less. However, the invalidity does not spread to the whole contract because there is ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) on this point; Shafi'i permits it, so it is not clearly invalid. Therefore, the valid part stands alone.

Key Terms
ضم إليه (ḍumma ilayhi): Adding another item to a sale.
لا يشيع الفساد (lā yashī‘u al-fasād): The invalidity does not spread to other parts of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mixed sale example, why does the invalidity not spread to the entire contract?
A) Because both items are identical
B) Because the invalid part is subject to ijtihad and disagreement
C) Because the buyer agreed
D) Because it is a gift

Key Takeaway
A sale can be partially valid and partially invalid if the invalid part is weak due to scholarly disagreement.

Selling Oil in a Container Without Known Weight
Ruling on sale with unknown net weight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling oil with its container and identify the two conditions that validate the sale.

Core Idea
Uncertainty in quantity invalidates a sale unless the container's weight is known or its deduction is stipulated.

When you buy olive oil in a jar, you need to know how much oil you're getting. Islamic law prohibits gharar (uncertainty) in sales. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains when selling oil with its container is valid and when it becomes invalid due to unknown quantity. The key distinction is whether the condition (like deducting container weight) is part of the contract's inherent requirements (muqtada al-aqd) or an external imposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The passage begins by stating it is not permissible to sell oil on the condition that it is weighed with its container. Understand that the default ruling is invalidity (fasid) due to uncertainty in the quantity of oil.
The genitive case 'wa zaytin' indicates this is conjoined to a previously negated item.
2
Recognize the first valid condition
The text then says 'unless the weight of the container is known' – if the container's weight is known, the sale becomes permissible because the oil quantity can be calculated.
Knowledge removes gharar.
3
Recognize the second valid condition
The passage adds that if the parties stipulate that the container's weight be deducted from the total, the sale is valid even without prior knowledge of the container weight.
This condition is considered muqtada al-aqd (required by the contract) because the purpose is to buy oil, not the container.
4
Distinguish between types of conditions
The author explains that a condition required by the contract (muqtada al-aqd) is one that is obligatory without mention, like delivery. An external condition is one that only becomes obligatory due to the stipulation. The deduction condition falls into the first category.
This distinction is crucial for determining validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزيت إلخ: بالجر عطف على المنفي، وكان ينبغي أن لا يفصل بينهما بالمثبت؛ لما فيه من الإبهام، أي لم يجز بيع زيت على أن يزنه بظرفه إلا إذا عرف مقدار وزن الظرف فيجوز، كما لو شرط أن يطرح عنه بوزن الظرف؛ لأنه مقتضيات العقد، والطرح بدون معرفة مقدار الوزن شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، والمراد بالشرط الذي يقتضيه العقد هو الذي يجب بالعقد من غير شرط كشرط تسليم المبيع أو الثمن، والذي لا يقتضيه هو الذي لا يجب في العقد إلا بالشرط. وصح لو شرط أن يطرح عنه بوزن الظرف لأن هذا الشرط يقتضيه العقد؛ لأن مقتضاه أن يطرح عن الزيت وزن الظرف.
And oil etc.: in the genitive, conjoined to the negated item. It would have been better not to separate them with the positive statement because of the ambiguity. Meaning, it is not permissible to sell oil on the condition that it be weighed with its container unless the weight of the container is known, in which case it is permissible. Similarly, if he stipulates that the weight of the container be deducted from the total, because that is required by the contract. Without knowing the weight, the deduction is a condition not required by the contract. What is meant by a condition required by the contract is that which is obligatory in the contract without stipulation, like delivery of the sold item or the price. A condition not required is that which is only obligatory by stipulation. And it is valid if he stipulates that the container's weight be deducted, because this condition is required by the contract; since the contract's requirement is that the weight of the container be deducted from the oil.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage addresses the sale of oil in a container when the net weight is unknown. The default ruling is invalidity due to gharar (uncertainty) because the exact quantity of oil is not known. However, if the weight of the container is known, it becomes permissible. Alternatively, if the parties stipulate that the container's weight will be deducted (i.e., the buyer pays for the net oil), it is permissible because the condition is consistent with the contract's purpose. The distinction is drawn between conditions that are implied in the contract (like delivery) and those that are external (like deduction of weight without knowledge).

Key Terms
ظرف (ẓarf): A container, e.g., a skin or jar.
مقتضى العقد (muqtaḍā al-‘aqd): That which is necessarily required by the contract without additional stipulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it valid to sell oil in its container without specifying the net weight?
A) When the container is made of leather.
B) When the buyer trusts the seller.
C) When both parties agree to deduct the weight of the container.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
A sale of oil with its container is valid only if the container's weight is known or its deduction is stipulated as required by the contract.

Dispute Over Container Weight: Buyer's Word Prevails
Ruling on dispute over container weight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dispute between buyer and seller over the weight of a container and apply the principle of qawl al-munkir.

Core Idea
The buyer's word with oath is taken because he denies the seller's claim of a higher weight.

In sales transactions, disputes can arise over incidental elements like the weight of a container. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses such a dispute and applies a key procedural principle: the word of the denier (al-munkir) is accepted with oath. The ruling ensures that the party denying an extra claim is not burdened with proof, unless the other party provides evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize that the buyer bought an item in a container (ziqq) and later disputes the weight of the container returned to the seller. Two possible characterizations: dispute over the container's identity or over the quantity (affecting price).
The container weight is incidental to the main sold item.
2
Apply the principle of qawl al-munkir
The buyer denies the seller's claim that the container was lighter (or heavier). Since the buyer is the denier, his statement with oath is accepted according to the general rule: 'al-qawl li al-munkir ma'a yamīnih'.
The seller is the claimant (mudda'ī) and must produce evidence if he persists.
3
Analyze the two justifications
The passage gives two lines of reasoning: (1) if the dispute is about the identity of the container, the receiver's (qābiḍ) word is taken; (2) if it is about the quantity, it reverts to a price dispute, and the buyer as denier wins. Both lead to the same conclusion: buyer's word with oath.
Either way, the result is consistent.
4
Explain the absence of mutual oaths (taḥāluf)
Mutual oaths occur when the dispute is about the principal object of sale (al-thaman). Here the dispute is incidental (about the container), which is not the principal subject. Therefore, taḥāluf is not required.
Mutual oaths are only for cases where the core contract terms are disputed.
5
Identify the burden of proof
If the seller disagrees with the buyer's oath, he must provide evidence (bayyina) to support his claim. Without evidence, the buyer's oath settles the matter.
This upholds the principle that the claimant must prove, and the denier must swear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اختلفا في الزق: أي لو اختلف المتبايعان في مقدار وزن الزق بأن اشترى ثمناً مثلاً في زق، فرد المشتري الزق، وهو عشرة أرطال مثلاً، فقال البائع: هو كان خمسة أرطال أو اختلفا في تعيين الزق، فالقول للمشتري مع يمينه؛ لأنه إن اعتبر الاختلاف في تعيين الزق المقبوض ، فالقول قول القابض سواء كان ضميناً كالغاصب، أو أمينا كالمودع، وإن اعتبر الاختلاف في قدر المبيع الراجع إلى الاختلاف في الثمن، فالقول أيضاً قول المشتري؛ لأنه ينكر الزيادة والبائع يدعيها، والقول قول المنكر مع اليمين ولا يتحالفان بناء على أن الاختلاف في الثمن. التحالف؛ لأنه . يوجب إذا كان قصداً وههنا هو ضمني، لوقوعه في ضمن الاختلاف في الزق، وعلى البائع البينة.
And if they differ about the container: i.e., if the two parties differ about the weight of the container, for example, if one buys a price (item) in a container, then the buyer returns the container, which is ten ratls, and the seller says it was five ratls, or they differ about the identity of the container, then the word is the buyer's with his oath. Because if one considers the difference to be about the identity of the delivered container, the word is that of the receiver (qābiḍ) whether he is a guarantor like a usurper or a trustee like a depositee. And if one considers the difference to be about the quantity of the sold item, which goes back to a difference about the price, then also the word is the buyer's because he denies the increase and the seller claims it, and the word is that of the denier with oath, and they do not take mutual oaths (taḥāluf) because the difference is about the price. [Mutual oaths] are only required when [the difference] is in the principal object, whereas here it is incidental, occurring within the difference about the container. And the seller must provide evidence (bayyina).
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage deals with a dispute between buyer and seller about the weight of a container used for a liquid item. The ruling is that the buyer's statement is accepted with oath. There are two ways to view the dispute: if it is about the identity of the container, the receiver's word is taken; if it is about the quantity (which affects the price), the buyer as denier of an extra claim has his word. In either case, the buyer prevails with oath. There is no mutual oath (taḥāluf) because the primary dispute is about the container, not the main object of sale. The seller must bring evidence if he disputes.

Key Terms
زق (ziqq): A container, usually made of hide for liquids.
قول المنكر (qawl al-munkir): The statement of the person who denies a claim is accepted with oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over the weight of a container after sale, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The seller's word is taken with oath.
B) The buyer's word is taken with oath.
C) Both parties must take oaths and then split the difference.
D) The container is weighed by a third party.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over incidental matters like container weight, the Hanafi school accepts the denier's (buyer's) word with oath, placing the burden of proof on the claimant (seller).

Sale with Inclusion of Another Item: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Explaining ruling on including another item in a sale from original buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on including an item in a sale back to the original seller and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, applying the principle that the invalidity does not spread.

Core Idea
Including a new item in a buyback does not invalidate the new item's sale, only the original item's.

When a buyer sells an item back to the original seller along with another item before paying the first price, does the entire contract become invalid? The Hanafi school makes a careful distinction: the new item is sold validly, but the original item's sale is invalid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling and the inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
Recognize the scenario: A buys a slave-girl for 1000 dirhams, then sells her back to the original seller along with a horse for 1000 dirhams before paying the first price.
2
Separate the two sales
Understand that the contract involves two items: the slave-girl (originally bought) and the horse (new item). The Hanafi analysis treats them separately.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on buyback at a discount
The original item (slave-girl) cannot be sold back at a lower price before payment of the original price. Since some of the total price must be allocated to the horse, the effective price for the slave-girl is less than 1000 dirhams, making that part of the sale fasid.
This is based on qiyas: the prohibition of buying back at a discount applies only to the original item.
4
Note the non-spread of invalidity
The invalidity does not spread to the horse's sale because the defect (lower price) is not present in the new item. The contract is partially fasid.
This is a point of ijtihad; the Shafi'i school holds the entire contract valid.
5
Appreciate the practical consequence
If a judge rules the entire sale valid (following Shafi'i), it stands because of the ijtihad nature. But the relied-upon Hanafi position is that the new item's sale is valid and the original item's sale is fasid, requiring its return.
The tarjih: Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح فيما ضم إليه: أي إلى المشترى - بفتح الراء - بأن اشترى مثلاً جارية بألف وقبضها، ثم باعها المشتري وضم الأخرى معها بألف من البائع قبل نقد الثمن الأول، جاز البيع في التي لم يشترها من البائع، ويفسد في الأخرى؛ لأنه لابد أن يجعل بعض الثمن بمقابلة التي لم يشترها منه، فيكون مشترياً للأخرى بأقل مما باع، وهو فاسد، ولا يشيع الفساد؛ لأنه ضعيف فيها، لكونه مجتهداً فيه؛ لأن الشافعي يقول بجوازه، حتى لو قضى القاضي بجوازه صح. رمز الحقائق: ۳۸/۲] (فتح)
And it is valid regarding what is included with it: meaning to the buyer (with fath on the ra) - for example, if he bought a slave-girl for a thousand and took possession, then the buyer sold her and included another with her for a thousand from the seller before paying the first price, the sale is valid regarding the one he did not buy from the seller, and is invalid regarding the other; because it is necessary that some of the price is in exchange for the one he did not buy from him, so he becomes a buyer of the other at a lower price than what he sold, and that is invalid, and the invalidity does not spread because it is weak in it, being a matter of ijtihad; because al-Shafi'i says it is permissible, so if a judge ruled by its permissibility it is valid. Reference: Ramz al-Haqaiq: 38/2 (Fath).
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq elaborates on the ruling that including another item in a sale from the original buyer is valid for the new item. The example: a buyer buys a slave-girl for 1000 dirhams and takes possession. Before paying the price, the buyer sells the slave-girl back to the original seller along with another item for 1000 dirhams. The sale of the new item is valid because it is not a buyback at a lower price, but the sale of the original slave-girl is invalid because some of the price must be allocated to the new item, effectively making the price for the slave-girl less than what the buyer originally paid. The invalidity does not spread to the entire sale because this is a matter of ijtihad (al-Shafi'i allows it). Thus, if a judge rules it valid, it stands. This is a typical Hanafi ruling on fasid sales.

Key Terms
ضم (damm): Including an additional item along with the original sold item in a single sale
فساد (fasad): Invalidity that does not render the contract void ab initio but may be subject to correction or partial validity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi commentary, if a buyer purchases a slave-girl for 1000 dirhams, takes possession, then sells her back to the original seller along with a horse for 1000 dirhams before paying the first price, what is the ruling?
A) The entire sale is invalid because it is a buyback at a lower price
B) The sale of the horse is valid but the sale of the slave-girl is invalid
C) The entire sale is valid because the price is the same
D) The sale of the slave-girl is valid but the horse sale is invalid

Key Takeaway
When a buyer includes another item in a sale back to the original seller before paying the first price, the new item's sale is valid, but the original item's sale is fasid; the invalidity does not spread.

Sale of Oil with Condition of Container Weight
Ruling on selling oil with condition of weighing with container

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling oil with an unknown container weight, distinguish between conditions required by the contract and extra conditions, and apply the rule to a dispute about the container's weight.

Core Idea
A sale condition must be inherent to the contract; extra conditions like unknown container weight invalidate the sale.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries addresses a classic sale scenario: selling oil by weight including the container. The Hanafi school distinguishes between conditions that the contract inherently requires and those that are extraneous. Understanding this distinction is key to evaluating sales with conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sale and its condition
The sale is of oil on condition that the seller weighs it with its container and later deducts the container's weight. The container's weight is unknown at the time of sale.
2
Determine the ruling when weight is unknown
Such a sale is ruled fasid (invalid) because the condition is not required by the contract and introduces uncertainty (gharar).
Fasid means the contract is defective and requires rectification.
3
Understand the exception
If the container's weight is known and the condition is to deduct it, the sale is valid because that condition is implied by the contract (mqtada al-aqd).
4
Distinguish between conditions required by contract and extra conditions
The commentary explains that a condition that the contract inherently requires (like delivery of the sold item) does not invalidate. An extra condition not required by the contract invalidates unless it is known or the condition itself is valid.
5
Apply to a dispute about the container's weight
If buyer and seller differ on the weight of the container, the buyer's word is taken with oath. The seller must provide evidence (baiyyina).

And oil on condition that he weighs it with its container, and he deducts, meaning with its container from it, at the place of each container fifty ratls, and it is valid if he stipulated that he deducts from it by the weight of the container, meaning from the oil. Then the condition for impermissibility is that the purchase is from his buyer or from his heir, not from the donee or legatee, and that the two prices are of the same type. And the intended meaning of the condition that the contract requires is that which is obligatory by the contract without condition, like the condition of delivery of the sold item or the price. And that which it does not require is that which is not obligatory in the contract except by condition. (Ayni, Fath) And it is valid if he stipulated etc. because this condition is required by the contract; because its requirement is that the weight of the container be deducted from the oil. (Ayni) And if they differ regarding the container: i.e., if the two transactors differ regarding the amount of the weight of the container, e.g., he bought oil in a container, and the buyer returns the container, and it is e.g., ten ratls, and the seller says it was five ratls, or they differ in identifying the container, then the statement is the buyer's with his oath; because if the difference is in identifying the received container, the statement is that of the receiver whether he is a guarantor like the usurper or a trustee like the depositee. And if the difference is in the amount of the sold item referring to the difference in the price, then the statement is also the buyer's; because he denies the increase and the seller claims it, and the statement is that of the denier with his oath, and they do not take mutual oaths based on the difference being in the price, because mutual oaths are required when it is the main issue, and here it is subsidiary, occurring within the difference about the container. And the seller must provide evidence. (Ayni, Fath, Inaya)
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage covers two related rulings from the chapter on invalid sales. First, selling oil with the condition of weighing it in its container without knowing the container's weight is invalid. However, if the seller stipulates that he will deduct the known weight of the container, it is valid because that condition is in line with the contract's requirements. The commentary distinguishes between conditions that the contract inherently requires (like delivery) and those that are additional. The second ruling addresses a dispute between buyer and seller about the weight of the container (zirq). If they differ, the buyer's word is taken with his oath, whether the difference is about the identity of the container or the weight of the oil (which affects the price). This is because the buyer is the denier of the seller's claim. The seller bears the burden of proof.

Key Terms
زق (ziqq): container for oil or wine
شرط يقتضيه العقد (shart yaqtadihi al-'aqd): Condition that is inherently required by the contract, like delivery

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The seller, because he claims more weight
B) The buyer, because he has the item
C) Both must provide evidence
D) The judge decides

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school invalidates sale conditions that are not inherent to the contract, such as an unknown container weight, to prevent uncertainty.

Purchase from Relatives and Heirs: Invalidity and Conditions
Ruling on buying back from relatives and heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purchasing from a person whose testimony is not accepted, and apply the conditions that determine whether the purchase is invalid.

Core Idea
Buying from a relative whose testimony is rejected is like buying from yourself – invalid unless the price type differs.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq expands on a rule about invalid sales. If you buy an item from someone closely related to you (like your son or father), the Hanafi school treats it as if you bought from yourself. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow it for non-slave/free relatives. The passage also covers buying from the heir of your buyer, and distinguishes between value loss due to defect vs. market change.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited persons
List those whose testimony is not accepted in favor of the buyer: son, father, slave, mukatab. These are considered legally connected to the buyer.
The bond of relationship or ownership creates a legal unity in testimony.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's blanket rule vs. Sahibayn's exception
Abu Hanifa treats all such purchases as invalid (like buying from oneself). The Sahibayn permit it for father and son, but maintain invalidity for slave and mukatab.
The dispute hinges on whether the relationship alone suffices to extend the ruling.
3
Apply the condition of same price type
Invalidity only occurs if the repurchase price is of the same type (e.g., both in dirhams). If the buyer uses a different currency or commodity, the sale is valid even if the amount is less.
Dinars and dirhams are considered one genus for this rule.
4
Distinguish defect vs. market loss
If the item's intrinsic value decreased due to a physical defect, selling it back at a lower price is permissible. If the value dropped due to market fluctuation, the same restriction applies (repurchase at lower price is invalid).
The rule targets intentional loss-shifting, not accidental depreciation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشراء من لا تجوز شهادته له كالولد والوالد والعبد والمكاتب بمنزلة شراء البائع بنفسه، وقال أبو ومحمد: يجوز في غير العبد والمكاتب وشراء الوكيل وبيعه كبيعه بنفسه، وكذا لو اشترى من وارث مشتريه بأقل مما اشترى به المورث لم يجز؛ لقيام الوارث مقام المورث، ثم شرط عدم الجواز أن يكون الشراء من مشتريه أو من وارثه، لا من الموهوب له أو الموصى له، وأن يكون الثمنان متحدين جنساً، فإذا اشتراه بجنس آخر غير الثمن الأول يجوز، وإن كان الثمن الثاني أقل، والدنانير جنس الدراهم، وأما إذا تعيب المبيع ذاتاً في يد المشتري، فباعه البائع بأقل من الثمن جاز، ولو انتقصت قيمته بتغير السعر لا بنقصان الذات لم يجز شراؤه بالأقل. (عيني، فتح، نقاية)
And the purchase from one whose testimony is not acceptable for him, like the son, father, slave, and mukatab, is like the seller buying from himself. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: it is permissible in other than the slave and mukatab. And the agent's purchase and sale is like his own sale. Similarly, if he bought from the heir of his buyer for less than what the deceased bought it for, it is not permissible; because the heir stands in the place of the deceased. Then the condition for impermissibility is that the purchase is from his buyer or his heir, not from the donee or legatee, and that the two prices are of the same type. So if he buys it with a different type than the first price, it is permissible even if the second price is less, and dinars are the same type as dirhams. And if the sold item is defective in essence in the hand of the buyer, and the seller sells it for less than the price, it is permissible. And if its value decreased due to change in price not due to defect in essence, his buying it for less is not permissible. (Ayni, Fath, Niqaya)
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage expands on a ruling regarding invalid sales: buying an item from a person whose testimony is not acceptable for you (such as your son, father, slave, or mukatab) is treated like you buying from yourself, which is invalid. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ: they permit it except for slave and mukatab. The ruling also applies to buying from the heir of your original buyer at a lower price (the heir stands in the place of the deceased). Conditions: the buyback must be from the buyer or his heir (not a donee or legatee), and the two prices must be of the same type; if different type, it is permissible. Also, if the item's value decreased due to a defect in its essence (like damage), selling it back at a lower price is permissible; but if the value dropped due to market change, it is not permissible.

Key Terms
لا تجوز شهادته له (la tajuz shahadatuhu lahu): Person whose testimony is not acceptable in favor of the buyer due to close relationship

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Buying back from a close relative or heir at a lower price is invalid if the prices are of the same type, unless the value loss is due to a physical defect.

Selling a Slave-Girl with a Condition of Manumission: The Hanafi Position
Ruling on conditional sale with manumission and similar conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school invalidates a sale conditioned on the buyer's manumission of the slave and distinguish the Shafi'i counter-argument, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A condition that benefits the subject of sale without being required by the contract invalidates the sale.

This passage addresses a specific conditional sale: selling a slave-girl on the condition that the buyer frees her, makes her mudabbara, enters a kitaba, or makes her umm walad. The Hanafi school rules such sales invalid, while the Shafi'i school permits them. Understanding the reasoning behind each school is crucial for grasping the Hanafi theory of conditions in contracts, which distinguishes between inherent conditions and extra stipulations that confer undue benefit.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the sale is invalid (fasid) because the condition is not required by the contract. Note the specific conditions mentioned: manumission, tadbir, kitaba, istiwlad.
The passage begins with 'لم يجز بيع أمة على أن يعتق المشتري' – the sale is not permissible.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The opposing school is Imam al-Shafi'i, and also Malik and Ahmad. They hold the sale is valid because such conditions are customary in 'bay' al-nasamah' (sale of a soul) and similar to wills.
The text mentions a weak Hanafi narration (Hasan from Abu Hanifa) that also permits it.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent's dalil is custom ('urf) and analogy (qiyas) with wills, where manumission is valid. They argue the condition benefits the slave, which is commendable.
The text says: 'تفسير بيع النسمة: أن العبد ممن يعرف أنه يعتقه'.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi responds that the sale contract requires any disposition the buyer chooses, not a specific one. Stipulating a specific act like manumission introduces an extra benefit for the slave that the contract does not require, thus invalid. The hadith 'نهى عن بيع وشرط' is cited.
The Hanafi proof: the condition is not 'muqtada al-aqd' (required by the contract).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, the sale is void; the slave-girl must be returned, and if manumitted, the buyer owes the price by istihsan. Under Shafi'i, the sale is valid and the condition binding.
The practical difference affects property rights and obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمة على أن يعتق المشتري أو يدبّر أو يكاتب أو يستولد . أي أو على أن إلخ أي و لم يجز بيع أمة على أن يعتق المشتري أو يدبّر أو يكاتب أو يستولد . شروع في الفساد الواقع في العقد بسبب الشرط؛ لنهيه عليه عن بيع وشرط، لكن ليس كل شرط يفسد البيع، بل لابد أن لا يقتضيه العقد، ولا يلائمه ولا يتعارف وكان فيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين، أو للمعقود عليه وهو من أهل الاستحقاق، ولم يرد الشرع بجوازه، وإذا عرف هذا فاشتراط العتق وتوابعه مما لا يقتضيه العقد، وفيه منفعة للمعقود عليه، ولهذا قال: لم يجز بيع أمة على أن يعتقها المشتري؛ لنهيه عليه عن بيع و شرط، رواه أبو حنيفة عن عمرو بن شعيب عن أبيه جده مرفوعاً. عن يبيع وقال الشافعي: يجوز البيع بشرط الإعتاق؛ لأن النسمة متعارف في الوصايا، وتفسير بيع بيع النسمة: أن العبد ممن يعرف أنه يعتقه وهو رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة، وبه قال مالك وأحمد؛ ولنا أن هذا الشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، بل يقتضي أي تصرف شاء، لا تصرفاً معيناً، فاشتراط مثله فيه مفسد له كاشتراط التدبير والاستيلاد والكتابة، ولو أعتقه المشتري جاز الإعتاق استحساناً عند أبي حنيفة، ويجب عليه الثمن، وقالا: لا يجب، وهو القياس. أو يدبر إلخ أي لم يجز أيضاً البيع بهذه الشروط؛ لما روينا، وعند الثلاثة: صح في الكل إلا في قول الشافعي وأحمد في رواية رمز الحقائق: ٣٩/٢] (فتح)
And a slave-girl on condition that the buyer manumits or makes tadbir or kitaba or istiwlad. i.e., or on condition that etc. i.e., it is not permissible to sell a slave-girl on condition that the buyer manumits or makes tadbir or kitaba or istiwlad. Beginning of the invalidity occurring in the contract due to the condition, because of his (the Prophet) prohibition of sale with a condition, but not every condition invalidates the sale; rather it must be a condition that the contract does not require, nor is it compatible with it, nor customary, and there is benefit in it for one of the contracting parties or for the subject of the contract who is capable of entitlement, and the Shari'ah has not permitted it. When this is understood, the stipulation of manumission and its like is something the contract does not require, and there is benefit for the subject of the contract, therefore he said: it is not permissible to sell a slave-girl on condition that the buyer manumits her, because of his (the Prophet's) prohibition of sale with a condition, narrated by Abu Hanifa from Amr bin Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather marfu'an. And al-Shafi'i said: the sale with condition of manumission is permissible, because manumission of a slave (al-nasamah) is customary in wills, and the interpretation of the sale of al-nasamah is that the slave is known to be one whom the buyer will manumit. This is a narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, and Malik and Ahmad said the same. And our proof: this condition is not required by the contract; rather the contract requires any disposition the buyer wishes, not a specific disposition. So stipulating a specific disposition invalidates it, like stipulating tadbir, istiwlad, or kitaba. And if the buyer manumits (without condition), the manumission is valid by istihsan according to Abu Hanifa, and he owes the price. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not owed, and that is qiyas. Or makes tadbir etc. i.e., the sale is also not permissible with these conditions based on what we narrated. And according to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), it is valid in all except according to Shafi'i and Ahmad in one narration. Reference: Ramz al-Haqaiq: 39/2 (Fath).
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage discusses the invalidity of selling a slave-girl with the condition that the buyer manumits her, makes tadbir (promise of freedom after death), kitaba (contract of manumission), or istiwlad (bearing a child for the owner). The Hanafi position is that such a sale is invalid because the condition is not required by the contract of sale. The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited sale with a condition that is not inherent to the contract. A hadith from Amr bin Shu'ayb is cited. Al-Shafi'i and others permit it, reasoning that such conditions are customary in the context of 'selling a soul' (bay' al-nasamah) where the buyer intends freedom for the slave. The Hanafi response distinguishes between a condition that the contract requires (like delivery) and an extra condition that imposes a specific beneficial act on the subject of the contract (the slave). The latter invalidates the sale. It is noted that if the buyer actually manumits the slave without the condition being part of the contract, it is valid by istihsan according to Abu Hanifa, and the buyer owes the price; the Sahibayn say the price is not owed based on qiyas (analogy). The passage also mentions that according to the three Hanafi imams, the sale with these conditions is invalid, contrary to Shafi'i and Ahmad in one narration.

Key Terms
بيع وشرط (bay' wa shart): Sale with a condition that is not part of the contract's requirements.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; in this context, the manumission is valid by istihsan even if the sale is invalid.
تدبير (tadbir): The promise of freedom after the owner's death; one of the conditions that invalidates the sale.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract of manumission where the slave pays for freedom in installments.
استيلاد (istiwlad): Making the slave-girl an umm walad (mother of the owner's child), which automatically frees her upon the owner's death.
بيع النسمة (bay' al-nasamah): A customary sale where the buyer is known to manumit the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone sells a slave-girl with the condition that the buyer makes her into a mudabbara (freed upon the owner's death), what is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid and the condition is binding
B) The sale is invalid because the condition is not required by the contract
C) The sale is valid but the condition is void
D) The sale is invalid only if the condition is written

Key Takeaway
A sale conditioned on an act not required by the contract and that benefits the subject or a party is invalid according to the Hanafi school.
