Muslim Appointing a Dhimmi to Trade Wine: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on Muslim-dhimmi agency for wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a Muslim appointing a dhimmi to buy or sell wine and evaluate the Sahibayn's counter-argument using the concepts of dual capacity and thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Agency is valid in capacity but prohibited in command: the Muslim must give the price in charity.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute that tests the boundaries of agency (wakalah) when the subject matter is prohibited (wine). The key question: can a Muslim delegate an act he himself cannot perform? Abu Hanifa says yes, but with a charity requirement; his two leading students disagree. We will dissect their proofs and understand the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position (Imam Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling: the agency is valid (sahih) but the command is makruh tahrimi. The Muslim must give the sale price in charity.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
2
Identify the Opposing View (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the agency is void (batil). They argue that what the principal cannot do himself, he cannot delegate.
Their ruling: no ownership, no charity.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's Proof
Two proofs: (1) Analogy to a Muslim appointing a Magian to marry a Magian, which is invalid by consensus. (2) The agent's act transfers to the principal, so it is as if the principal did it himself.
They consider the act of the agent attributable to the principal.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the agent's capacity (ahl al-tasarruf) and the principal's capacity for the ruling. He cites exceptions: inheritance, ma'dhun slave, judge ordering dhimmi to sell wine, and Muslim executor appointing a dhimmi. He also rebuts the marriage analogy by noting that in marriage the agent is a mere messenger (safir).
The two capacities are independent: the agent can act, and the principal can own the legal effect.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
Under Abu Hanifa, the Muslim must give the price in charity; under Sahibayn, the agency is void and the wine is not owned. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.
The difference affects whether the principal has any obligation regarding the price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أمر ذمّياً بشراء خمر أو بيعها صح، هذا الأمر مع كراهة التحريم، ويجب عليه أن يتصدق بالثمن عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا لو أمر محرم حلالاً ببيع صيد يجوز عنده مع الكراهة، وقالا: لا يصح، أي باطل؛ لأن الموكل لا يليه بنفسه، فلا يوليه غيره كتوكيل المسلم محوسياً بتزويج مجوسية؛ فإنه لا يجوز إجماعاً، ولأن ما يثبت للوكيل ينتقل إلى الموكل فصار كأنه باشره بنفسه، وله: أن المعتبر هذا الباب أهليتان أهلية الوكيل وهي أهلية التصرف في المأمور به وللنصراني ذلك، وأهلية الموكل، وهي أهلية ثبوت الحكم له، وللموكل ذلك حكماً للعقد؛ لئلا يلزم انفكاك الملزوم عن اللازم، فلا يمتنع بسبب الإسلام، ألا ترى إلى صحة ثبوت ملك الخمر للمسلم إرثاً إذا أسلم مورثه الذمي ومات عن خمر أو خنزير، وكذا العبد المأذون له الذي إذا اشترى خمراً ثبت الملك فيها لمولاه المسلم اتفاقاً. ثم في التوكيل بشراء الخمر يملكها الموكل حكماً؛ فيخللها، وإن كان خنزيراً يسيبه، وقولهما: "الموكل لا يليه فلا يوليه غيره" منقوض بأن الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شراءه لنفسه، وبأن للقاضي أن يأمر ذمياً ببيع خمر تركها ذمي، وإن لم يملك بيعها، وبأن وصي الذمي إذا كان مسلماً يملك أن يوكل ذمياً ببيع حمره وإن لم يملكه الوصي، والقياس على تزويج المجوسي مدفوع بأن حقوق العقد في النكاح ترجع إلى الموكل، والوكيل سفير ومعبر عنه، فيكون مضافاً إلى الموكل.
If a Muslim ordered a dhimmi to buy wine or sell it, it is valid. This command is with karahah tahrimiyyah, and he must give the price in charity according to Abu Hanifa. Likewise, if a muhrim ordered a halal person to sell game, it is valid according to him with karahah. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is not valid, i.e., void; because the principal (muwakkil) cannot do it himself, so he cannot delegate it to another, like a Muslim appointing a Magian to marry a Magian woman; that is invalid by consensus. And because what is established for the agent transfers to the principal, so it is as if he did it himself. For him (Abu Hanifa): the consideration in this chapter is two capacities: the capacity of the agent, which is the capacity to act in the commanded matter, and the Christian has that; and the capacity of the principal, which is the capacity for the ruling to be established for him, and the principal has that by legal effect of the contract, lest the necessary be separated from the necessary. So it is not prevented because of Islam. Do you not see the validity of ownership of wine for a Muslim by inheritance if his dhimmi ancestor became Muslim and died owning wine or pork? Likewise, a ma'dhun slave who buys wine, ownership is established for his Muslim master by agreement. Then in the agency to buy wine, the principal owns it by legal effect; so he may turn it into vinegar, and if it is a pig, he sets it free. Their statement: 'The principal cannot do it himself, so he cannot delegate it to another' is refuted by the fact that an agent to buy a specific thing cannot buy it for himself, and by the fact that a judge may order a dhimmi to sell wine left by a dhimmi even though he cannot sell it, and by the fact that the executor of a dhimmi if he is Muslim may appoint a dhimmi to sell his donkeys even though the executor cannot sell them. The analogy to the marriage of a Magian is rebutted because the rights of the contract in marriage revert to the principal, and the agent is a messenger and interpreter, so it is attributed to the principal.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a Muslim appointing a dhimmi to buy or sell wine. The ruling is presented with the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. According to Abu Hanifa, the agency is valid but makruh tahrimi, and the Muslim must give the sale price in charity. The Sahibayn say it is invalid. The commentary provides the reasoning for both sides: the Sahibayn argue that since the Muslim cannot perform the act himself, he cannot delegate it, analogous to a Muslim appointing a Magian to marry a Magian woman. Abu Hanifa responds by distinguishing between the capacity of the agent (which the dhimmi has) and the capacity of the principal (which the Muslim has in terms of the legal effect). He supports his view with evidence from inheritance and from the case of a ma'dhun slave. The text then refutes the Sahibayn's analogy by pointing to cases where a delegation is valid even if the principal cannot do it himself, such as the judge ordering a dhimmi to sell wine, or the Muslim executor of a dhimmi's will appointing a dhimmi to sell donkeys. Finally, the analogy to marriage is rejected because in marriage the agent is merely a messenger, whereas in sale the agent acts on behalf of the principal. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view as per the text, though it notes the karahah tahrimiyyah.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Muslim rule with a covenant of protection.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed to act on behalf of another (principal).
موكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent.
كراهة التحريم (karahah tahrimiyyah): A level of prohibition approaching haram, but not as severe; the act is sinful but not invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what must a Muslim do with the price obtained from a dhimmi agent's sale of wine?
A) Keep it as lawful income
B) Give it in charity
C) Return it to the buyer
D) Destroy it

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's appointment of a dhimmi to trade in wine is valid due to the agent's capacity, but the principal must give the price in charity because the command itself is prohibited.

The Three Categories of Excepting the Unborn Child in Contracts
Ruling on exception of child from contracts and three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effects of excepting the unborn child in sale, gift, and bequest contracts, and state the Hanafi ruling for each category.

Core Idea
What cannot be sold alone cannot be excluded from a sale — the exception of a child is null and its effect varies by contract type.

The Hanafi school provides a clear categorization for the effect of excepting an unborn child from a contract. This rule, derived from the principle that an object which cannot be independently contracted cannot be excluded, yields three distinct results depending on the nature of the contract. Understanding these categories prevents invalid transactions and clarifies the legal status of the child.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the foundational principle: what cannot be the sole object of a contract (like an unborn child) cannot be validly excluded from it.
This rule applies to commutative contracts like sale.
2
List the three categories
Enumerate the three categories of contracts based on the effect of the exception: (1) contracts where both contract and exception are invalid, (2) contracts where the contract is valid but the exception is void, (3) contracts where both are valid.
Examples for each category are provided in the text.
3
Explain the reasoning for each category
For the first category (sale, lease, kitaba, rahn), the exception is a corrupt condition benefiting the seller, so the entire contract is void. For the second (gift, charity, marriage, khul', diyat settlement), the contracts are gratuitous or non-commutative, so the exception fails but the contract stands. For the third (bequest), the exception is valid because bequest is analogous to inheritance.
The analogy with inheritance is key for the bequest category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو إلا حملها : أي لم يجز بيع الأمة إلا حملها، وفي العبارة تسامح؛ لأن مقتضى العطف أن يكون التقدير: أو بيع أمة على أن يستثني حملها، وليس مراداً، ووجه عدم جواز بيع الحمل أن ما لا يصح إفراده بالعقد لا يصح استثناؤه، والحمل لا يجوز إفراده بالبيع، فكذا استثناؤه، وإذا لم يصح بقي شرطا فاسداً وفيه نفع للبائع، والبيع يبطل. ثم استثناء الحمل في العقود على ثلاث مراتب الأول يفسد فيه العقد والاستثناء كالبيع والإجارة والكتابة والرهن والثاني العقد فيه جائز ، والاستثناء باطل كالهبة والصدقة والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، فلا يبطل العقد ويبطل الاستثناء، ويكون الحمل تابعاً للأم في هذه العقود. والثالث: يجوز فيه العقد والاستثناء وهو الوصية، حتى لو أوصى بجاريته لإنسان إلا حملها صح، وكذا لو أوصى بحملها الآخر صح؛ لأن الوصية أخت الميراث، والميراث يجري فيه فكذا الوصية
Or except her child: i.e., it is not permissible to sell the slave-girl except her child. There is looseness in the expression because the requirement of the conjunction would be that the estimation is: or the sale of a slave-girl on the condition that he excludes her child, but that is not intended. The reason for the impermissibility of selling the child is that what cannot be made the sole object of a contract cannot be excluded from it. The unborn child cannot be sold independently, so its exclusion is likewise. When the exclusion is invalid, it remains a corrupt condition with benefit to the seller, and the sale is voided. Then the exception of a child in contracts is of three ranks: First, it invalidates both the contract and the exception, such as sale, lease, kitaba, and pledge. Second, the contract is valid but the exception is void, such as gift, charity, marriage, khul', and settlement for deliberate bloodshed: the contract is not invalidated, the exception is void, and the child follows the mother in these contracts. Third, both the contract and the exception are permissible, and that is the bequest: if one bequeaths his slave-girl to someone except her child, it is valid; likewise, if he bequeaths her child to another, it is valid, because the bequest is the sister of inheritance, and inheritance applies to it, so does the bequest.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on excluding the unborn child from the sale of a slave-girl. The sale is invalid because the unborn child cannot be sold separately, so its exclusion is also invalid, leaving a corrupt condition. The text then provides a comprehensive categorization of contracts regarding the exception of a child. In the first category (sale, lease, kitaba, rahn), the exception invalidates both the contract and the exception. In the second (gift, charity, marriage, khul', diyat settlement), the contract remains valid but the exception is void, and the child follows the mother. In the third (bequest), both are valid because bequest is analogous to inheritance. This categorization is a key legal tool for understanding the effect of such conditions across different areas of fiqh.

Key Terms
استثناء الحمل (istithna' al-haml): Exception of the unborn child from a contract, excluding it from the object of the contract.
مراتب (maratib): Categories or ranks of contracts regarding the effect of excepting the child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which type of contract does the exception of a child invalidate both the contract and the exception?
A) Gift (hibah)
B) Marriage (nikah)
C) Sale (bay')
D) Bequest (wasiyyah)

Key Takeaway
The exception of the unborn child invalidates commutative contracts but is void in gratuitous contracts and valid in bequests.

Invalid Conditions in Sale and Valid Sandal Condition by Istihsan
Rulings on various conditions in sale: invalid and valid exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conditions that invalidate a sale from those validated by istihsan and explain the role of custom in overriding the general prohibition.

Core Idea
Conditions not required by the contract that benefit one party invalidate the sale, but custom (urf) can make exceptions via istihsan.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents several conditions that, when attached to a sale, render the contract invalid (fasid), such as the seller's use of the sold item or a loan condition. However, it also highlights a key exception: the sale of a sandal with the condition that the seller cobbles it is valid by istihsan, based on established custom. Understanding why some conditions are invalid and why custom can override the general hadith prohibition is crucial for applying Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed invalid conditions
List each condition mentioned: seller's use of the slave/house, buyer's loan, buyer's gift, non-delivery of the sold item, and seller sewing cloth into a shirt. Note that these are conditions not required by the contract and benefit one party.
2
Understand the legal ruling for invalid conditions
The contract becomes fasid (invalid) because these conditions constitute either ijarah fi al-bay' (lease within sale) if compensated, or i'arah fi al-bay' (loan within sale) if uncompensated. The hadith prohibits 'two transactions in one' and 'selling with a condition,' making the sale impermissible.
The ruling is supported by dalil naqli (hadith) and the illa of causing potential dispute.
3
Recognize the valid exception: sandal with cobbling condition
The sale of a sandal with the condition that the seller cobbles it or attaches the strap is valid by istihsan. This contradicts the general rule, but is justified by established custom ('urf) where people transact thus without disapproval.
This exception is analogous to istisna' and hiring dyers, wet nurses, and cuppers.
4
Analyze the reasoning behind istihsan
The passage anticipates an objection: custom cannot override a clear hadith. The response is that the hadith's underlying rationale (illa) is to prevent disputes. Custom achieves the same goal by eliminating dispute, so it aligns with the hadith's intent.
This illustrates the Hanafi use of istihsan as a method of reasoning that balances text with practical custom.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Zufar held the sandal condition invalid by qiyas (strict analogy), but the majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) adopted istihsan. The mufta bihi (operative opinion) is that the condition is valid.
The dispute highlights the hierarchy of dalil: istihsan can override qiyas when custom is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يستخدم البائع: أي لو باع عبداً على أن يستخدم البائع، أو داراً على أن يسكن البائع لم يجز؛ لأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وفيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين؛ لأن الخدمة والسكنى لو يقابلهما شيء من الثمن يكون إجارة في البيع، ولو كان لا يقابلهما شيء يكون إعارة في البيع، وقد نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم صفقتين في صفقة، ونهى أيضاً عن بيع وشرط، فلم يجز العقد فيفسد. أو يقرض إلخ : أي أو باع شيئًا على أن يقرض. أو يهدى له: أي أو باع شيئاً على أن يهدي المشتري. لا يسلمه: أي على أن لا يسلم المبيع. وثوب أي ولم يجز أيضاً بيع ثوب على أن يقطعه البائع ويخيطه قميصاً؛ لأنه على نهى عن صفقتين في صفقة واحدة. وصح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه أو يشركه استحسانا؛ لأن فيه تعامل الناس به من غير نكير، ولهذا يجوز الاستصناع واستيجار الصباغ والظئر والحجام وإن كانت إجارة على استهلاك الأعيان. فإن قيل: كون الشرط مفسدا لبيع ثابت بالحديث والتعامل المتعارف ليس بحاكم عليه؟ قلت: إن الحديث معلل بالإفضاء إلى المنازعة المخرج للعقد من الذي قصد به، وهو قطع المنازعة، والعرف يقطع النزاع، فكان موافقا لمعنى الحديث.
Or the seller uses (the slave): i.e., if he sells a slave on condition that the seller uses him, or a house on condition that the seller resides in it, it is not permissible; because it is a condition not required by the contract and contains a benefit for one of the contracting parties. Because if the service and residence are compensated by part of the price, then it is a lease within the sale; if not compensated, then it is a loan within the sale. And the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited two transactions in one, and also prohibited selling with a condition, so the contract is not permissible and becomes invalid. Or loans, etc.: i.e., or he sells something on condition that he gives a loan. Or a gift to him: i.e., or he sells something on condition that the buyer gives a gift. Or he does not deliver: i.e., on condition that he does not deliver the sold item. And cloth: i.e., it is also not permissible to sell a cloth on condition that the seller cuts and sews it into a shirt; because it is prohibited as two transactions in one. And the sale of a sandal on condition that he cobbles it or attaches the strap is valid by istihsan; because people customarily deal with it without disapproval. Hence, istisna' and hiring a dyer, wet nurse, and cupper are permissible, even though they are leases involving consumption of the objects. If it is said: that the condition invalidates the sale is established by hadith, and customary dealing does not override it? I say: the hadith is reasoned by leading to dispute which removes the contract from its intended purpose, which is to cut off dispute. And custom cuts off dispute, so it is in accordance with the meaning of the hadith.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage lists several conditions that invalidate the sale: seller's use of the sold item, buyer's loan, gift, delayed delivery, and the seller sewing the cloth into a shirt. These are prohibited because they constitute two transactions in one or a sale with a condition that benefits one party and is not intrinsic to the contract. The hadith prohibition is cited. However, the sale of a sandal with the condition that the seller cobbles it or attaches the strap is valid by istihsan, based on established custom (urf). The commentary addresses a potential objection: that custom cannot override a hadith. The response is that the hadith's underlying rationale (illa) is to prevent disputes, and custom achieves that same purpose. Therefore, custom can make such conditions permissible, as seen in the permissibility of istisna' (manufacturing contracts) and hiring professionals like dyers, wet nurses, and cuppers, which also involve consumption of the object.

Key Terms
إجارة في البيع (ijarah fi al-bay'): A lease (for service) included within a sale, treated as two transactions in one.
إعارة في البيع (i'arah fi al-bay'): A loan (for use) included within a sale, also two transactions.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference departing from strict qiyas due to custom or necessity.
عرف ('urf): Custom that is consistent and accepted by people without disapproval.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the sale of a sandal with the cobbling condition valid by istihsan?
A) Because cobbling is essential to the sale
B) Because custom allows it and it prevents disputes
C) Because the hadith does not apply to sandals
D) Because the condition is required by the contract

Key Takeaway
Conditions that benefit one party and are not intrinsic to the sale generally invalidate it, but custom can validate them via istihsan if they prevent disputes.

Invalid Stipulations in Sale (Shurut Fasida)
Lists and explains stipulations that invalidate a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid conditions in a sale and state the Hanafi ruling, applying the distinction between conditions that invalidate and those allowed by istihsan.

Core Idea
A sale is fasid by any condition not inherent that benefits one party, unless custom removes dispute (istihsan).

The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes between conditions that invalidate a sale and those that are permitted. This lesson examines the rules and exceptions for stipulations in sales, using the author's treatment in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. You will encounter examples like excepting a slave-girl's pregnancy, retaining use of sold property, and the fascinating shoe-shop custom that overrides strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the principle that any condition not inherent to the contract and benefiting one party invalidates the sale (makes it fasid). Base this on the hadith prohibition of 'two transactions in one' and 'sale with condition'.
The rule applies to commutative contracts like sale, lease, etc.
2
Analyze the exception of pregnancy and three categories
Study the example of selling a slave-girl while excepting her pregnancy. Understand why the exception is invalid and the three ranks of contracts regarding such an exception: invalid in both, valid contract/void exception, valid both.
The fetus cannot be sold independently, so its exception is a corrupt condition.
3
Examine specific invalid conditions
Review conditions like seller's continued use, buyer lending/giving gift, non-delivery, and tailoring into a shirt. For each, identify how the condition benefits one party and is not inherent, thus invalidating the contract.
These are all explicitly prohibited by the hadith.
4
Understand the istihsan exception for the shoe sale
Analyze the shoe example: selling a shoe on condition that the seller makes and straps it is valid by istihsan. Understand Imam Zufar's qiyas objection and the Hanafi response based on custom preventing dispute.
This illustrates the role of custom in overriding strict analogy.

أو إلا حملها : أي لم يجز بيع الأمة إلا حملها، وفي العبارة تسامح؛ لأن مقتضى العطف أن يكون التقدير: أو بيع أمة على أن يستثني حملها، وليس مراداً، ووجه عدم جواز بيع الحمل أن ما لا يصح إفراده بالعقد لا يصح استثناؤه، والحمل لا يجوز إفراده بالبيع، فكذا استثناؤه، وإذا لم يصح بقي شرطا فاسداً وفيه نفع للبائع، والبيع يبطل. ثم استثناء الحمل في العقود على ثلاث مراتب الأول يفسد فيه العقد والاستثناء كالبيع والإجارة والكتابة والرهن والثاني العقد فيه جائز ، والاستثناء باطل كالهبة والصدقة والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم عمد، فلا يبطل العقد ويبطل الاستثناء، ويكون الحمل تابعاً للأم في هذه العقود. والثالث: يجوز فيه العقد والاستثناء وهو الوصية، حتى لو أوصى بجاريته لإنسان إلا حملها صح، وكذا لو أوصى بحملها الآخر صح؛ لأن الوصية أخت الميراث، والميراث يجري فيه فكذا الوصية [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٩/٢، ٤٠] (فتح) أو يستخدم البائع: أي لو باع عبداً على أن يستخدم البائع، أو داراً على أن يسكن البائع لم يجز؛ لأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وفيه منفعة لأحد المتعاقدين؛ لأن الخدمة والسكنى لو يقابلهما شيء من الثمن يكون إجارة في البيع، ولو كان لا يقابلهما شيء يكون إعارة في البيع، وقد نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم صفقتين في صفقة، ونهى أيضاً عن بيع وشرط، فلم يجز العقد فيفسد عيني، جوهري) أو يقرض إلخ : أي أو باع شيئًا على أن يقرض. (عيني) أو يهدى له: أي أو باع شيئاً على أن يهدي المشتري. (عيني) لا يسلمه: أي على أن لا يسلم المبيع. وثوب أي ولم يجز أيضاً بيع ثوب قميصاً: للمشتري؛ لأنه علي نهى عن صفقتين في صفقة واحدة. (عيني) وصح بيع إلخ من حذوت النعل بالنعل إذا قدرت كل واحد منهما على صاحبه وأراد بالنعل الصرم من تسمية الشيء باسم ما يؤول إليه. وقوله : "ويشركه" الضمير المنصوب للنعل بالمعنى الحقيقي على طريق الاستخدام وهو من الشرك بمعنى وضع الشراك على النعل وهو سيرها الذي ظهر القدم، وقال زفر: لا يجوز، وهو القياس؛ لأن فيه شرطاً لا يقتضيه العقد، وجه الاستحسان: تعامل الناس به من غير نكير، ولهذا يجوز الاستصناع واستيجار الصباغ والظئر والحجام وإن كانت إجارة على استهلاك الأعيان. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٠/٢] فإن قيل: كون الشرط مفسدا لبيع ثابت بالحديث والتعامل المتعارف ليس بحاكم عليه؟ قلت: إن الحديث معلل بالإفضاء إلى المنازعة المخرج للعقد من الذي قصد به، وهو قطع المنازعة، والعرف يقطع النزاع، فكان موافقا لمعنى الحديث. (عيني، فتح، چلپی)
Or except its pregnancy: i.e., the sale of a slave-girl except her pregnancy is not permissible. In the expression there is leniency because the requirement of the conjunction would be that the estimation is: or a sale of a slave-girl on condition that her pregnancy is excluded, and that is not intended. The reason for non-permissibility of selling the pregnancy is that whatever cannot be made independent in a contract cannot be excepted. The pregnancy cannot be sold independently, so likewise its exception. If it is not valid, it remains a corrupt condition containing benefit for the seller, and the sale is invalidated. Then the exception of pregnancy in contracts is on three levels: first, the contract and the exception are both invalidated, like sale, lease, kitabah, and pledge; second, the contract is valid and the exception is void, like gift, charity, marriage, khul', and settlement for intentional homicide, so the contract is not invalidated and the exception is void, and the pregnancy follows the mother in these contracts; third, both the contract and exception are valid, which is the will, so if one bequeaths his slave-girl to someone except her pregnancy, it is valid, and likewise if he bequeaths her pregnancy to another, it is valid, because the will is the sister of inheritance, and inheritance operates in it, so likewise the will. (Fath) Or the seller uses: i.e., if he sold a slave on condition that the seller uses him, or a house on condition that the seller lives in it, it is not permissible, because it is a condition not required by the contract and it contains benefit for one of the contracting parties; because service and residence, if they are met by something from the price, it is hire in the sale, and if they are not met by anything, it is a loan in the sale. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prohibited two transactions in one transaction, and also prohibited a sale with a condition, so the contract is not permissible and thus invalidates. ('Ayni, Jawhari) Or lends etc.: i.e., he sold something on condition that he lends. Or gives a gift to him: i.e., he sold something on condition that the buyer gives a gift. He does not deliver: i.e., on condition that he does not deliver the sold item. And a cloth: i.e., it is also not permissible to sell a cloth as a shirt for the buyer, because he prohibited two transactions in one transaction. And the sale is valid etc. from 'hadaftha al-na'la bi al-na'l' when you measure each one of them to its companion. He intended by 'na'l' the sandal, from naming a thing by the name of what it ends up being. His saying 'and he shares' the accusative pronoun refers to 'na'l' in the real sense via usage, and it is from 'shirk' meaning placing the strap on the sandal, which is the thong that appears on the foot. Zufar said: it is not permissible, and that is the analogy, because it contains a condition not required by the contract. The reason for istihsan: the dealings of people with it without rejection, and for this reason it is permissible to commission manufacture and hire the dyer, wet nurse, and cupper, even if it is a hire for consumption of objects. Then if it is said: The condition corrupting the sale is established by hadith, and common custom is not a judge over it? I say: The hadith is reasoned by leading to dispute, which removes the contract from what was intended by it, namely cutting off dispute, and custom cuts off dispute, so it was in agreement with the meaning of the hadith.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining various stipulations that invalidate a sale (bay' fasid) in the Hanafi school. The author begins with the example of selling a slave-girl while excepting her pregnancy. The exception of pregnancy is invalid because the fetus cannot be sold independently; hence the condition is corrupt and benefits the seller, voiding the sale. He then categorizes contracts into three ranks regarding exception of pregnancy: in commutative contracts like sale, lease, kitabah, and pledge, both contract and exception are void; in gratuitous contracts like gift, charity, marriage, khul', and settlement for intentional homicide, the contract remains valid but the exception is void, and the pregnancy follows the mother; in wills, both are valid because the will resembles inheritance. Next, he covers conditions where the seller retains use of the slave or house, or where the buyer must lend money or give a gift, or the seller delays delivery, or the seller tailors cloth into a shirt—all void due to the hadith prohibiting two transactions in one and a sale with a condition. However, the sale of a shoe on condition that the seller makes and straps it is valid by istihsan (juristic preference), as common custom permits it, even though Zufar disagreed by strict analogy. The passage concludes by defending istihsan against the objection that the hadith should override custom: the hadith's rationale is preventing dispute, and custom eliminates dispute, so it aligns with the hadith's meaning.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict analogy for reasons of custom, necessity, or equity.
شرط فاسد (shart fasid): A condition that is not part of the contract and benefits one party, rendering the contract corrupt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is selling a shoe with the condition that the seller makes and straps it valid by istihsan?
A) Because the condition is required by the contract
B) Because people customarily deal in such transactions and it prevents dispute
C) Because Imam Zufar said it is valid
D) Because the hadith permits it

Key Takeaway
Conditions that benefit one party and are not inherent to the sale invalidate it, unless custom renders them acceptable by preventing dispute.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Deferment to Unknown Times Invalidates Sale
Ruling with proof: lists invalid deferments and exception for known durations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which deferments invalidate a sale due to jahalah and explain the exception when the term becomes known.

Core Idea
A sale to an unknown future time is invalid; but if the time becomes fixed, the sale is valid.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies that deferring payment to an uncertain future event renders the sale fasid (voidable). However, there is a crucial exception: if the parties know the exact duration at the time of contracting—such as the Christian fast of fifty days once it has started—the sale is valid. The text also distinguishes sale from kafalah, where minor uncertainty is tolerated. Understanding this helps avoid invalid contracts in daily transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify invalid deferments
List the events mentioned as invalid: Nowruz, Mehrajan, Christian fast, Jewish break-fast, arrival of pilgrim, harvest, threshing, grape-picking. Note that their times are unknown at contract formation.
These are examples of ajal majhul (unknown term).
2
Understand the rationale
The ruling is based on jahalah (ignorance) leading to dispute. The contract is fasid because the term is not specified with certainty.
This is a talil argument: the illa is jahalah.
3
Apply the exception
If the parties know the term (e.g., Christian fast period is 50 days once started), the sale is valid. The chosen madhhab accepts this because jahalah is removed.
The text says this is the mufta bihi position.
4
Distinguish sale from kafalah
In kafalah (guarantee), minor ignorance is tolerated because it is a gratuitous contract. In sale (commutative), no ignorance is tolerated, even minor.
The difference is based on the nature of the contract.
5
Distinguish minor vs. excessive ignorance
Minor ignorance: uncertainty only in timing (advance/delay). Excessive ignorance: uncertainty in existence (e.g., wind blowing). The latter is always invalid.
This distinction applies to both sale and kafalah.

Source Text

لا البيع إلى النيروز والمهرجان وصوم النصارى وفطر اليهود إن لم يدر العاقدان ذلك، وإلى قدوم الحاج والحصاد والدياسة والقطاف، ولو كفّل لا البيع إلخ: فساد البيع لجهالة الأجل، وهي مفضية إلى المنازعة، ولو باع مطلقاً عن هذه الآجال، ثم أجل الثمن إليها لم يفسد؛ لكونه تأجيلاً للدين والمفسد ما كان في صلب العقد. قال العيني: وهما معربان، والأصل ركان الأول : هو أول يوم في طرف الربيع، والثاني: هو أول يوم في طرف الخريف. وقال في "تاريخ كوشيار": "النيروز" أول اليوم من فروري ماه، والمهرجان بكسر الميم وسكون الهاء، وهو متعدد، مهرجان العامة، وهو اليوم السادس عشر من مهرماه القديم، ومهرجان الخاصة وهو اليوم الحادي والعشرين منه، وفي ذلك اليوم ظفر فريدون بالضحاك"، وحكي عن أبي حفص الكبير لله قال : إن رجلا لو عبد الله خمسين سنة وأهدى إلى بعض المشركين بيضة يريد به تعظيم ذلك اليوم، فقد كفر . رمز الحقائق: ٤٠/٢] (فتح) إلى النيروز: معرب نوروز، وهو أول يوم من نزول الشمس في برج الحمل. (ط) عيني) والمهرجان: أي وإلى مهرجان، وهو معرب مهرگان، وهو أول يوم من نزول الشمس في برج الميزان. (ط، عيني) وصوم النصارى إلخ والمعنى لا يصح البيع بتأجيل الثمن إلى صوم النصارى وفطرهم، وإلى فطر اليهود وصومهم فاكتفى بذكر أحدهما؛ لأن هذه الآجال مجهولة، فتفضي إلى المنازعة، حتى إذا علم المتعاقدان ذلك جاز، وقيل: إذا باع إلى فطر النصارى بعد ما شرعوا في صومهم جاز، وهو قول صحيح، وهو المذهب المختار عندنا؛ لأن مدة صومهم بالأيام، وهي معلومة رمز الحقائق: ٤٠/٢] وهي خمسون يوما، فلا جهالة حينئذ؛ لأنهم يببتدؤون الصوم من نيروز ويعيدون بعد خمسين يوما، والنيروز غير معلوم لا يتعين إلا بظن وممارسة بعلم النجوم، أما إذا دخل صومهم فيوم فطرهم معلوم، واليهود بعكسهم. (فتح) وإلى قدوم أي ولا يصح أ أيضاً مؤجلاً إلى قدوم الحاج والحصاد بفتح الحاء وكسرها، وهو قطع الزرع، وقرئ بهما، قوله تعالى: ﴿وَآتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ ﴾ (الأنعام: ١٤١) وكذا لا يجوز إلى الجزاز، وهو جز الصوف، وإلى الجذاذ - بالجيم والذال المعجمة - وهو قطع الثمار، وبالدال المهملة خاص بالنخلة، وجه عدم الجواز: جهالة الأجل؛ لأن هذا يتقدم ويتأخر رمز الحقائق ٤٠/٢] والدياسة أي وإلى الدياسة، وهي أن يوطأ الزرع بقوائم الدواب. (عيني) والقطاف: أي وإلى القطاف وهو بفتح القاف وكسرها قطع العنب من الكرم. (عيني، صراح) ولو كفل إلخ: لأنها جهالة يسيرة، وهي محتملة في الكفالة؛ لكونها تبرعاً، ألا ترى أنها تتحمل في أصل الدين بأن يكفل بما ذاب على فلان ففي وصفه ،أولى بخلاف الفاحشة كالكفالة إلى هبوب الريح، ومجيء المطر. أما البيع فمعاوضة، ومبادلة المال بالمال، فيكون مبناه على المضايقة، فلا تحتمل فيه الجهالة أصلاً، وإن كانت يسيرة، والفرق بين اليسيرة والفاحشة أن اليسيرة هي ما كان فيها الاختلاف في التقديم والتأخير، ولو كان الاختلاف في وجودها كهبوب الريح كانت فاحشة. (عيني، فتح)
Not the sale to Nowruz, Mehrajan, the fast of Christians and the breaking of fast of Jews if the two contracting parties do not know that, and to the arrival of the pilgrim, the harvest, the threshing, and the grape-picking. And if he guarantees... The corruption of the sale is due to ignorance of the term, which leads to dispute. If he sold absolutely without these terms, then later deferred the price to them, it is not corrupted, because it is a deferment of debt, and the corrupting factor is what is in the substance of the contract. Al-'Ayni said: They are both Arabized, and the origin is... The first is the first day at the edge of spring, the second is the first day at the edge of autumn. And it was said in 'Tarikh Koushiyar': Nowruz is the first day of Farvardin month, and Mehrajan with kasr of meem and sukoon of ha, and it is multiple: the public Mehrajan is the sixteenth day of the ancient Mehr month, and the private Mehrajan is the twenty-first day thereof, and on that day Fereydun defeated Zahhak. And it was narrated from Abu Hafs al-Kabir: If a man worshipped Allah for fifty years and then gifted an egg to some polytheist intending to honor that day, he has committed kufr. To Nowruz: Arabized of Nowruz, and it is the first day of the sun's descent into the constellation of Aries. And the fast of Christians etc.: The meaning is that the sale is not valid by deferring the price to the fast of Christians and their breaking of fast, and to the breaking of fast of Jews and their fast, so he sufficed with mentioning one of them, because these terms are unknown, thus leading to dispute, so if the two contracting parties know that, it is permissible. And it was said: if he sells to the breaking of fast of Christians after they have started their fast, it is permissible, and it is a sound statement, and it is the chosen madhhab with us, because the duration of their fast is in days, and it is known... it is fifty days, so there is no ignorance then, because they begin the fast from Nowruz and break it after fifty days, and Nowruz is not known, it is not determined except by speculation and practice of astrology. But if their fast has entered, the day of their breaking of fast is known. And the Jews are the opposite. And to the arrival: it is also not valid deferred to the arrival of the pilgrim, and the harvest... and likewise it is not permissible to the shearing, which is shearing of wool, and to the cutting, which is cutting of fruits... the reason for non-permissibility is ignorance of the term, because this comes early and late. And if he guarantees etc.: because it is minor ignorance, and it is tolerated in guarantee because it is gratuitous. Do you not see that it bears in the original debt by guaranteeing what is owed by so-and-so? So in its description it is more fitting, unlike excessive ignorance like guarantee to the blowing of the wind or the coming of rain. As for sale, it is a commutative contract and exchange of property for property, so its basis is strictness, so it does not tolerate ignorance at all, even if minor. The difference between minor and excessive is that minor is where the difference is in advance and delay, and if the difference is in its existence like the blowing of wind, it is excessive.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining that deferring payment to unknown times (jahalah fi al-ajal) invalidates a sale due to the uncertainty leading to dispute. The author lists examples: Nowruz (Persian New Year), Mehrajan (autumn festival), Christian fasting period, Jewish breaking of fast, arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, and grape-picking. All these are uncertain because their exact times vary. However, there is an exception: if the contracting parties know the time (e.g., Christian fast period is fifty days if they have already started), the sale is valid. This is the chosen Hanafi position. The passage also distinguishes between sale and guarantee (kafalah): in kafalah, minor uncertainty regarding time is tolerated because it is a gratuitous contract, whereas sale is a commutative contract and strictly requires certainty. The difference between minor and major ignorance is whether the uncertainty is only in timing (advance/delay) or in existence (e.g., wind blowing). The commentary cites historical origins of Nowruz and Mehrajan and warns that honoring them leads to kufr, even with an egg.

Key Terms
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance or uncertainty regarding essential terms, here the time of payment, leading to potential dispute.
أجل مجهول (ajal majhul): An unknown or uncertain term for payment, which invalidates the sale.
كفالة (kafalah): Guarantee; a contract where a person becomes surety for another's debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider a sale with payment deferred to 'harvest time' invalid?
A) Because harvest is not a religious event
B) Because the exact harvest date is uncertain at contract formation
C) Because both parties must know the exact day for any deferred payment
D) Because harvest is a seasonal event

Key Takeaway
A sale with deferred payment to an unknown event is invalid due to jahalah; but if the event's duration is fixed and known at contract time, it is valid.

Deferred Payment to Unknown Festivals: Validity Conditions
Ruling on sale with deferred payment to unspecified festivals and its validity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a sale deferred to an unknown future event is fasid and identify the conditions under which such a sale becomes valid.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if the payment term is unknown, but if the term becomes known the sale is valid.

In this lesson, we examine a classical Hanafi ruling regarding sales where the payment is deferred to an event whose date is unknown, such as ancient Persian festivals or religious observances. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the general principle: uncertainty (jahāla) in the term leads to dispute and invalidates the sale. However, there are important exceptions, particularly when the event's duration is fixed and known, as in the case of Christian Lent after it has begun. We will also explore the rare warning about glorifying pagan festivals, which underscores the gravity of such contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling and its basis
The matn opens with 'Not the sale to Nowruz, Mehregan, Christian Lent, and Jewish Passover...' This establishes that such sales are invalid (fasid) by default. The reason, as the sharh explains, is jahālat al-ajal (ignorance of the term), which leads to dispute.
Jahāla fāḥisha (gross uncertainty) invalidates a commutative contract.
2
Trace the underlying principle (jahala leads to dispute)
The commentary states that uncertainty leads to conflict between the parties. Since the aim of a contract is clarity and mutual consent, any term that is unknown at the time of contract makes the sale fasid. This is a core principle in Hanafi mu'āmalāt.
The principle is derived from the general prohibition of gharar.
3
Apply the exception for known terms
The text notes that if the two contracting parties know the date (i.e., they have specific knowledge of the event), the sale becomes valid. For example, if both parties know exactly when Nowruz falls in that year, the sale is valid.
Knowledge of the exact date removes the uncertainty.
4
Examine the specific case of Christian Lent and the Hanafi tarjih
The commentary mentions a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school: some held that sale to Christian Lent is invalid even if the fast has started. However, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that if the sale is made after the Christian Lent has begun, it is valid because the duration of the fast is known (50 days). The Jews' calendar is opposite, so their Passover is known before the fast begins.
The tarjih authority is Qadi al-'Ayni and others; the text says 'wa huwa al-madhhab al-mukhtār 'indanā'.
5
Distinguish between contractual term and subsequent deferral
The passage adds: if a sale is made absolutely (without mentioning these uncertain terms) and then the seller defers the payment to those times, it is valid. This is because the invalidating factor only applies when the uncertainty is part of the core contract; a post-contractual deferment of an existing debt is permissible.
The mufsid (invalidating factor) must be in the core contract ('asl al-'aqd).

Chapter of Invalid Sale. Book of Sales. Not the sale to Nowruz, Mehregan, Christian Lent, and Jewish Passover if the two contracting parties do not know that, and to the arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, and grape-picking. And if one guarantees to these times, it is valid. And if one waives the term before its arrival, it is valid. And whoever combines a free person and a slave, a slaughtered sheep and a dead one, the sale in both is void. And if one combines a slave and a mudabbar, and between his slave and another's slave, and owned property and waqf, the sale is valid in the qinn, his slave, and the owned property. 'Not the sale, etc.': Invalidity of the sale due to ignorance of the term, which leads to dispute. And if one sold absolutely without reference to these terms, then deferred the price to them, it is not invalid because it is a deferment of a debt and the invalidating factor is what is in the core of the contract. Al-Ayni said: Both are arabicized, the origin of the first is: it is the first day at the beginning of spring, and the second: it is the first day at the beginning of autumn. And in 'Tarikh Kushyar': 'Nowruz' is the first day of Farwarmah, and Mehregan with kasra of the mim and sukun of the ha, and it is multiple: the commoners' Mehregan is the sixteenth day of the old Mahrmah, and the special Mehregan is the twenty-first day thereof, and on that day Fereydun defeated Zahhak. And it is narrated from Abu Hafs al-Kabir that he said: 'If a man worshipped Allah for fifty years and then gave a gift of an egg to some polytheist intending thereby to glorify that day, he would be a disbeliever.' (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 40/2) (Fath) 'To Nowruz': arabicized from Nowruz, it is the first day of the sun's descent into Aries. (T, Ayni) 'And Mehregan': i.e., to Mehregan, arabicized from Mehregan, it is the first day of the sun's descent into Libra. (T, Ayni) 'And Christian Lent, etc.': The meaning is that the sale is not valid by deferring the price to the Christian Lent and their breaking of fast, and to the Jewish breaking of fast and their fast; he sufficed with mentioning one of each because these terms are unknown, so they lead to dispute. However, if the two contracting parties know that, it is permissible. And it was said: if he sells to the Christian breaking of fast after they have started their fast, it is permissible, and it is a correct statement, and it is the chosen madhhab with us, because the duration of their fast is by days, and it is known (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 40/2) and it is fifty days, so there is no ignorance then, because they begin the fast from Nowruz and celebrate after fifty days. Nowruz is unknown, only determined by conjecture and practice of astronomy. But if their fast has entered, their day of breaking fast is known. The Jews are the opposite. (Fath)
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening of the chapter of invalid sales. The matn states that several types of deferred sales are invalid: selling with deferment to Nowruz (Persian New Year), Mehregan (Persian autumn festival), the Christian Lent, the Jewish Passover, the arrival of pilgrims, harvest time, threshing time, and grape-picking time. The reason cited by the sharh is ignorance (jahāla) of the term of payment. Such uncertainty leads to disputes (tanāzuʿ). However, if the seller had absolute contract not mentioning these terms and later deferred the price to them, it is valid because the deferment is an addition after the contract; the invalidating element must be in the core contract. The commentary then explains the origins and dates of Nowruz and Mehregan: the former is the first day of spring (sun in Aries), the latter the first day of autumn (sun in Libra). A severe warning is quoted from Abu Hafs al-Kabir: even a lifetime of worship is nullified by honoring such a day with a trivial gift to a polytheist. The key ruling on Christian Lent and Jewish Passover: the default is invalidity due to the uncertainty of the event. But a correct opinion—the relied-upon position—is that if the sale is made to the Christian breaking of fast after they have already begun their fast, it is valid because the fast period is known (50 days). Before the fast begins, the date is unknown because it depends on astronomical observation (Nowruz). The Jews' calendar is opposite: their fast (Yom Kippur) depends on a fixed date, so the uncertainty is reversed.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bayʿ al-fāsid): An invalid sale due to a defect in the subject matter or consideration (e.g., uncertainty in time) that does not affect the core contract; it can become valid if the defect is removed before separation.
جهالة الأجل (jahālat al-ajal): Ignorance about the time of payment, which makes the contract uncertain and leads to dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because Nowruz is a pagan festival.
B) Because the exact date of Nowruz is unknown in advance.
C) Because the contract is contingent on a future event.
D) Because the price is not specified.

Key Takeaway
A sale with deferred payment to an unknown future event is fasid; knowledge of the term makes it valid, as illustrated by the specific rulings on Christian Lent and Jewish Passover.

Sale Deferred to Harvest and Suretyship Comparison
Ruling on sale deferred to uncertain events and comparison with suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sales deferred to uncertain events and explain why jahāla yasīra invalidates a sale but is tolerated in suretyship.

Core Idea
A sale deferred to an uncertain time is invalid; suretyship tolerates slight timing uncertainty.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rule that sales with deferred payment linked to uncertain events—like harvest—are invalid due to even slight ignorance of the term. We explore the underlying reason (jahāla yasīra) and contrast with suretyship, where such uncertainty is tolerated. The key is the nature of the contract: commutative vs gratuitous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states that sales deferred to events like the arrival of pilgrims, harvest, threshing, grape-picking are invalid. State the ruling clearly.
2
Locate the illa (effective cause)
The commentary explains that the reason is jahāla (ignorance) of the term (ajal) because these events can advance or recede in timing. Even though the event is certain to occur, the exact date is unknown.
3
Apply qiyas to the asl
Compare with a sale to a fixed date (asl) which is valid. The illa of jahāla in timing is present in the far' (deferred to harvest), making it invalid. This is a jaliyy qiyas.
Hanafi qiyas uses the illa of takhalluf fi al-taqdīm wa al-taʾkhīr.
4
Distinguish jahāla yasīra from fāḥisha
Slight (yasīr) uncertainty is variation in timing only; gross (fāḥish) uncertainty is about the event's existence. Sale tolerates neither, but the distinction matters for suretyship.
Yasīr: occurrence certain, time uncertain. Fāḥish: existence uncertain.
5
Contrast with suretyship (kafāla)
Suretyship is a gratuitous contract (tabarruʿ), so it tolerates slight jahāla, as seen in guaranteeing an unspecified debt. Sale is commutative (muʿāwaḍa), built on strictness, rejecting all jahāla.
Example: kafāla to harvest time is valid; sale to harvest time is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى قدوم أي ولا يصح أ أيضاً مؤجلاً إلى قدوم الحاج والحصاد بفتح الحاء وكسرها، وهو قطع الزرع، وقرئ بهما، قوله تعالى: ﴿وَآتُوا حَقَّهُ يَوْمَ حَصَادِهِ ﴾ (الأنعام: ١٤١) وكذا لا يجوز إلى الجزاز، وهو جز الصوف، وإلى الجذاذ - بالجيم والذال المعجمة - وهو قطع الثمار، وبالدال المهملة خاص بالنخلة، وجه عدم الجواز: جهالة الأجل؛ لأن هذا يتقدم ويتأخر رمز الحقائق ٤٠/٢] والدياسة أي وإلى الدياسة، وهي أن يوطأ الزرع بقوائم الدواب. (عيني) والقطاف: أي وإلى القطاف وهو بفتح القاف وكسرها قطع العنب من الكرم. (عيني، صراح) ولو كفل إلخ: لأنها جهالة يسيرة، وهي محتملة في الكفالة؛ لكونها تبرعاً، ألا ترى أنها تتحمل في أصل الدين بأن يكفل بما ذاب على فلان ففي وصفه ،أولى بخلاف الفاحشة كالكفالة إلى هبوب الريح، ومجيء المطر. أما البيع فمعاوضة، ومبادلة المال بالمال، فيكون مبناه على المضايقة، فلا تحتمل فيه الجهالة أصلاً، وإن كانت يسيرة، والفرق بين اليسيرة والفاحشة أن اليسيرة هي ما كان فيها الاختلاف في التقديم والتأخير، ولو كان الاختلاف في وجودها كهبوب الريح كانت فاحشة. (عيني، فتح)
And to the arrival: i.e., it is not valid also as deferred to the arrival of pilgrims, and harvest (with fatha and kasra on the ḥā), which is cutting of crops, and it was recited in both ways in the verse: 'And give its due on the day of its harvest' (Sūrat al-Anʿām 6:141). Likewise, it is not permissible to the jazāz (shearing of wool), and to the jidhādh (with jīm and dhāl muʿjama) which is cutting of fruits, and with the important dāl specifically for the palm tree. The reason for impermissibility is ignorance of the term, because this advances and recedes. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 40/2) 'And the threshing': i.e., to the threshing, which is to trample the crop with the feet of animals. (Ayni) 'And the grape-picking': i.e., to the grape-picking, which with fatḥ and kasr of the qāf is cutting grapes from the vine. (Ayni, Surah) 'And if he guarantees, etc.': Because it is a slight ignorance, and it is tolerated in suretyship because it is a gratuitous contract. Do you not see that it tolerates in the original debt such that one guarantees 'what is owed by so-and-so', so describing it is more appropriate, contrary to gross ignorance like suretyship to the blowing of the wind or the coming of rain. As for sale, it is a commutative contract, an exchange of property for property, so it is based on strictness, and it does not tolerate any ignorance at all, even if slight. The difference between slight and gross is that the slight is that in which the variation is in advancing and delaying (i.e., occurrence is certain but timing uncertain), and if the variation is in its very existence like the blowing of wind, it is gross. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn's ruling that selling with deferred payment to harvest time, threshing time, or grape-picking time is invalid. The commentary explains that these events, though bound to occur, can vary in timing from year to year (advance or recede), causing uncertainty. The verse 'Give its due on the day of its harvest' is cited to show that harvest is a known day, but that does not fix a future date for payment. The same applies to shearing wool and cutting fruits. Then the text contrasts sale with suretyship (kafāla): a guarantee can be made to a slightly uncertain time because it is a gratuitous contract (tabarruʿ), so it tolerates some ambiguity. For example, one can guarantee 'what is owed by so-and-so' even without specifying the exact amount or due date. But a sale is a commutative exchange (muʿāwaḍa), built on exactness, so it cannot tolerate any uncertainty. The distinction between slight (yasīr) and gross (fāḥisha) uncertainty is: slight uncertainty means the event will definitely happen but the exact time may vary (like harvest); gross uncertainty means the event itself is uncertain (like wind blowing). Sale invalidates even slight uncertainty; suretyship can tolerate slight but not gross.

Key Terms
جهالة يسيرة (jahāla yasīra): Slight uncertainty where the event is certain but its exact timing varies. Tolerated in gratuitous contracts but not in commutative contracts like sale.
الكفالة (al-kafāla): Suretyship or guarantee; a gratuitous contract thus more flexible regarding uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a sale deferred to harvest time considered invalid even though harvest will definitely occur?
A) Because harvest is not a known day.
B) Because the exact date of harvest can vary from year to year.
C) Because the price of crops is uncertain.
D) Because the buyer might not be able to pay at harvest.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is between slight jahāla (timing variation) and gross jahāla (existence uncertainty); sale rejects even slight jahāla, while suretyship tolerates it.

Waiving the Deferred Term Before Arrival: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on curing an invalid term

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on waiving an invalid deferred term before separation, explain the dispute with Imaam Zufar, and apply the distinction between core defects and external conditions.

Core Idea
Invalid conditions external to the core contract can be waived before separation to validate the sale.

A sale concluded with an invalid deferred term (e.g., to harvest time) appears irrevocably defective. But the Hanafi school holds that if the buyer waives the term before the parties leave the session, the sale becomes valid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, the dissent of Imaam Zufar, and how the Hanafis distinguish this case from ribā and temporary marriage.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling of the matn
Identify the core ruling: if the buyer waives the deferred term before it arrives, the sale becomes valid (صح). Note the condition: waiver must occur before the parties separate (tafarruq).
2
Identify Imaam Zufar's opposing view and his analogies
Zufar argues the contract was concluded invalid and cannot become valid by removing the defect, likening it to the ribā case (extra dirham) and marriage with a fixed term.
Zufar's analogies target the principle: an invalid act cannot be cured post-conclusion.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: distinction from ribā and marriage
In ribā, the excess is part of the countervalue (core defect). In marriage, a term changes the nature of the contract (nikāḥ → mutʿa). Here, the deferred term is an external condition (shart) that is slight (yasīr) and does not affect the core.
The invalidity arises only from potential dispute, which does not exist before the term arrives.
4
Determine the condition: waiver before separation
If the parties separate (tafarruq) before the waiver, the invalidity is confirmed and cannot be cured. This shows the ruling hinges on the contract session (majlis al-ʿaqd).
After separation, the defect is considered 'gross ignorance' (jahāla fāḥisha) and the contract is irrevocably void.
5
Conclude the muftā bihī position
The text declares the validating view as 'الصحيح' (the correct one). This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Imaam Zufar's view, though known, is not acted upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أسقط الأجل: أي لو باع إلى هذه الآجال، ثم أسقط المشتري الأجل قبل تحقق هذه الأوقات، يعني قبل أن يأخذ الناس في الحصاد وغيره وقبل قدوم الحاج صح البيع؛ لأن سبب الفساد قد ارتفع بالإسقاط، وهو محمول على ما قبل الافتراق، حتى لو تفرقا قبل الإسقاط تأكد الفساد، ولا ينقلب جائزاً اتفاقاً؛ لجهالة فاحشة كهبوب الريح ومجيء المطر، فلا ينقلب جائزاً وإن أبطل الأجل. وقال زفر : لا يصح؛ لأنه انعقد فاسداً، فلا ينقلب صحيحاً بإسقاط المفسد، كما إذا أسقط الدرهم الزائد عن بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين، وكما إذا تزوج امرأة إلى عشرة أيام ثم أسقط الأجل، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا: أن المفسد شرط خارج عن صلب العقد، وهو يسير، ولهذا اختلفت الصحابة فيه، فينقلب صحيحاً عند إزالته، أو نقول: العقد موقوفاً، فبالإسقاط تبين أنه كان جائزاً، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن فساده باعتبار أنه يفضي إلى المنازعة، وقبل مجيئه لا منازعة فلا يفسد بخلاف الدرهم الزائد؛ لأن الفساد فيه في صلب العقد؛ لأنه في أحد العوضين، وبخلاف الأجل في النكاح؛ لأنه عبد غير النكاح، وهو المتعة، والعقد لا ينقلب عقداً آخر. (عيني، فتح)
And if he waives the term: i.e., if he sold to these terms, then the buyer waived the term before these times materialize, meaning before people start harvesting etc., and before the arrival of pilgrims, the sale becomes valid. Because the cause of invalidity has been removed by waiver, and it is carried on that this is before separation. However, if they separated before the waiver, the invalidity is confirmed and does not become valid by agreement; for a gross ignorance like the blowing of wind and coming of rain, it does not become valid even if he cancels the term. Zufar said: It is not valid because it was concluded invalid, so it does not become valid by removing the invalidating factor, just as if he waives the extra dirham in the transaction of one dirham for two dirhams, and just as if he marries a woman for ten days then waives the term. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said this. Our proof: the invalidating factor is a condition external to the core of the contract, and it is slight. For this reason, the Companions differed on it. So it becomes valid upon its removal. Or we say: the contract is suspended, so by waiver it becomes clear that it was permissible. And this is the correct view. Because its invalidity is based on the fact that it leads to dispute, and before its arrival there is no dispute, so it is not invalid. Unlike the extra dirham because the invalidity there is in the core of the contract because it is in one of the countervalues. And unlike the term in marriage because it changes the nature of the contract (the term is for enjoyment, not marriage), and the contract does not turn into another contract. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: if a sale was concluded with an invalid deferred term (e.g., to harvest time), but before that time arrives the buyer waives the term, does the sale become valid? The matn says 'صح' (it becomes valid). The commentary explains that the waiver must occur before the parties separate (tafarruq); if they separate while the invalid term is still in effect, the invalidity is confirmed and cannot be cured. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagreed: an invalid contract cannot become valid by removing the defect later, analogizing to the case of ribā (one dirham for two) where the excess is part of the price and invalidity is in the core contract. The text rebuts: the invalidating term here is a condition (shart) external to the core of the contract (ṣulb al-ʿaqd) and is slight (yasīr). The invalidity arises only from the potential for dispute (tanāzuʿ), but before the time arrives, no dispute can occur. Thus, the contract is considered suspended (mawqūf); the waiver reveals that it was always valid. The analogies to ribā and marriage with a fixed term are distinguished: in ribā, the defect is in the countervalue (price) itself; in marriage with a term, the term changes the essential nature of the contract (from nikāḥ to mutʿa). Therefore, waiver can cure the sale. The text concludes that this is the correct view (ṣaḥīḥ).

Key Terms
صلب العقد (ṣulb al-ʿaqd): The essential core of the contract, i.e., the mutual exchange (ījāb and qabūl) and the countervalues (thaman and muthman).
فراق (tafarruq): The separation of the parties from the contract session (majlis al-ʿaqd).
فساد (fasād): Invalidity due to a defect that is not in the core of the contract; in Hanafi law, fāsid is distinct from bāṭil (void).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when does waiving the deferred term cure the invalidity of a sale?
A) At any time before or after separation.
B) Only before the parties separate from the contract session.
C) Only after the deferred time has arrived.
D) It never cures the invalidity.

Key Takeaway
A sale with an invalid deferred term becomes valid if the buyer waives the term before the parties separate, because the defect is external and slight, not in the core of the contract.

Combining Saleable and Non-Saleable Items in One Contract
Expansion of matn on mixed-item sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining a owned slave with a mudabbar, another's slave, or waqf in one sale, and explain Zufar's opposing view.

Core Idea
Sale valid for the owned part; non-saleable part does not invalidate the whole.

This lesson expands on the matn's ruling: if one combines in a single contract a owned slave with a mudabbar, one's own slave with another's slave, or owned property with waqf, the sale is valid for the owned item. The commentary clarifies that 'valid' means enforceable (nāfidh), not merely concluded, and notes Zufar's dissent who holds the entire contract void. Understanding this distinction is crucial for multi-item sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three combinations
The passage presents three scenarios: (1) owned slave + mudabbar, (2) own slave + another's slave, (3) owned property + waqf. List each.
Mudabbar: a slave whose freedom is deferred after master's death.
2
Determine the ruling for each
For each scenario, the sale is valid ('sahh') for the owned item: the slave in (1) and (2), the owned property in (3). The other item's sale is either suspended (mawqūf) or invalid.
The owned item must be clearly separable.
3
Understand the meaning of 'sahih'
The commentary specifies that 'sahih' here means nāfidh (enforceable), not just inʿiqād (concluded). A sale of another's slave is mawqūf (suspended) until permission.
This distinction prevents confusion with the free+slave case where the free part is unsellable.
4
Contrast with the free+slave case
In the earlier lesson, mixing a free person with a slave made the entire sale void because the free person is not legally subject to sale. Here, the mixed items are all property; the issue is enforceability, not validity.
Free person: not a commodity; mudabbar: still property though restricted.
5
Analyse Zufar's objection
Zufar (a Hanafi jurist) argues the entire contract is void because the subject of the contract is the total (al-majmūʿ), which cannot be partly valid. The majority view treats the items as separate for this purpose.
Zufar's position is not adopted in the relied-upon ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح في القن إلخ فيه لف ونشر ،مرتب، أي لو جمع في البيع بين عبد ومدبر، أو جمع بين عبده وعبد غيره، أو جمع بين ملك ووقف صح البيع في العبد فيما جمع بين العبد والمدبر، وفيما جمع بين عبده وعبد غيره، وصح في الملك فيما جمع بين ملك ووقف والمراد من الصحة النفوذ لا الانعقاد بدون النفوذ؛ إذ لو كان مراده ذلك لما صح الاقتصار على القن والعبد والملك؛ إذ بيع عبد الغير موقوف، وكان الأظهر أن يقول: "صح في العبد والملك"؛ إذ لا ذكر للفظ القن في الكتاب وقال :زفر لا يصح في الكل؛ لأن محل العقد المجموع، ولا يتصور ذلك؛
Valid in the qinn (owned slave), etc. There is orderly luff wa nashr (wrapped and unfolded) here. Meaning: if one combined in a sale a slave and a mudabbar, or combined his own slave and another's slave, or combined owned property and waqf, the sale is valid in the slave in the combination of slave and mudabbar, and in the combination of his slave and another's slave, and valid in the owned property in the combination of owned property and waqf. What is meant by validity is enforceability (nufūdh), not mere conclusion without enforceability; because if that was meant, it would not be sufficient to restrict to the qinn, his slave, and owned property, since the sale of another's slave is suspended (mawqūf). It would have been more apparent to say: 'valid in the slave and the owned property' since the word qinn is not in the book. Zufar said: it is not valid in all, because the subject of the contract is the total, and that cannot be conceived (i.e., a unit cannot be partly valid).
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage clarifies the matn's ruling: combining a slave (owned) with a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is deferred after master's death) in one sale is valid for the owned slave. Similarly, combining one's own slave with another's slave is valid for one's own slave (the other slave's sale is suspended). And combining owned property with waqf property is valid for the owned property. The commentary notes that 'valid' here means enforceable (nāfidh), not merely concluded, because the sale of another's slave is suspended (mawqūf) and requires the owner's permission. Zufar disagreed: he held the entire transaction void because the subject of the contract is the total (al-majmūʿ), and it is impossible for a single contract to be partly valid and partly void. This echoes the logic of the previous lesson: according to Zufar, the contract is one unit. However, the text implies that the relied-upon position (from the matn) is validity for the owned part. The distinction from the previous case (free + slave) is that here each item is potentially saleable (even the mudabbar can be sold under certain conditions? Actually mudabbar is not saleable until after the master's death, but it is a form of property; the owned slave is fully saleable. The commentary says the validity is nufūdh, not pure inʿiqād, indicating that the contract for the owned slave goes through, while the other part may be suspended or void. Zufar's objection is overruled by the majority view.

Key Terms
النفوذ (al-nufūdh): Enforceability; the contract is concluded and takes effect immediately. Used in contrast to inʿiqād (mere conclusion).
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose freedom is promised after the master's death; such a slave cannot be sold during the master's lifetime but remains property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the word 'sahih' (صح) mean in the matn's phrase 'sahh fi al-qinn'?
A) The contract is concluded (inʿaqada).
B) The contract is enforceable (nāfidh).
C) The contract is valid for both items.
D) The contract is void.

Key Takeaway
When a saleable and a restricted item are sold together, the sale is valid and enforceable for the saleable part; the restriction does not void the entire contract.

Dropping the Term in a Fasid Deferred Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute with rational proofs and analogies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that dropping a term before separation makes a fasid sale valid and evaluate Zufar's objection.

Core Idea
An external minor condition can be removed to cure a fasid sale, but not if the defect lies in the essence.

This lesson dives into an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a deferred sale that was initially fasid due to an uncertain term can become valid if the buyer drops the term before it matures. The core issue is whether the corrupting element can be removed or if the contract is irredeemably corrupted. We'll analyze the proofs from both sides and see how Abu Hanifa's view prevailed.

Guided Path
1
State the Base Ruling
Identify the initial ruling: the sale with a future term is fasid. Then identify the scenario discussed: buyer drops the term before its due time. What does Abu Hanifa rule?
The passage begins with how the ruling applies before the term matures.
2
Condition of Separation
Note the critical condition: the dropping is effective only if done before the parties separate. If they separate before dropping, the fasid is confirmed and cannot be cured.
This is agreed upon by all schools.
3
Zufar's Objection and Analogy
Present Zufar's view: the contract was contracted fasid, so removing one element cannot make it valid. He analogizes to dropping an extra dirham in a riba sale and to a marriage with a fixed term.
Zufar's qiyas is stronger according to the three other imams.
4
Hanafi Distinction
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response: the corrupting element here is external to the essence (sulb al-aqd) and minor, unlike the dirham case (corruption in countervalue) and marriage (changes contract type).
Also mention the alternative view that the contract was suspended (mawquf).
5
Tarjih and Thamarah
Identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) as the Hanafi view ('wa huwa al-sahih'). Understand the practical consequence: if buyer drops term before separation, sale is valid; in other schools, it remains invalid.
This difference matters for daily transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد إلى هذه الأوقات صح، وإن أسقط الأجل قبل حلوله صح. وإن أسقط الأجل: أي لو باع إلى هذه الآجال، ثم أسقط المشتري الأجل قبل تحقق هذه الأوقات، يعني قبل أن يأخذ الناس في الحصاد وغيره وقبل قدوم الحاج صح البيع؛ لأن سبب الفساد قد ارتفع بالإسقاط، وهو محمول على ما قبل الافتراق، حتى لو تفرقا قبل الإسقاط تأكد الفساد، ولا ينقلب جائزاً اتفاقاً؛ لجهالة فاحشة كهبوب الريح ومجيء المطر، فلا ينقلب جائزاً وإن أبطل الأجل. وقال زفر : لا يصح؛ لأنه انعقد فاسداً، فلا ينقلب صحيحاً بإسقاط المفسد، كما إذا أسقط الدرهم الزائد عن بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين، وكما إذا تزوج امرأة إلى عشرة أيام ثم أسقط الأجل، وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولنا: أن المفسد شرط خارج عن صلب العقد، وهو يسير، ولهذا اختلفت الصحابة فيه، فينقلب صحيحاً عند إزالته، أو نقول: العقد موقوفاً، فبالإسقاط تبين أنه كان جائزاً، وهو الصحيح؛ لأن فساده باعتبار أنه يفضي إلى المنازعة، وقبل مجيئه لا منازعة فلا يفسد بخلاف الدرهم الزائد؛ لأن الفساد فيه في صلب العقد؛ لأنه في أحد العوضين، وبخلاف الأجل في النكاح؛ لأنه عبد غير النكاح، وهو المتعة، والعقد لا ينقلب عقداً آخر. (عيني، فتح)
To these times it is valid, and if he drops the term before its due date it is valid. And the dropping of the term: i.e., if he sold to these terms, then the buyer dropped the term before these times materialize, meaning before the people start harvesting etc. and before the arrival of the pilgrims, the sale is valid; because the cause of corruption has been removed by the dropping, and it is considered [effective] before separation, such that if they separated before the dropping the corruption is confirmed, and it does not become valid by agreement; due to extreme ignorance like the blowing of the wind and the coming of rain, so it does not become valid even if he annuls the term. And Zufar said: it is not valid; because it was contracted as fasid, so it does not become valid by removing the corrupting element, just as if he drops the extra dirham in a sale of one dirham for two, and just as if he marries a woman for ten days then drops the term, and the three [imams] also said this. And for us: the corrupting element is a condition external to the essence of the contract, and it is minor, and for this the Companions differed about it, so it becomes valid upon its removal. Or we say: the contract is suspended, by the dropping it becomes clear that it was valid; and this is correct; because its corruption is due to leading to dispute, and before it comes there is no dispute so it does not become corrupted, unlike the extra dirham because the corruption there is in the essence of the contract, because it is in one of the two countervalues, and unlike the term in marriage because it is a different contract from marriage, which is for enjoyment, and the contract does not change into a different contract.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of dropping a term (ajal) before its maturation in a deferred sale. The base ruling states that if a sale is made to a future term that renders it fasid, but the buyer drops the term before that time arrives, the sale becomes valid. The commentator explains that this ruling applies only if the dropping occurs before the parties separate; after separation, the corruption is confirmed and cannot be undone even by dropping the term, because the ignorance becomes extreme (like selling until the wind blows). Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl disagreed, arguing that a fasid contract cannot become valid by removing the corrupting element, analogizing to the case of selling one dirham for two dirhams and then dropping the extra dirham, or a marriage with a fixed term. The 'three' (likely the other three founders of madhahib: Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) agree with Zufar. The Hanafi response (of Abu Hanifa) distinguishes: the corrupting element here is an external condition (shart) not part of the contract's essence (sulb al-aqd), and it is minor (yasir), so removing it restores validity. Alternatively, the contract is considered suspended (mawquf), and the dropping reveals it was valid from the start. The distinction from the dirham sale is that there the corruption is in the object itself (one of the countervalues), and from marriage, that contract is for enjoyment (mut'ah) and dropping the term would change the contract type. This ruling is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) as stated: 'wa huwa al-sahih'.

Key Terms
أَجَل (ajal): The specified future time for payment or delivery in a deferred sale.
إسقاط (isqāt): The act of removing a condition or term from the contract.
فساد (fasād): Corruption or invalidity due to a defect in the contract that does not affect the essence.
صلب العقد (sulb al-‘aqd): The essence/core of the contract, such as the countervalues.
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; the contract's validity depends on subsequent approval or removal of the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when does dropping the term (ajal) in a deferred sale make the sale valid?
A) Anytime before the term arrives
B) Only if done before the parties separate
C) Only if done after separation
D) It never makes it valid

Key Takeaway
A fasid deferred sale can become valid if the buyer drops the uncertain term before the parties separate, provided the defect is external to the contract's essence.
