Combining Permissible and Impermissible Items in One Sale
Ruling on a single sale combining permissible and impermissible items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) on a single sale combining a permissible and impermissible item, apply the condition of price specification, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa voids the entire sale; Sahibayn validate the permissible part if prices are specified.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a single contract that bundles a saleable item (e.g., a slave or slaughtered sheep) with a non-saleable one (a free person or dead animal). The disagreement hinges on whether specifying separate prices can split the transaction into two independent sales. Let us examine the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed items
Determine which items are property (mal) and which are not. Free persons and dead animals are not property, so they cannot be sold. Identify the permissible items (slave, slaughtered sheep) and impermissible ones (free person, dead animal).
Only property can be subject to a sale contract in Hanafi fiqh.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the entire sale is void (batil) regardless of price specification. His proof: the bargain is one (safqah wahidah), so it cannot be partly valid and partly void. Including a non-property item corrupts the whole contract.
He considers any condition that includes a non-saleable item as a corrupt condition.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if separate prices are specified, the contract splits into two transactions (safqatayn). The corruption is confined to the impermissible item; the sale of the permissible item becomes valid. If no separate prices are given, the entire contract is a sale by share (bi al-hissah) and impermissible.
They argue that price specification creates multiplicity of transactions.
4
Analyze the requirement for multiplicity
Abu Hanifa demands both repetition of the word of sale (takarur lafz al-bay') and price specification to create multiple transactions. The Sahibayn consider price specification sufficient.
This is the core usul difference: what constitutes a single or multiple contracts?
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the entire contract is void; neither party gets anything. Under the Sahibayn, if prices are specified, the permissible item transfers validly. This affects ownership and restitution.
The thamarah is the difference in outcome when prices are itemized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطل البيع فيهما: أي في الحر والعبد والشاة الذكية والميتة مطلقاً، سواء سمى لكل واحد منهما ثمناً أو لم يسم عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: إن سمى لكل واحد منهما ثمناً صح في العبد والشاة الذكية، وجه البطلان عنده: أن الحر والميتة لا يدخلان في العقد؛ لعدم شرطه، وهو المالية، فيكون العقد في الحر والميتة شرطاً بجواز العقد في العبد والذكية فيبطل. وعندهما يصح إن سمى؛ لأنه إذا بين ثمنهما صارا صفقتين، فيقدر الفساد بقدر المفسد، بخلاف ما إذا لم يسم لكل واحد ثمناً؛ لأنه يبقى بيعاً بالحصة ابتداء، وهو لا يجوز. وله: أن الصفقة متحدة، فلا يمكن وصفها بالصحة والفساد فيبطل. ومعنى الخلاف أن الصفقة تتعدد عندهما بمجرد تفصيل الثمن، فلا يسري الفساد من أحدهما إلى الأخرى، وبه قال الشافعي في قول واحد، وأحمد في رواية، وعنده: لابد لتعدد الصفقة من تكرر لفظ البيع مع تفصيل الثمن. (عيني، فتح)
The sale is void in both of them: i.e., in the free person and the slave, and the slaughtered sheep and the dead sheep, absolutely, whether he specified a price for each one or not, according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if he specified a price for each one, then the sale is valid in the slave and the slaughtered sheep. The reason for invalidity according to him (Abu Hanifa): that the free person and the dead animal are not included in the contract due to lack of the condition, which is being property, so the contract regarding the free person and the dead animal becomes a condition for the validity of the contract regarding the slave and the slaughtered, so it becomes void. And according to the two, it becomes valid if he specified, because when he specifies their prices they become two transactions, so the corruption is confined to the corrupt part. Unlike if he did not specify a price for each one, because then it remains a sale by share from the beginning, which is not permissible. And for him (Abu Hanifa): that the bargain is one, so it cannot be described partly as valid and partly as void, so it becomes void. The meaning of the disagreement is that the transaction becomes multiple according to the two merely by detailing the price, so the corruption from one does not spread to the other. And al-Shafi'i said this in one opinion, and Ahmad in one narration, and according to him (Abu Hanifa): for multiplicity of transactions it is necessary to repeat the word of sale along with detailing the price.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a single contract that combines a permissible item (a slave or a slaughtered sheep) with an impermissible item (a free person or a dead animal). According to Abu Hanifa, the entire sale is void whether or not separate prices were specified, because the contract is one and cannot be partly valid and partly invalid. The free person and dead animal are not property (mal), so they cannot be subject to sale; including them as a condition destroys the contract. The Sahibayn distinguish: if the seller specifies a price for each item, the contract splits into two transactions (safqatayn), and the corruption remains only in the invalid part. But if no separate prices are given, it becomes a sale by share (bi al-hissah) which is impermissible due to ignorance of the price allotment. The passage also notes that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold a similar view to the Sahibayn, while Abu Hanifa requires both repetition of the word of sale and specification of price for multiplicity.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A bargain/transaction; multiplicity of transactions (safqatayn) occurs when prices are specified.
بالحصّة (bi al-hissah): Sale by share or unspecified portion; impermissible due to ignorance of the price.
مال (māl): Property; a condition for an item to be saleable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if separate prices are specified
B) Always
C) Never
D) Only if the free person is released

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi dispute on bundled sales turns on whether price specification alone can split a single contract, with Abu Hanifa requiring repetition of the sale word for multiplicity.

Validity of Combining Saleable and Non-Saleable Items in One Sale
Ruling on validity when combining saleable items with partially or fully non-saleable items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining a slave with a mudabbar in one sale, explain the difference between the free person case and this case, and identify the practical consequence of the dispute with Zufar.

Core Idea
A sale combining a slave with a mudabbar is valid for the slave because the mudabbar enters the contract but exits by right.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common commercial scenario: selling a slave together with another item that has partial ownership restrictions. The Hanafi school distinguishes between items that are not property at all (like free persons) and items that are property but have attached rights (like mudabbar). The ruling here establishes that the sale of the owned slave remains valid and enforceable even when combined with such items.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and combined items
Recognise that the contract involves a saleable slave (qinn) combined with one or more items: mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad, another's slave, or waqf. Note that the items have varying degrees of transferability.
The passage also mentions combining owned property with waqf.
2
State the Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (with a condition on price specification), the sale of the qinn (or owned property in the waqf case) is valid and enforceable (nafidh). The price is allocated proportionally between the items.
Validity here means enforceability (nufudh), not mere formation; some cases require permission (e.g., another's slave).
3
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues the entire contract is void because the mudabbar etc. lack legal capacity (mahalliya) for sale. He analogises to the free person case where the contract is entirely void.
Zufar's view is that the contract cannot be divided; the whole subject matter must be valid.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes based on property status: mudabbar, mukatab, etc. are property (mal) and enter the sale contract. They are later excluded due to their own rights (e.g., tadbir). The free person never enters the contract. Evidence: a judge's ruling to sell a mudabbar is enforceable.
The difference (qiyas khafi) is that the non-saleable item here is still property, unlike a free person.
5
Apply the ruling to waqf and mosque
Combining owned property with waqf: the preferred view (asahh) is that the owned portion's sale is valid because waqf is property. But combining with a mosque (which is not property) voids the entire sale.
This shows the principle: non-property attached items render the whole invalid; property attached items only affect their own portion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح في القن إلخ فيه لف ونشر ،مرتب، أي لو جمع في البيع بين عبد ومدبر، أو جمع بين عبده وعبد غيره، أو جمع بين ملك ووقف صح البيع في العبد فيما جمع بين العبد والمدبر، وفيما جمع بين عبده وعبد غيره، وصح في الملك فيما جمع بين ملك ووقف والمراد من الصحة النفوذ لا الانعقاد بدون النفوذ؛ إذ لو كان مراده ذلك لما صح الاقتصار على القن والعبد والملك؛ إذ بيع عبد الغير موقوف، وكان الأظهر أن يقول: "صح في العبد والملك"؛ إذ لا ذكر للفظ القن في الكتاب وقال :زفر لا يصح في الكل؛ لأن محل العقد المجموع، ولا يتصور ذلك؛ لانتفاء المحلية في المدبر ونحوه كأم الولد والمكاتب، وقد جعل قبول العقد فيه شرطاً لصحة العقد في المال، فيفسد كالفصل الأول، والفرق بين الفصلين لأبي حنيفة مطلقاً، ولهما: إذا لم يفصل الثمن؛ لأن المدبر ونحوه يدخل تحت البيع، ثم ينقض البيع في حقه، فيقسم الثمن عليهما حالة البقاء، وهو غير مفسد، وفي الفصل الأول الحر ونحوه لا يدخل في البيع أصلاً، ولو جاز البيع فيما ضم إليه، لكان بيعاً بالحصة ابتداء فلا يجوز؛ لجهالة الثمن عند العقد، بخلاف النكاح حيث يجوز نكاح المحللة فيما إذا ضم إليها المحرمة، فعقد عليهما جملة؛ لأن النكاح لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة، ولا بجهالة المهر، والدليل على أن المدير وأم الولد والمكاتب وعبد الغير يدخل في البيع: أن القاضي لو قضى بجواز بيع المدبر وأم الولد ينفذ، وفي المكاتب ينفذ برضاه في الأصح، وفي عبد الغير بإجازة مولاه، وإنما يخرجون من العقد بعد الدخول؛ لاستحقاقهم أنفسهم. وأما في الملك والوقف ففيه روايتان في رواية يفسد في الملك؛ لأن البيع لا ينعقد على الوقف، فصار كما لو جمع بين حر وعبد، والأصح أنه يجوز في الملك؛ لأن الوقف مال، ولهذا ينتفع به انتفاع الأموال، غير أنه لا يباع لأجل تعلق حق الفقراء به، فصار الفساد ضعيفاً، وذلك لا يوجب فساد العقد فيما ضم إليه كالمدبر ونحوه، وقيد بالوقف للاحتراز عما لو ضم المسجد إلى الملك؛ لأن المسجد العامر ليس بمال، فيبطل البيع فيما ضم إليه، وفي "المحيط": لو باع قرية ولم يستثن ما فيها من المقابر والمساجد، الأصح الصحة في الملك؛ لأن المقابر والمساجد مستثنى عادة، وقيل: لا يصح؛ جمع العقد المال وغير المال، فيبطل في الكل. (عيني، فتح، كافي)
Valid regarding the slave etc.: there is a fold-and-spread ordering. Meaning: if he combines in a sale a slave and a mudabbar, or combines his own slave and another's slave, or combines owned property and waqf, the sale is valid regarding the slave in what combines a slave and a mudabbar, and regarding his slave and another's slave, and valid regarding the owned property in what combines owned property and waqf. And the meaning of validity here is enforceability, not mere formation without enforceability; because if that were intended, it would not suffice to restrict to the slave, his slave, and owned property, since the sale of another's slave is suspended. It would have been more apparent to say 'valid regarding the slave and owned property' since the word 'qinn' is not mentioned in the book. And Zufar said: it is not valid in all; because the subject matter of the contract is the whole, and that is not conceivable due to the lack of legal capacity in the mudabbar and similar (umm walad, mukatab), and he made the acceptance of the contract in it a condition for the validity of the contract in property, so it becomes invalid like the first chapter. The difference between the two chapters according to Abu Hanifa is absolute, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): when the price is not detailed, because the mudabbar etc. enter the sale, then the sale is rescinded in its respect, so the price is divided between them at the time of persistence, which is not corrupting. In the first chapter, the free person etc. does not enter the sale at all. And if the sale were permissible in the attached item, it would be a sale by share initially, which is impermissible due to ignorance of the price at the contract, unlike marriage where marriage with a free woman is permissible when a prohibited woman is appended, so he contracts on both together; because marriage is not invalidated by corrupt conditions nor by ignorance of the dowry. The evidence that the mudabbar, umm walad, mukatab, and another's slave enter the sale: that if a judge rules the permissibility of selling a mudabbar or umm walad, it is enforceable; for a mukatab, it is enforceable with his consent in the most correct view; for another's slave, with the permission of his master. They only exit the contract after entry due to their own right. As for owned property and waqf, there are two narrations: in one narration it becomes invalid regarding the owned property, because the sale is not contracted over waqf, so it becomes like combining a free person and a slave. The most correct opinion is that it is valid regarding the owned property, because waqf is property, and for this reason it is utilized like properties, except that it is not sold due to the right of the poor attached to it, so the corruption is weak and does not necessitate the corruption of the contract in the attached item, like the mudabbar etc. And it is restricted to waqf to exclude the case of appending a mosque to owned property, because a mosque that is in use is not property, so the sale becomes invalid in the attached item. In al-Muhit: if someone sells a village and does not exclude the cemeteries and mosques in it, the most correct is validity regarding the owned property, because cemeteries and mosques are customarily excluded. And it is said: it is not valid; because the contract combines property and non-property, so it becomes invalid in all.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage deals with cases where a saleable item (like a slave) is combined in one sale with items that are either partially owned or not fully transferable: a mudabbar (slave promised emancipation upon master's death), a mukatab (slave who contracted to purchase freedom), an umm walad (slave mother of master's child), another's slave, or waqf property. The matn apparently said 'valid regarding the slave' and the commentator explains that this validity means enforceability (nufudh), not mere formation, because the sale of another's slave is suspended on the owner's permission. Zufar argued that the whole contract is void because the subject matter cannot include mudabbar etc. The difference between this section and the previous one (free person/dead animal) is explained: in the previous case, the free person is not property at all, so it never enters the contract; here, these items do enter the contract but are later excluded due to their own rights, so the price can be split. The text also discusses waqf: there are two narrations about combining waqf with owned property; the preferred opinion (al-ashah) is that the sale of the owned property is valid, because waqf is property but just restricted from sale, so the corruption is weak. However, if a mosque (masjid) is combined, that is not property at all, so the sale of the attached owned property becomes void. The passage also references al-Muhit regarding the sale of a village containing cemeteries and mosques, where the custom of exclusion preserves validity.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who is promised freedom upon his master's death.
مكاتب (mukātib): A slave who has made a contract to purchase his freedom.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bore a child to her master and is therefore free upon his death.
وقف (waqf): Endowment property; its sale is restricted but it is still considered property.
نفوذ (nufūdh): Enforceability; the sale becomes binding after necessary permissions or conditions are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core difference between combining a slave with a mudabbar and combining a slave with a free person, according to the Hanafi school?
A) The mudabbar is property that enters the contract; the free person is not property.
B) The mudabbar requires price specification; the free person does not.
C) The mudabbar can be sold with the slave's consent; the free person cannot.
D) There is no difference; both void the entire contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds partial validity in combined sales where the non-saleable item is nonetheless property, based on the distinction between property that enters the contract and non-property that does not.

Possession in a Fasid Sale: Trust or Guarantee?
Ruling on the legal effect of the buyer taking possession in a voidable sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi view on whether the buyer's possession in a fasid sale creates a trust or a guarantee, and explain the qiyas proof used by the three imams.

Core Idea
In a fasid sale, the buyer's possession with permission creates guarantee, not trust, because it resembles usurpation.

When a buyer takes possession of goods in a voidable (fasid) sale, a critical question arises: is he holding them as a trustee (amanah) with no liability for accidental loss, or as a guarantor (madhmun) who must pay the value if they perish? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, the intra-madhhab disagreement, and the analogical reasoning that settles the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the buyer taking possession of the sold item in a fasid sale with the seller's permission. The ruling being debated is whether the goods are held as a trust (amanah) or a guarantee (daman).
The fasid sale is one where the contract is defective but the subject matter is property (mal).
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is a qiyas: the buyer's possession is likened to usurpation (ghasb). The text states: 'because he took possession for himself, so it resembles usurpation.'
3
Trace wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illah) is that the buyer takes possession for his own benefit, just as a usurper does. Therefore, he becomes liable for the goods, like a usurper.
This is a clear analogy (qiyas jali).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some earlier Hanafi scholars held that possession in a fasid sale is a trust, with no liability for destruction. However, the opinion of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that it is a guarantee.
The trust view is considered weak; the guarantee view is the preferred (mufta bihi) position.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Al-Sarakhsi and the author of al-Qunyah declare the guarantee view as correct (al-sahih). It is also supported by a further analogy: it is not inferior to possession on approval (al-maqboodh 'ala sawm al-shira'), which is already a guarantee.
Thus, the mufta bihi ruling is that the buyer is liable for the price or value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وفيه بيان أحكام البيع الفاسد وتصرفات البائع والمشتري في الثمن والمبيع، وفيما يكره من البيوع وما لا يكره. وحكم الشيء أثره فيتبع المؤثر. حكم البيع الباطل أنه إن هلك المبيع في يد المشتري فعند البعض يهلك أمانة فلا ضمان على المشتري، وعند البعض مضمونا بالقيمة أو المثل، واختاره السرخسي وغيره وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة، وفي القنية: أنه هو الصحيح؛ لكونه قبضه لنفسه فشابه الغصب؛ ولأنه لا يكون أدنى حالا من المقبوض على سوم الشراء. البيع الفاسد كالبيع بالخمر والخنزير واحترز به عن بيع الباطل فإنه لا يفيد الملك بالقبض. لأنه بأمر البائع أي بإذنه صريحا أو دلالة؛ لأن القبض بغير الإذن لا يفيد الملك أصلا. عوضيه: أي عوضي البيع وهما الثمن والمبيع. مال فلو كان أحدهما غير مال بطل البيع لا يفيد الملك بالقبض.
Section: Containing an explanation of the rulings of the fasid sale and the dispositions of the seller and buyer regarding the price and the sold item, and regarding what is disliked and not disliked among sales. The ruling of a thing is its effect and follows the effective cause. The ruling of the batil sale is that if the sold item perishes in the hands of the buyer, according to some it perishes as an amanah (trust) with no liability on the buyer, and according to some it is guaranteed by the value or the like, and al-Sarakhsi and others chose this, and it is the saying of the three imams. And in al-Qunyah: it is the correct view, because he took possession for himself so it resembles usurpation, and because it is not less than the possession on the basis of a deposit. The fasid sale is like the sale of wine or pork, and it is distinguished from the batil sale because the batil sale does not confer ownership by possession. Because it is with the command of the seller, i.e., with his express or implicit permission, because possession without permission does not confer ownership at all. Its two countervalues: i.e., the two countervalues of the sale, which are the price and the sold item. Property: so if one of them is not property, the sale is invalid and does not confer ownership by possession.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the rulings of the fasid sale, specifically the effect of the buyer taking possession. The commentator explains that a fasid sale is one like selling wine or pork (i.e., the subject matter is permissible to own but the contract has a defect). This is contrasted with a batil sale, where the subject matter itself is not property; possession in a batil sale confers no ownership. The key ruling: if the buyer takes possession of the subject matter in a fasid sale with the seller's permission (express or implied), the goods are held as a guarantee (madhmun). If the goods perish, the buyer must pay the price or value. This is the preferred view of al-Sarakhsi and others, and is also the opinion of the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). The opposing view (that it is a trust) is rejected. The condition for this guarantee is that the buyer took possession at the seller's command; otherwise, possession without permission does not confer ownership. Additionally, both countervalues (price and subject matter) must be property (mal); if one is not, the sale is invalid and possession does not affect ownership. The passage also references 'Ramz al-Haqa'iq' as a source.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fāsid): A voidable sale; the contract is defective but the subject matter is property.
بيع باطل (bay' bātil): A void sale; the subject matter is not property, so possession confers no right.
أمانة (amānah): Trust; the goods are held in trust, no liability for loss unless negligence.
مضمون (madhmūn): Guaranteed; the possessor is liable for loss or destruction.
قبض (qabḍ): Possession; taking physical control of the goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale, what happens if the buyer takes possession of the goods with the seller's permission and the goods are destroyed?
A) The buyer must pay the price or value
B) The buyer is not liable as it is a trust
C) The seller must return the price
D) The sale becomes valid

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer's possession with permission creates a guarantee (daman), not a trust, and the buyer is liable to compensate if the goods perish.

Combining a Non-Saleable Entity in a Sale: The Hanafi Intra-School Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining non-saleable entities in a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a sale that combines a saleable item with a non-saleable entity (mudabbar, umm walad, mukatab), and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Combining a non-saleable entity (like a mudabbar) with a saleable item: Abu Hanifa invalidates the whole; the Sahibayn invalidate only the part if the price is separable.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of including a non-saleable entity—such as a mudabbar, umm walad, or mukatab—in a single sale contract alongside a saleable item. The core question is whether the entire contract becomes invalid (fasid) or only the portion related to the non-saleable entity. Understanding this disagreement clarifies the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to contract validity and highlights the practical consequences for transactions involving restricted property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
Recognize that the dispute concerns a sale where the seller includes both a saleable item and an entity that has restricted saleability (like a mudabbar, umm walad, or mukatab) in one contract. The crucial factor is whether the price is stated as a lump sum or allocated separately.
This is not about a free person, which is absolutely non-saleable.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, the entire sale contract is invalid (fasid) regardless of price allocation. His reasoning: the non-saleable entity cannot be subject to sale, and including it creates an ambiguity (jahalah) in the price that spoils the whole contract.
He analogizes to combining a free person with a slave—the free person is not property, so the whole sale is invalid.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguish based on price separation. If the price is a lump sum (not allocated), the whole sale is invalid. If the price is separated (each item priced individually), the sale is valid for the saleable item and invalid only for the non-saleable entity.
They argue the mudabbar etc. enter the contract initially and are then rescinded, allowing the contract to stand for the rest.
4
Identify the key factor: price separation
The critical difference is whether the price is divided between the items at the time of contract. For the Sahibayn, a divided price allows the contract to survive for the saleable part; for Abu Hanifa, even a divided price does not salvage the contract.
This factor is also applied to waqf property and other similar cases.
5
Apply to waqf and mosque scenarios
The text extends the discussion to waqf (endowment) and mosques. For waqf, there are two narrations: one makes the contract invalid (like Abu Hanifa's view), the more correct view says it is valid because waqf is property (like the Sahibayn's approach). For a mosque, which is not property, including it invalidates the attached owned property.
This demonstrates how the principle is applied across different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قبض المشتري المبيع في البيع الفاسد بأمر البائع، وكل من عوضيه مال =: الواو للحال لانتفاء المحلية في المدبر ونحوه كأم الولد والمكاتب، وقد جعل قبول العقد فيه شرطاً لصحة العقد في المال، فيفسد كالفصل الأول، والفرق بين الفصلين لأبي حنيفة مطلقاً، ولهما: إذا لم يفصل الثمن؛ لأن المدبر ونحوه يدخل تحت البيع، ثم ينقض البيع في حقه، فيقسم الثمن عليهما حالة البقاء، وهو غير مفسد، وفي الفصل الأول الحر ونحوه لا يدخل في البيع أصلاً، ولو جاز البيع فيما ضم إليه، لكان بيعاً بالحصة ابتداء فلا يجوز؛ لجهالة الثمن عند العقد، بخلاف النكاح حيث يجوز نكاح المحللة فيما إذا ضم إليها المحرمة، فعقد عليهما جملة؛ لأن النكاح لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة، ولا بجهالة المهر، والدليل على أن المدير وأم الولد والمكاتب وعبد الغير يدخل في البيع: أن القاضي لو قضى بجواز بيع المدبر وأم الولد ينفذ، وفي المكاتب ينفذ برضاه في الأصح، وفي عبد الغير بإجازة مولاه، وإنما يخرجون من العقد بعد الدخول؛ لاستحقاقهم أنفسهم. وأما في الملك والوقف ففيه روايتان في رواية يفسد في الملك؛ لأن البيع لا ينعقد على الوقف، فصار كما لو جمع بين حر وعبد، والأصح أنه يجوز في الملك؛ لأن الوقف مال، ولهذا ينتفع به انتفاع الأموال، غير أنه لا يباع لأجل تعلق حق الفقراء به، فصار الفساد ضعيفاً، وذلك لا يوجب فساد العقد فيما ضم إليه كالمدبر ونحوه، وقيد بالوقف للاحتراز عما لو ضم المسجد إلى الملك؛ لأن المسجد العامر ليس بمال، فيبطل البيع فيما ضم إليه، وفي "المحيط": لو باع قرية ولم يستثن ما فيها من المقابر والمساجد، الأصح الصحة في الملك؛ لأن المقابر والمساجد مستثنى عادة، وقيل: لا يصح؛ جمع العقد المال وغير المال، فيبطل في الكل. (عيني، فتح، كافي)
And if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in an invalid sale by the seller's command, and both of its countervalues are property, then: the waw is for the state, due to the lack of capacity for saleability in the mudabbar and similar like the umm walad and the mukatab. Acceptance of the contract in it has been made a condition for validity of the contract regarding property, so it is invalid like the first section. The difference between the two sections is absolute for Abu Hanifa. For the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if the price is not separated, because the mudabbar and similar enter into the sale, then the sale is rescinded with respect to him, so the price is divided between them at the time of existence, and this is not invalidating. In the first section, the free person and similar do not enter the sale at all, and if the sale were valid in what is attached to it, it would be a sale by share initially, so it is not permissible due to ignorance of the price at the time of contract, unlike marriage where marriage to a permissible woman is allowed when a prohibited woman is attached to her, so the contract is concluded on both as a whole, because marriage is not invalidated by invalid conditions or ignorance of the dower. The evidence that the mudabbar, umm walad, mukatab, and slave belonging to another enter the sale: that if the judge ruled on the permissibility of selling a mudabbar and umm walad, it would be executed; regarding the mukatab, it is executed with his consent according to the most correct; regarding the slave of another, with his master's permission. They only exit from the contract after entry because they deserve themselves. As for the case of owned property and waqf, there are two narrations: in one narration, it invalidates with ownership because the sale is not concluded on waqf, so it becomes like combining a free person and a slave. The most correct is that it is permissible with ownership because waqf is property, and thus it is benefited from like property benefits, except that it is not sold due to the attachment of the right of the poor, so the invalidity is weak, and that does not necessitate invalidity of the contract regarding what is attached to it, like the mudabbar and similar. It is restricted to waqf to exclude what if a mosque is attached to owned property, because a populated mosque is not property, so the sale is invalid regarding what is attached to it. In al-Muhit: if one sells a village and does not exclude the cemeteries and mosques in it, the most correct is validity regarding the owned property because cemeteries and mosques are customarily excluded. It is said: it is not valid because the contract combines property and non-property, so it invalidates everything. (Ayni, Fath, Kafi)
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of combining a saleable item with a non-saleable entity (like a mudabbar, umm walad, mukatab, or slave belonging to another) in a single sale contract. The discussion arises from the condition 'both countervalues are property' in the ruling on invalid sale possession. The commentator explains that the waw in 'wa kullun min 'iwaḍayhi māl' is for the state, meaning both countervalues must be property. The question is: if a seller includes a non-saleable item (like a mudabbar) with a saleable item in one sale, does the entire contract become invalid or only that part? Abu Hanifa holds that the entire contract is invalid absolutely because the non-saleable item cannot be subject to sale, so the whole contract is spoiled. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) distinguish: if the price is not separated (i.e., a lump sum price), the contract is invalid for the whole; but if the price is separated, then the sale is valid for the saleable part and invalid for the non-saleable part, because the non-saleable item enters the contract initially but is then rescinded, and the price is divided proportionally. They compare this to the case of combining a free person with a slave: since a free person cannot be sold at all, the contract is entirely invalid. For a mudabbar etc., they can be sold in some circumstances (with judicial ruling or consent), so they are considered property. The text then discusses the evidential basis: a judge's ruling on selling a mudabbar or umm walad would be enforced, showing they are property. For waqf (endowment), there are two narrations: one says it invalidates the whole contract like combining free and slave; the more correct view is that it does not invalidate because waqf is property, except that it cannot be sold due to the right of the poor, so the invalidity is weak and does not spoil the rest. For a mosque, it is not property, so including it invalidates the attached owned property. Finally, the commentator reports from al-Muhit that if one sells a village without excluding cemeteries and mosques, the most correct view is validity for the owned part because those are customarily excluded; a minority opinion says the whole contract is invalid because it mixes property and non-property.

Key Terms
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death, like a tadbir contract.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah (manumission by payment) with his master.
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale contract. In this lesson, the separation of thaman is crucial for the Sahibayn's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire sale is invalid.
B) The sale is valid for both items.
C) The sale is valid for the saleable item and invalid for the mudabbar.
D) The sale is batil (void).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds an intra-school dispute on combining a non-saleable entity with a saleable item: Abu Hanifa invalidates the entire contract, while the Sahibayn allow partial validity if the price is separated; the latter view is often relied upon in practice.

Ownership by Possession in a Fasid Sale
Definition and ownership in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a fasid sale confers ownership upon possession and explain why the Hanafi school upholds this ruling against the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A fasid sale transfers ownership by possession if both countervalues are property and possession is with seller's command.

Invalid sales (fasid) are a unique category in Hanafi law. While the contract is flawed, it is not entirely void. This lesson focuses on the critical question: does taking possession of the item in a fasid sale make the buyer the owner? We will explore the conditions, the legal effect, and the Hanafi proof that counters the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the invalid sale (bay' fasid). The ruling is that ownership is established by possession with conditions.
Contrast with batil (void) sale where ownership is impossible.
2
List the conditions for ownership
Three conditions: (1) buyer takes possession, (2) with seller's explicit or implicit command, (3) both countervalues are property (mal).
If any condition is missing, the sale is either batil or no ownership transfers.
3
Examine the proof and refute the opponent
The Hanafi proof: the contract pillar is present, so it is concluded. The prohibition is only an attached description, not affecting the core. The Shafi'i claim that prohibition abrogates legality is refuted by distinguishing the essence from the defect.
The text uses logical reasoning (rkn present) and analogy.
4
Determine the value/property type
If the item is fungible (mithli), ownership is by equivalent (misl); if non-fungible (qimi), by value (qimah) on the day of possession.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference on the date of valuation.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Example: A buys wine (fasid sale) with seller's permission. Both wine and price are property? Wine is property in the sense of having a value, but not a legitimate commodity. According to Hanafi, wine is mal (property) so ownership transfers. A owns the wine by its value and is liable if it perishes.
This leads to the guarantee ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: وفيه بيان أحكام البيع الفاسد وتصرفات البائع والمشتري في الثمن والمبيع، وفيما يكره من البيوع وما لا يكره. رمز الحقائق ٤٢/٢] وحكم الشيء أثره فيتبع المؤثر، حكم البيع الباطل أنه إن هلك المبيع في يد المشتري فعند البعض يهلك أمانة فلا ضمان على المشتري، وعند البعض مضمونا بالقيمة أو المثل، واختاره السرخسي وغيره وهو قول الأئمة الثلاثة، وفي القنية: أنه هو الصحيح؛ لكونه قبضه لنفسه فشابه الغصب؛ ولأنه لا يكون أدنى حالا من المقبوض على سوم الشراء. (عيني، فتح) البيع الفاسد كالبيع بالخمر والخنزير واحترز به عن بيع الباطل فإنه لا يفيد الملك بالقبض. (ط، عيني) لأنه بأمر البائع أي بإذنه صريحا أو دلالة؛ لأن القبض بغير الإذن لا يفيد الملك أصلا. (ط، عيني) عوضيه: أي عوضي البيع وهما الثمن والمبيع. (عيني) مال فلو كان أحدهما غير مال بطل البيع لا يفيد الملك بالقبض. (ط، عيني) ملك المبيع بقيمته، ولكل منهما فسخه، إلا أن يبيع المشتري، أو يهب ملك المبيع بقيمته : [يوم القبض في ذوات القيم، وبمثله في ذوات الأمثال. (عيني مس] أي إذا قبض المشتري المبيع في البيع الفاسد بأمر البائع، وكل من العوضين مال، ملك المبيع بقيمته فقيد بقوله: "قبض"؛ لأن الملك لا يثبت في البيع الفاسد بدون القبض، وقيد بـ"البيع الفاسد؛ للاحتراز عن الباطل، فإنه لا يفيد الملك ولو اتصل به القبض والبيع الفاسد كالبيع بالخمر والخنزير أو بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وقيد بقوله: "بأمر البائع"؛ لأنه لو قبض بدون أمره لا يفيد الملك سواء كان الأمر صريحاً بأن كان قبل الافتراق، أو بعده، وكان المبيع مما يملك بالقبض، أو كان الأمر دلالة، وهو أن يقبضه عقيب العقد بحضرة البائع، فإن لم يكن بحضرته لم يملكه، بخلاف الصريح فإنه يفيد الملك مطلقاً. التلف؛ وقيد بقوله: "وكل من عوضيه مال؛ لأنه إن لم يكن أحد العوضين مالاً كالميتة والدم والحر، لا يثبت الملك، ويكون البيع باطلاً، والمبيع أمانة في يده، حتى لو هلك لا ضمان على القابض وعند الثلاثة: يضمن، وهو رواية عن أصحابنا، والمعتبر في القيمة يوم القبض؛ لأن المبيع بالقبض دخل في ضمانه وعند محمد: يعتبر قيمة يوم لأنه به يتقرر عليه، وهذا إذا كان المبيع قيمياً، وإن كان مثلياً ملكه بمثله؛ إذ هو أعدل؛ لكونه مثلاً له صورة ومعنى، وهو الأصح، وقال مشايخ عراق: لا يملك العين، وإنما يملك فيها التصرف خاصة بحكم تسليط البائع عليه. وقال الشافعي: لا يملك العين ولا التصرف وإن قبضه؛ لأنه محظور، فلا تنال به نعمة الملك، ولأن النهي ينسخ المشروعية لتناف بينهما، ولهذا لا يفيده قبل القبض، وصار كالبيع بالميتة ولنا أن ركن البيع صدر من مضافاً إلى محله، فوجب القول بانعقاده، فنفس البيع مشروع و به تنال نعمة الملك، إنما المحظور ما اتصل به وصفاً، وعدم ثبوت الملك قبل القبض مدار تقرير الفساد والميتة ليست بمال، فانعدم الركن.(عيني، فتح، عناية) ولكل منهما فسخه أي يجب على كل واحد منهما فسخه قبل القبض رفعاً للفساد، فاللام بمعنى "على" كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا ﴾ (الإسراء:٧) ووارثه يخلفه، ولا يشترط القضاء، فيفسخ بمحضر من الآخر أي بعلمه، رضي أم لا؛ لأن في الفسخ إلزام الفسخ على صاحبه فلا يلزم بدون علمه، وأما بعد القبض، فإن كان الفساد في صلب العقد بأن باع عبداً بخمر أو خنزير، فكذلك لكل منهما سبيل من فسخ العقد؛ لقوة الفساد، وإن كان بشرط زائد بأن باع إلى أجل مجهول، فحق الفسخ لمن له منفعة الشرط دون الآخر عند محمد، وعندهما لكل منهما فسخه بعلم صاحبه في الكل. (عيني، فتح) إلا أن يبيع المشتري: [الشيء الذي اشتراه بالبيع الفاسد قبل الفسخ. (ط، عيني)] شروع في التصرفات التي تمنع الفسخ، وقدم التصرفات القولية فمنها : أن يبيع المشتري المبيع الذي اشتراه بالبيع الفاسد قبل الفسخ، فيمتنع الفسخ بعد بيعه؛ لتعلق حق العبد بالثاني، ونقض الأول لحق الشرع، وحق العبد مقدم لحاجته، لكنه مقيد بقيود: الأول: أن لا يكون فيه خيار شرط. الثاني: أن يكون البيع الثاني صحيحاً، فلو كان فاسداً لم يمتنع الفسخ.
Section: Containing an explanation of the rulings of the invalid sale and the transactions of the seller and buyer regarding the price and the sold item, and regarding what is disliked among sales and what is not disliked. Rasm al-Haqa'iq 42/2] And the ruling of a thing is its effect, so it follows the effect. The ruling of a void sale is that if the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand, according to some it perishes as an trust, so there is no liability on the buyer; and according to some it is guaranteed by value or similar, and al-Sarakhsi and others chose this, and it is the saying of the three Imams. In al-Qunyah: that it is the correct one because he took possession for himself, so it resembles usurpation; and because it is not less than what is taken on the basis of bargaining. (Ayni, Fath) The invalid sale is like sale for wine or swine, and it is distinguished from the void sale because it does not confer ownership by possession. (Tahtawi, Ayni) Because it is by the seller's command, meaning with his explicit or implicit permission; because possession without permission does not confer ownership at all. (Tahtawi, Ayni) Its two countervalues: meaning the two countervalues of the sale, which are the price and the sold item. (Ayni) Property: so if one of them is not property, the sale is void and does not confer ownership by possession. (Tahtawi, Ayni) He owns the sold item by its value, and each of them has the right to rescind it, except if the buyer sells it, or gives it as a gift. He owns the sold item by its value: [on the day of possession for items of value, and by its equivalent for fungible items. (Ayni, Mus] Meaning: if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in an invalid sale by the seller's command, and both of the countervalues are property, he owns the sold item by its value. So it is restricted by his saying "possession" because ownership is not established in an invalid sale without possession. And it is restricted by "the invalid sale" to distinguish from the void sale, for it does not confer ownership even if possession is attached to it. The invalid sale is like sale for wine or swine or with a condition that the contract does not require. And it is restricted by his saying "by the seller's command" because if he takes possession without his command, it does not confer ownership, whether the command is explicit, such as before separation or after, and the sold item is of the type that can be owned by possession, or the command is implicit, which is that he takes possession immediately after the contract in the presence of the seller. If not in his presence, he does not own it, unlike the explicit command which confers ownership absolutely. Perishing; And it is restricted by his saying "and both of its countervalues are property" because if one of the countervalues is not property, like a carcass, blood, or a free person, ownership is not established, and the sale is void, and the sold item is a trust in his hand, so if it perishes, there is no liability on the possessor. According to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), he guarantees it, and this is a narration from our companions. The considered value is on the day of possession because the sold item entered his guarantee by possession. According to Muhammad, the value on the day it becomes established on him is considered. This is if the sold item is a item of value. If it is a fungible item, he owns it by its equivalent because it is more just, being equal in form and meaning, and this is the most correct. The scholars of Iraq said: he does not own the thing itself, but only has the right to dispose of it specifically by virtue of the seller's authorization. Al-Shafi'i said: he does not own the thing nor the right to dispose, even if he takes possession, because it is prohibited, so the blessing of ownership is not attained through it, and because prohibition abrogates legality due to contradiction between them, and for this reason it does not benefit before possession, and it becomes like sale for a carcass. Our proof: that the pillar of the sale emanated from someone capable and was connected to its proper place, so it is necessary to say it is concluded. Therefore, the sale itself is legitimate and through it the blessing of ownership is attained. What is prohibited is only a description attached to it, and the non-establishment of ownership before possession is a factor of invalidity. The carcass is not property, so the pillar is absent. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) And each of them has the right to rescind it: meaning it is obligatory for each of them to rescind it before possession to remove the invalidity, so the laam means 'upon' as in the verse: 'And if you do evil, it is against yourselves' (17:7). And his heir succeeds him, and a court order is not required, so he rescinds in the presence of the other, meaning with his knowledge, whether he agrees or not, because rescinding necessitates imposing rescission on the other, so it is not binding without his knowledge. After possession, if the invalidity is in the essence of the contract, such as selling a slave for wine or swine, then similarly each has a way to rescind the contract due to the strength of the invalidity. If it is due to an added condition, such as sale for an unknown term, then the right of rescission belongs to the one who benefits from the condition, according to Muhammad. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), each has the right to rescind with the knowledge of the other in all cases. (Ayni, Fath) Except if the buyer sells: [the thing he bought in the invalid sale before rescission. (Tahtawi, Ayni)] This is a beginning of the transactions that prevent rescission, and he gives precedence to verbal transactions. Among them: that the buyer sells the sold item which he bought in the invalid sale before rescission, then rescission is prevented after his sale because the right of a servant (the second buyer) becomes attached, and revoking the first would be overriding the right of the servant, and the right of the servant takes precedence due to his need. But it is restricted by conditions: first, that there is no option of stipulation in it. Second, that the second sale is valid; if it were invalid, rescission would not be prevented.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This lesson presents the core rulings of an invalid sale (bay' fasid) in the Hanafi school. It begins by distinguishing between fasid and batil (void). In a fasid sale, the contract is valid in its essence but defective due to some imperfection (e.g., selling wine or with a prohibited condition). In a batil (void) sale, the pillar (rukn) is missing. The key ruling: if the buyer takes possession of the item in a fasid sale with the seller's permission, and both countervalues (price and item) are property, then the buyer owns the item by its value (or its equivalent for fungibles). This ownership is established by possession, not by the contract alone. The value is determined as of the day of possession. The text then discusses the liability: if the item perishes, the buyer is liable for its value according to the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). There is a dispute: some early Hanafis said it is an amanah (trust), but the relied-upon view (al-Sarakhsi, etc.) is that it is guaranteed. The text also refutes the Shafi'i position that no ownership is established at all, arguing that the contract is concluded and the prohibition is only attached as a description, so ownership can be attained. Next, the right of rescission (faskh): both parties are obliged to rescind the contract before possession to remove the invalidity. After possession, if the invalidity is in the essence (e.g., selling a slave for wine), both still have the right to rescind. If the invalidity is due to a condition (e.g., unknown term), then according to Muhammad only the party benefiting from the condition has the right to rescind; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two), each can rescind with the other's knowledge. Finally, an exception: if the buyer sells the item before rescission, the right to rescind is prevented because the rights of a third-party (the second buyer) have attached, and those rights take precedence over the religious obligation to rescind. However, this prevention is conditional: the second sale must be valid and without a stipulation option.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is valid in its essence (rukn present) but invalid due to a defect in description (e.g., selling wine or with an invalid condition).
مثلي (mithli): A fungible item that has equivalents in the market (e.g., wheat, oil).
قيمي (qimi): A non-fungible item that does not have exact equivalents (e.g., a unique animal, a building).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale where the buyer takes possession with the seller's command, which of the following is true?
A) The buyer does not own the item and cannot sell it.
B) The buyer owns the item by its value and can sell it to a third party.
C) The buyer owns the item but must return it immediately without compensation.
D) The sale becomes void automatically and the item is a trust.

Key Takeaway
A fasid sale transfers ownership to the buyer by possession if both countervalues are property and possession is with the seller's command; the buyer guarantees the item and must ultimately rescind the contract.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
Rescission of a Fasid Sale and Its Prevention
Rulings on rescission of fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when rescission is obligatory, who has the right to rescind, and what actions prevent rescission in a fasid sale.

Core Idea
Rescission is obligatory before possession; after possession, the right to rescind depends on the type of invalidity and may be blocked if the buyer sells the item.

Once a fasid sale is concluded and possession possibly taken, the parties must address the invalidity. The primary remedy is rescission (faskh). This lesson clarifies when rescission is obligatory, how it differs before and after possession, and what transactions by the buyer can permanently block the right to rescind.

Guided Path
1
Understand the obligation of rescission before possession
Before possession, rescission is wajib (obligatory) on both parties to remove the invalidity. The laam in 'li-kullin minhuma faskhuhu' is for obligation (like in Quran 17:7).
No court order required; rescission can be done in the presence of the other party.
2
Distinguish rescission after possession by type of invalidity
If the invalidity is in the essence (e.g., selling a slave for wine), both parties still have the right to rescind. If the invalidity is due to an added condition (e.g., unknown term), the right belongs to the party benefiting from the condition according to Muhammad; the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say both have the right with the other's knowledge.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Identify actions that prevent rescission
If the buyer sells the sold item to a third party before rescission, the right to rescind is lost because the second buyer's rights take precedence. This prevention has conditions: the second sale must be valid and without a stipulation option.
The text mentions this as an exception (illa an yabi'a al-mushtari).
4
Apply the rule to scenarios
Example: A buys a car for an unknown delivery date (fasid). Before taking possession, A must rescind. If A takes possession, then according to Muhammad only the seller (who does not benefit from the condition?) Actually the condition benefits the buyer (unknown term is advantageous to the seller? Actually it benefits the buyer by giving him time, but the text says 'man lahu manfa'at al-shart' — the one who benefits from the condition. So if the condition benefits the seller, only seller can rescind? The text is ambiguous; typically unknown term benefits the buyer (flexibility). We'll assume the condition benefits the buyer. So according to Muhammad, only the buyer can rescind; according to the two, both can rescind.
If A then sells the car to B (valid sale), the right to rescind is lost.

(Ayni, Fath) The invalid sale is like sale for wine or swine, and it is distinguished from the void sale because it does not confer ownership by possession. (Tahtawi, Ayni) Because it is by the seller's command, meaning with his explicit or implicit permission; because possession without permission does not confer ownership at all. (Tahtawi, Ayni) Its two countervalues: meaning the two countervalues of the sale, which are the price and the sold item. (Ayni) Property: so if one of them is not property, the sale is void and does not confer ownership by possession. (Tahtawi, Ayni) He owns the sold item by its value, and each of them has the right to rescind it, except if the buyer sells it, or gives it as a gift. He owns the sold item by its value: [on the day of possession for items of value, and by its equivalent for fungible items. (Ayni, Mus] Meaning: if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in an invalid sale by the seller's command, and both of the countervalues are property, he owns the sold item by its value. So it is restricted by his saying "possession" because ownership is not established in an invalid sale without possession. And it is restricted by "the invalid sale" to distinguish from the void sale, for it does not confer ownership even if possession is attached to it. The invalid sale is like sale for wine or swine or with a condition that the contract does not require. And it is restricted by his saying "by the seller's command" because if he takes possession without his command, it does not confer ownership, whether the command is explicit, such as before separation or after, and the sold item is of the type that can be owned by possession, or the command is implicit, which is that he takes possession immediately after the contract in the presence of the seller. If not in his presence, he does not own it, unlike the explicit command which confers ownership absolutely. Perishing; And it is restricted by his saying "and both of its countervalues are property" because if one of the countervalues is not property, like a carcass, blood, or a free person, ownership is not established, and the sale is void, and the sold item is a trust in his hand, so if it perishes, there is no liability on the possessor. According to the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), he guarantees it, and this is a narration from our companions. The considered value is on the day of possession because the sold item entered his guarantee by possession. According to Muhammad, the value on the day it becomes established on him is considered. This is if the sold item is a item of value. If it is a fungible item, he owns it by its equivalent because it is more just, being equal in form and meaning, and this is the most correct. The scholars of Iraq said: he does not own the thing itself, but only has the right to dispose of it specifically by virtue of the seller's authorization. Al-Shafi'i said: he does not own the thing nor the right to dispose, even if he takes possession, because it is prohibited, so the blessing of ownership is not attained through it, and because prohibition abrogates legality due to contradiction between them, and for this reason it does not benefit before possession, and it becomes like sale for a carcass. Our proof: that the pillar of the sale emanated from someone capable and was connected to its proper place, so it is necessary to say it is concluded. Therefore, the sale itself is legitimate and through it the blessing of ownership is attained. What is prohibited is only a description attached to it, and the non-establishment of ownership before possession is a factor of invalidity. The carcass is not property, so the pillar is absent. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) And each of them has the right to rescind it: meaning it is obligatory for each of them to rescind it before possession to remove the invalidity, so the laam means 'upon' as in the verse: 'And if you do evil, it is against yourselves' (17:7). And his heir succeeds him, and a court order is not required, so he rescinds in the presence of the other, meaning with his knowledge, whether he agrees or not, because rescinding necessitates imposing rescission on the other, so it is not binding without his knowledge. After possession, if the invalidity is in the essence of the contract, such as selling a slave for wine or swine, then similarly each has a way to rescind the contract due to the strength of the invalidity. If it is due to an added condition, such as sale for an unknown term, then the right of rescission belongs to the one who benefits from the condition, according to Muhammad. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), each has the right to rescind with the knowledge of the other in all cases. (Ayni, Fath) Except if the buyer sells: [the thing he bought in the invalid sale before rescission. (Tahtawi, Ayni)] This is a beginning of the transactions that prevent rescission, and he gives precedence to verbal transactions. Among them: that the buyer sells the sold item which he bought in the invalid sale before rescission, then rescission is prevented after his sale because the right of a servant (the second buyer) becomes attached, and revoking the first would be overriding the right of the servant, and the right of the servant takes precedence due to his need. But it is restricted by conditions: first, that there is no option of stipulation in it. Second, that the second sale is valid; if it were invalid, rescission would not be prevented.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This lesson presents the core rulings of an invalid sale (bay' fasid) in the Hanafi school. It begins by distinguishing between fasid and batil (void). In a fasid sale, the contract is valid in its essence but defective due to some imperfection (e.g., selling wine or with a prohibited condition). In a batil (void) sale, the pillar (rukn) is missing. The key ruling: if the buyer takes possession of the item in a fasid sale with the seller's permission, and both countervalues (price and item) are property, then the buyer owns the item by its value (or its equivalent for fungibles). This ownership is established by possession, not by the contract alone. The value is determined as of the day of possession. The text then discusses the liability: if the item perishes, the buyer is liable for its value according to the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad). There is a dispute: some early Hanafis said it is an amanah (trust), but the relied-upon view (al-Sarakhsi, etc.) is that it is guaranteed. The text also refutes the Shafi'i position that no ownership is established at all, arguing that the contract is concluded and the prohibition is only attached as a description, so ownership can be attained. Next, the right of rescission (faskh): both parties are obliged to rescind the contract before possession to remove the invalidity. After possession, if the invalidity is in the essence (e.g., selling a slave for wine), both still have the right to rescind. If the invalidity is due to a condition (e.g., unknown term), then according to Muhammad only the party benefiting from the condition has the right to rescind; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two), each can rescind with the other's knowledge. Finally, an exception: if the buyer sells the item before rescission, the right to rescind is prevented because the rights of a third-party (the second buyer) have attached, and those rights take precedence over the religious obligation to rescind. However, this prevention is conditional: the second sale must be valid and without a stipulation option.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, returning to the pre-contract state.
شرط زائد (shart za'id): An added condition that is not required by the contract, e.g., an unknown term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid sale where the invalidity is due to an added condition (e.g., unknown delivery date), and the buyer has taken possession and then sells the item to a third party, what happens to the original seller's right to rescind?
A) The seller can still rescind because the sale is invalid.
B) The seller's right to rescind is lost because the buyer's sale blocks it.
C) The seller can rescind but must compensate the third party.
D) The right to rescind passes to the third party.

Key Takeaway
Before possession, rescission of a fasid sale is obligatory on both parties; after possession, who can rescind depends on the type of invalidity, and if the buyer sells the item, rescission is permanently blocked.

Ownership in a Fasid Sale: Conditions and Proof
Establishes ownership rule in fasid sale with conditions and disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a buyer acquires ownership in a fasid sale and explain the Hanafi proof for this ruling.

Core Idea
Ownership in a voidable sale requires possession with the seller's order; the prohibition only affects the description, not the essence.

In a fasid sale, the contract is defective in description but valid in pillar. This raises the question: can the buyer still own the item? The Hanafi school holds that ownership is possible, but only upon taking possession with the seller's order. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the intra-Hanafi details on valuation, and the proof against the Shafi'i view that such a sale cannot yield ownership at all.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
Find the statement: 'ملك المبيع بقيمته' and note the restrictions: possession (قبض), seller's order (بأمر البائع), and both countervalues being property (وكل من العوضين مال).
These conditions distinguish fasid from batil.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi disputes
Note the disagreements: Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad on the day of valuation; Mashayikh Iraq vs majority on whether the buyer owns the 'ayn or only the right to dispose.
The text sides with Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, declaring it 'al-ashah'.
3
Locate the Shafi'i counterargument
Al-Shafi'i argues that the prohibition abrogates legislativity, so no ownership can arise. Identify his proof: because it is prohibited, it cannot be a means to the blessing of ownership, similar to selling carrion.
This is a strong challenge based on the principle that 'the prohibition of a thing indicates its corruption.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: the pillar (rukn) of the sale is present, so the contract is concluded. The prohibition only attaches to a description, not the essence. Therefore, ownership can still be attained—hence the fasid sale is not like a batil sale where the pillar is missing.
This is a classic application of istihsan and the distinction between rukn and wasf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical implication
The mufta bihi is that the buyer owns the 'ayn by its value on the day of possession. This means if the item is destroyed after possession, the buyer is liable for that value; if the buyer resells, the second sale is valid.
The Shafi'i view would treat the initial sale as void, so any subsequent sale would be invalid too.

Source Text

Rulings of the voidable sale: The sold item is owned by its value, and each of them has the right to rescind it, unless the buyer sells or gives as gift. Ownership of the sold item by its value: on the day of possession for value-based items, and by its like for fungible items. That is, if the buyer takes possession of the sold item in a voidable sale with the seller's order, and both of the two compensations are property, he owns the sold item by its value. So it is restricted by his saying 'possession' because ownership is not established in a voidable sale without possession, and restricted by 'voidable sale' to distinguish from a void sale, for it does not give ownership even if possession is attached to it. The voidable sale is like selling for wine or swine or with a condition that the contract does not require. It is restricted by his saying 'with the seller's order' because if he took possession without his order, it does not give ownership, whether the order is explicit—that is before separation or after, and the sold item is of those owned by possession—or by indication, which is that he takes possession immediately after the contract in the presence of the seller; if not in his presence, he does not own it, unlike the explicit which gives ownership absolutely. [As for] destruction: And restricted by his saying 'and both of its compensations are property' because if one of the compensations is not property like a carcass, blood, or a free person, ownership is not established, and the sale is void, and the sold item is a trust in his hand, so if it perishes, there is no guarantee upon the taker. According to Muhammad, the value of the day is considered because it is established upon him. This is if the sold item is a qimi (value-based); if it is a mithli (fungible), he owns it by its like because it is more just, as it is its example in form and meaning. This is the most correct. The scholars of Iraq said: He does not own the entity, but rather he only has the right of disposal in it by virtue of the seller's authorization. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not own the entity nor disposal even if he took possession, because it is prohibited, so no blessing of ownership is attained thereby, and because prohibition abrogates legislativity due to contradiction between them, hence it does not give ownership before possession, and it becomes like selling for a carcass. Our proof: The pillar of the sale issued from one attributed to its place, so it is necessary to say it is concluded. The sale itself is legislated and by it the blessing of ownership is attained; only the description attached to it is prohibited. The non-establishment of ownership before possession is the locus for determining the defect, and a carcass is not property, so the pillar is absent.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the ruling of ownership (milk) in a fasid (voidable) sale, typical of the expansion (sharh) genre. The commentator is explaining a matn that states: 'The sold item is owned by its value, and each has the right to rescind, unless the buyer sells or gives as gift...' The focus here is the first part. The ruling: In a fasid sale, the buyer acquires ownership of the sold item by its value upon taking possession (qabd) with the seller's order. Several conditions are highlighted: (1) Possession: Without possession, ownership does not vest in a fasid sale, distinguishing it from a void (batil) sale where even possession does not yield ownership. (2) The sale must be fasid, not batil—examples of batil include selling for wine or swine. (3) The seller must order the possession; if the buyer takes without permission, ownership does not transfer. The order can be explicit (before or after separation) or implicit (taking immediately after the contract in the seller's presence). (4) Both countervalues must be property (mal); if one is non-property (e.g., carrion, blood, a free person), the sale is batil and the item remains a trust (amanah) in the buyer's hands—if it perishes, no liability unless, according to the three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) and a narration from our scholars, he is liable. The valuation day: The generally accepted view (as per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is the day of possession, because the item enters the buyer's guarantee then. Muhammad considers the day the price is determined. For value-based goods (qimiyyat), the buyer pays its value; for fungibles (mithliyyat), he pays its like (mithl). The scholars of Iraq held that the buyer does not own the entity but only has the right to dispose. Al-Shafi'i argued that ownership is not attained at all because the transaction is prohibited. The Hanafi response (our proof) is that the sale itself is valid (mun'aqid) because its pillar exists; the prohibition only attaches to a description, not to the essence. Therefore, the buyer can attain the blessing of ownership. This is a classic Hanafi argument using istihsan (juristic preference) and the distinction between the contract's validity and its prohibition.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fasid): A sale that is deficient in description (wasf) but not in pillar (rukn); ownership depends on possession.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession or control of the sold item with the seller's order.
قيمي (qimiyy): A good that has no exact standard (mithl) and is valued by its unique attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Upon taking possession with the seller's order
C) Only after paying the full price
D) Never, because the sale is void

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the buyer owns the item by its value upon taking possession with the seller's order, because the prohibition only affects a description, not the contract's pillar.

Right to Rescind in a Fasid Sale
Explains the right to rescind a fasid sale before and after possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ruling on rescission (faskh) in a fasid sale, distinguish between core defects and extra conditions, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the right to rescind after possession.

Core Idea
Rescission of a fasid sale is wajib before possession; after possession, who may rescind depends on the defect type and the Hanafi imam followed.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the legal ruling on faskh (rescission) in a fasid (defective) sale. It clarifies that rescission is not merely a right but an obligation before taking possession, to remove the defect. After possession, the ruling becomes nuanced: if the defect is in the core of the contract (e.g., selling prohibited items), both parties can rescind; if the defect is due to an extra condition (e.g., unknown term), there is a difference of opinion among the Hanafi masters. We will explore this intra-madhhab ikhtilaf and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial ruling
The passage begins with the matn 'wa likulli minhuma faskhuhu', which is explained as a wujub (obligation) to rescind before possession. Understand why the lam in 'likull' is interpreted as 'ala (upon).
Rescission before possession is wajib to eliminate the defect.
2
Examine the conditions for rescission
The commentator specifies that rescission does not require a judge; it is valid 'bi mahdari al-akhar' (with the knowledge of the other party). Also, the heir steps in if a party dies.
Knowledge of the other party is necessary because rescission imposes on them.
3
Differentiate after possession: core defect vs extra condition
After possession, if the defect is in the core (e.g., selling a slave for wine), both parties have the right to rescind due to the strength of the defect. If the defect is due to an extra condition (e.g., unknown term), the ruling is disputed.
Core defects are more severe, hence strong rescission right.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to Muhammad, only the party benefiting from the extra condition can rescind. According to 'the two' (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa, or Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in another transmission), both parties can rescind with knowledge of the other.
The mufta bihi (operative view) is not explicitly stated here, but the stronger defect allows both to rescind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكل منهما فسخه أي يجب على كل واحد منهما فسخه قبل القبض رفعاً للفساد، فاللام بمعنى "على" كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا ﴾ (الإسراء:٧) ووارثه يخلفه، ولا يشترط القضاء، فيفسخ بمحضر من الآخر أي بعلمه، رضي أم لا؛ لأن في الفسخ إلزام الفسخ على صاحبه فلا يلزم بدون علمه، وأما بعد القبض، فإن كان الفساد في صلب العقد بأن باع . عبداً بخمر أو خنزير، فكذلك لكل منهما سبيل من فسخ العقد؛ لقوة الفساد، وإن كان بشرط زائد بأن باع إلى أجل مجهول، فحق الفسخ لمن له منفعة الشرط دون الآخر عند محمد، وعندهما لكل منهما فسخه بعلم صاحبه في الكل. (عيني، فتح)
And each of them has the right to rescind it, meaning it is obligatory upon each one of them to rescind it before possession to remove the defect. So the 'lam' has the meaning of 'upon', as in His saying: 'And if you do evil, then it is upon you' (al-Isra:7). And his heir succeeds him, and it is not a condition that a judge be involved; he rescinds in the presence (i.e., with the knowledge) of the other, whether he is pleased or not, because in rescission there is imposing rescission upon the other, so it is not binding without his knowledge. After possession, if the defect is in the core of the contract—such as selling a slave for wine or swine—then each one has a way to rescind the contract due to the strength of the defect. If it is due to an extra condition—such as selling with an unknown term—then the right of rescission belongs to the one who benefits from the condition and not the other according to Muhammad, but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the later view?) each of them may rescind with the knowledge of the other in all cases.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on faskh (rescission) in a fasid sale, expanding on the matn's phrase 'wa likulli minhuma faskhuhu'. The commentator clarifies that the 'lam' in 'lakull' means 'upon' (wujub), so rescission is obligatory before possession. It does not require a judge; the rescinding party only needs to inform the other. If one party dies, the heir steps in. After possession, if the defect is in the core of the contract (e.g., selling a slave for wine), both parties can rescind due to the gross defect. If the defect is due to an extra condition (e.g., unknown term), then according to Muhammad, only the party benefiting from the condition can rescind; the other cannot. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the other view (or 'them' referring to the two), both can rescind with knowledge. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the contract, here obligatory before possession to remove defect.
فساد في صلب العقد (fasad fi suli al-'aqd): A defect inherent in the contract's essential elements, e.g., selling prohibited items.
شرط زائد (shart za'id): An extra condition not required by the contract, e.g., unknown term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only the buyer
B) Only the party benefiting from the condition
C) Both parties equally
D) The seller only

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, rescission is obligatory before possession; after possession, the right to rescind depends on the defect type and the imam followed—Muhammad restricts it to the beneficiary of an extra condition, while the two permit both parties.

Verbal Acts Preventing Rescission of a Fasid Sale
Lists verbal dispositions that cut off rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's verbal acts that cut off the seller's right to reclaim, list the conditions for sale to be effective, and identify the exceptions (ijara and nikah).

Core Idea
A buyer's valid sale, gift, or freeing of a fasid item cuts off the seller's right to reclaim.

When a sale is fasid (defective), the seller normally has the right to rescind and reclaim the item. However, if the buyer performs certain acts of disposition (tasarrufat) involving the item, that right is cut off. This lesson focuses on the verbal acts—selling, gifting, freeing—and the conditions that make them effective, as well as the exceptions where the right remains.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its rationale
The matn states: 'Unless the buyer sells, or gives as gift, or frees.' The commentary explains that these acts cut off rescission because they create rights for third parties (e.g., the second buyer) that take precedence over the original seller's right.
The rule is based on prioritizing the right of the new owner (slave/third party) over the original seller's right.
2
List the conditions for sale to cut off rescission
The sale must be: (1) without a condition of option (khiyar), (2) a valid sale (not fasid), and (3) to someone other than the original seller. If any condition is missing, rescission is not prevented.
Selling back to the original seller is considered a cancellation of the first fasid sale, not a new right.
3
Understand the other acts – gift and freeing
Gifting (hiba) with delivery, charity, pawning, freeing a slave, tadbir, kitabah, and making umm walad all cut off rescission because they transfer ownership or create rights that are irrevocable.
Freeing is considered a complete severance of ownership, thus cutting off the seller's right.
4
Recognize the exceptions – ijara and nikah
Ijara (lease) and nikah (marriage) do not prevent rescission. Ijara is a weak contract that can be rescinded for excuse (the fasd of the purchase is an excuse). Nikah only concerns usufruct, not the corpus, so rescission of the sale on the corpus remains possible.
The commentary compares nikah to ijara in that both pertain to usufruct.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Given a scenario, determine whether a buyer's disposition cuts off rescission by checking the conditions and exceptions.
Example: if the buyer sells with a khiyar, rescission is still possible. If the buyer leases, rescission is still possible.
