إلا أن يبيع المشتري: [الشيء الذي اشتراه بالبيع الفاسد قبل الفسخ . (ط، عيني)] شروع في التصرفات التي تمنع الفسخ، وقدم التصرفات القولية فمنها : أن يبيع المشتري المبيع الذي اشتراه بالبيع الفاسد قبل الفسخ، فيمتنع الفسخ بعد بيعه؛ لتعلق حق العبد بالثاني، ونقض الأول لحق الشرع، وحق العبد مقدم لحاجته، لكنه مقيد بقيد: الأول: أن لا يكون فيه خيار شرط. الثاني: أن يكون البيع الثاني صحيحاً، فلو كان فاسداً لم يمتنع الفسخ. الثالث: أن يكون من غير بائعه، فلو باعه منه كان نقضاً للأول، ومنها: أن يهب المشتري المبيع ويسلمه بأن كان عبداً مثلاً، فوهبه لرجل، وكذلك لو تصدق به أو رهنه؛ لتعلق حق الغير بالمبيع، ومنها: أن يحرر، ومثل التحرير توابعه من التدبير، والكتابة والاستيلاد. والحاصل: : أن تصرفات المشتري كلها نافذة فيه، ينقطع بها حق البائع في الاسترداد، سواء كان تصرفاً يقبل الفسخ أولا يقبله إلا الإجارة والنكاح؛ لأن الإجارة عقد ضعيف تفسخ بالعذر، وفساد الشراء عذر، والنكاح لا يمنع فسخ البيع؛ لأن له شبهاً بالإجارة بوروده على المنفعة، والبيع يرد على ملك الرقبة، والفسخ كذلك، فتعلق حق الزوج بالمنفعة، لا يمنع الفسخ على الرقبة، فيفسخ، ويرد على البائع، ويكون النكاح على حال، وفي من التصرفات ينقطع حق الاسترداد. (عيني، فتح، عناية) أو يبني بأن كان عقارا فبنى فيه، فهذه التصرفات تمنع الفسخ (عيني)) شروع فيما يمنع حق الاستراد من الأفعال الحسية، أي لو بنى المشتري في دار اشتراها شراء فاسداً أو اتخذها مسجداً، عليه قيمتها وينقطع حق الاسترداد عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا ينقطع حق الاسترداد، بل يقلع البناء ويرد الدار على صاحبها؛ لأن حق أضعف من حق البائع؛ لأنه يحتاج فيه إلى القضاء، ويبطل بالتأخير، ولا يورث، بخلاف حق البائع، حق ضعفه لا يبطل بالبناء، فحق البائع أولى وله أن البناء حصل بتسلط البائع، وهو مما يقصد به الدوام، فينقطع حقه في الاسترداد كالبيع بخلاف حق الشفيع؛ لأنه لم يوجد منه التسليط، ولهذا لا يسقط بيع المشتري وهبته فكذا ببنائه، وعلى هذا الخلاف الغرس، وعلى أصل الأئمة الثلاثة لا ينقطع حق الاسترداد، بل يسترد البائع بهذه التصرفات. (عيني، فتح) وله أن يمنع المبيع إلخ: بعد الفسخ اعتبارا للفاسد بالجائز إذا تفاسخا بعد قبض العوضين، وعلى هذا الإجارة والرهن الفاسدان، وإن مات البائع أو المؤجر أو الراهن فالمشتري والمستأجر والمرتهن أحق بما في يده من العين ويقدم على تجهيزه وغرمائه، إلا أن الرهن مضمون بقدر الدين فما فضل فللغرماء. وقوله : "حتى" يأخذ الثمن منه"، أي من البائع؛ لأن المبيع مقابل به، فيصير محبوساً به كالرهن، فإن كان قائما أخذ عينه؛ لتعين الدراهم في البيع الفاسد وهو الأصح؛ لأنه بمنزلة ،الغصب، وإن كان مستهلكا أخذ مثله رمز الحقائق: ٤٤/٢] (فتح) وطاب للبائع إلخ: أي لو اشترى شيئاً يتعين بالتعيين بما لم يتعين كالدراهم والدنانير، وربح كل واحد منهما، حل للبائع ما ربح في الثمن، و لم يحل للمشتري ما ربح في المبيع؛ لأن العقد يتعلق بما يتعين فيتمكن الخبث فيه ولا يتعلق العقد الثاني بما لا يتعين، بل يجب مثله في الذمة، فلا يتمكن الخبث فيه فلا يجب التصدق به، وهذا في الخبث =
Unless the buyer sells [the thing he bought in the fasid sale before rescission]. (Beginning of the acts that prevent rescission, and he begins with verbal acts.) Among them: that the buyer sells the sold item which he bought in the fasid sale before rescission. Then rescission is prevented after his sale because a right of a slave (third party) attaches to the second sale, and annulling the first (sale) would annul a divine right, and the right of a slave is given precedence due to his need. But it is restricted by conditions: first, that there be no option of condition in the second sale; second, that the second sale be valid; if it is fasid, rescission is not prevented; third, that it be to someone other than his seller. If he sells it to him, it is annulment of the first. Among them: that the buyer gives the sold item as a gift and hands it over—e.g., if it was a slave, he gives him to a man; and similarly if he gives charity with it or pawns it, because a third party's right attaches to the sold item. Among them: that he frees (the slave), and like freeing are its consequences such as tadbir, kitabah, and istilad (um al-walad). The summary: All of the buyer's dispositions are effective in it, and by them the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, whether the disposition is one that rescission is possible or not, except for ijara (lease) and nikah (marriage) because ijara is a weak contract that is rescinded for an excuse, and the defect of the purchase is an excuse; nikah does not prevent rescission of the sale because it has resemblance to ijara in that it concerns usufruct, while the sale concerns ownership of the corpus, and rescission is likewise. Thus the husband's right attaches to the usufruct and does not prevent rescission of the corpus, so he rescinds and returns to the seller while the marriage remains. And among the dispositions, the right to reclaim is cut off. Or he builds—if it is real estate and he builds on it. These acts prevent rescission. (Beginning of what prevents the right to reclaim from physical acts.) That is, if the buyer builds in a house he bought in a fasid sale or makes it a mosque, he owes its value and the right to reclaim is cut off according to Abu Hanifa. But according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the right to reclaim is not cut off; rather, he removes the building and returns the house to its owner because his right is weaker than the seller's right, as it requires a court, becomes void by delay, and is not inherited, unlike the seller's right. The weakness of his right does not nullify it by building, so the seller's right is more deserving. And because the building occurred with the seller's authorization, and it is intended for permanence, so his right to reclaim is cut off like in sale, unlike the right of preemption (shuf'ah) because there is no authorization from the seller. For this reason, the buyer's sale and gift do not cut off the preemptor's right, and similarly his building. On this dispute is planting (ghars). According to the three imams, the right to reclaim is not cut off; rather, the seller recovers the item despite these acts. And he has the right to prevent the sold item etc.: after rescission, considering the fasid like the permissible when they annul after receiving the compensations, and similarly for fasid ijara and rahn. If the seller, lessor, or pawner dies, the buyer, lessee, or pledgee has more right to what is in his hand of the property and it takes precedence over his shrouding and creditors, except that the pawn is guaranteed to the extent of the debt, so surplus goes to creditors. His saying 'until' he takes the price from him—i.e., from the seller—because the sold item is in exchange for it, so it becomes detained like a pawn. If it is still in existence, he takes its property because dirhams are determined in a fasid sale, and this is the most correct because it is like usurpation; if it is consumed, he takes its like. And it is permissible for the seller: i.e., if he bought something that becomes determined by specification (e.g., dirhams) and each of them made a profit, what the seller profited on the price is permissible, and what the buyer profited on the sold item is not permissible, because the contract attaches to what is determined and thus defect (khabath) can permeate it, while the second contract does not attach to what is not determined but rather its like is due in the liability, so defect cannot permeate it, so charity is not required. This is concerning defect.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage addresses the exceptions that prevent rescission (faskh) of a fasid sale. The matn says 'unless the buyer sells or gives as gift or frees or builds.' The commentary explains in detail. First, verbal dispositions: if the buyer sells the item to a third party, rescission is prevented, provided: (1) no option of condition in the second sale, (2) the second sale is valid (not fasid), (3) the buyer is not selling back to the original seller. The rationale is that the third party's right takes precedence over the original seller's right to reclaim. Similarly, if the buyer gives the item as a gift, charity, or pawns it. Freeing a slave (and its equivalents: tadbir, kitabah, making umm walad) also cuts off rescission. An exception is made for ijara and nikah: ijara is weak and can be rescinded for excuse (the fasid sale is an excuse); nikah does not prevent rescission of the sale because it pertains to usufruct while sale is on the corpus. Physical acts: building on real estate or turning it into a mosque. Here, Abu Hanifa holds that the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, and the buyer pays the value. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller can still reclaim and the buyer must remove the building. The commentary presents arguments for both sides. Then additional rulings: after rescission, the buyer can detain the item until paid, analogous to a pawn (rahn). If the seller dies, the buyer's right takes precedence over creditors. Finally, the profit ruling: if the transaction involves dirham/dinar (specified in fasid sale), the seller's profit on the price is halal, but the buyer's profit on the item is haram and must be given in charity, because the defect (khabath) attaches to specified items but not to fungibles due in liability. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
تصرف (tasarruf): Buyer's legal disposition (sale, gift, freeing) that affects the item.
حق الاسترداد (haqq al-istirdad): Seller's right to reclaim the sold item in a fasid sale.
شبهة (shubhah): Resemblance; used for nikah resembling ijara regarding usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following buyer actions does NOT cut off the seller's right to reclaim in a fasid sale?
A) Selling the item to a third party with a valid contract
B) Freeing the slave that was bought
C) Marrying the female slave bought
D) Giving the item as charity with delivery

Key Takeaway
Buyer's sale, gift, and freeing cut off rescission if conditions are met; ijara and nikah are exceptions.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Building on a Fasid Purchase
Intra-Hanafi dispute on building and planting as preventive acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether building cuts off rescission, and explain the arguments on both sides.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa says building cuts off rescission; the Sahibayn say it does not – a clash over the strength of the seller's right.

Among the buyer's physical acts, building on real estate purchased in a fasid sale creates a dispute within the Hanafi school. Abu Hanifa holds that the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the seller can still reclaim and the buyer must remove the building. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the two positions
The issue: does building on a fasid purchase cut off the seller's right to reclaim? Abu Hanifa says yes, buyer pays value. Sahibayn say no, seller can reclaim and buyer removes building.
The disagreement is on whether the seller's right is strong enough to override the buyer's building.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's argument
Abu Hanifa argues that the building was done with the seller's consent (since the seller allowed the buyer to take possession) and it is intended for permanence, analogous to a valid sale. Thus the seller's right is cut off.
He compares building to selling: both involve permanence and cut off rescission.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's argument
They argue that the buyer's right to reclaim is weaker than the seller's because it requires court action, is lost by delay, and is not inherited. Therefore building does not defeat the seller's stronger right.
They also note that the buyer's sale and gift do not cut off the shuf'ah (preemption) right, similarly building should not cut off rescission.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa: seller cannot reclaim land; buyer pays value of land (without building). Under Sahibayn: seller can force buyer to remove building and reclaim land.
The difference affects property rights significantly.
5
Consider the dispute on planting (ghars)
The same ikhtilaf applies to planting trees on agricultural land.
The ruling for planting follows the same principle as building.

Commentary
This passage addresses the exceptions that prevent rescission (faskh) of a fasid sale. The matn says 'unless the buyer sells or gives as gift or frees or builds.' The commentary explains in detail. First, verbal dispositions: if the buyer sells the item to a third party, rescission is prevented, provided: (1) no option of condition in the second sale, (2) the second sale is valid (not fasid), (3) the buyer is not selling back to the original seller. The rationale is that the third party's right takes precedence over the original seller's right to reclaim. Similarly, if the buyer gives the item as a gift, charity, or pawns it. Freeing a slave (and its equivalents: tadbir, kitabah, making umm walad) also cuts off rescission. An exception is made for ijara and nikah: ijara is weak and can be rescinded for excuse (the fasid sale is an excuse); nikah does not prevent rescission of the sale because it pertains to usufruct while sale is on the corpus. Physical acts: building on real estate or turning it into a mosque. Here, Abu Hanifa holds that the seller's right to reclaim is cut off, and the buyer pays the value. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the seller can still reclaim and the buyer must remove the building. The commentary presents arguments for both sides. Key Terms
تصرف (tasarruf): Physical act (building) that may cut off rescission.
حق الاسترداد (haqq al-istirdad): Seller's right to reclaim.
شبهة (shubhah): Resemblance between building and sale regarding permanence.

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, if a buyer builds on a fasid property, what is the seller entitled to?
A) The seller is entitled to the value of the land only.
B) The seller is entitled to reclaim the land and have the building removed.
C) The seller is entitled to half of the building.
D) The seller has no rights because building cuts off rescission.

Key Takeaway
Building on a fasid purchase creates an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa sees it as cutting off rescission, while the Sahibayn do not.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Buyer's Physical Actions and Seller's Reclaim Right in Voidable Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on irreversible actions voiding seller's right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a buyer's physical actions (building, freeing a slave) in a voidable sale cut off the seller's right to reclaim, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Irreversible actions by the buyer can terminate the seller's reclaim right under Abu Hanifa, but not under the Sahibayn.

The validity of a voidable sale is not automatically final; it can be rescinded. However, if the buyer performs certain irreversible physical actions, such as building on the land or freeing a slave, the question arises whether the seller can still reclaim the item. This lesson parses the intra-Hanafi dispute, focusing on the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The scenario is a voidable sale (bay' fasid), e.g., a sale lacking a condition. The buyer takes possession and then performs an irreversible action like building or freeing a slave.
The sale itself is not valid but the buyer's actions may affect rescission.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer's building or freeing cuts off the seller's right to reclaim. The buyer must pay the item's value.
The rationale: the action is permanent and was done with the seller's permission (by allowing possession).
3
Identify the Sahibayn's counter-position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the seller's right remains; the building must be removed and the item returned.
They analogize to the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) which is not cut off by the buyer's actions.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa relies on the seller's permission (taslit) and the permanence of the action. The Sahibayn emphasize the seller's superior right.
This is rational evidence (dalil aqli) based on analogical reasoning and public interest.
5
Determine the practical outcome and mufta bihi
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer keeps the item and pays value; under the Sahibayn, the seller can demand restoration. Later Hanafi authorities prefer Abu Hanifa's view as mufta bihi.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the fate of the building/improvements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يُحرّر أو يبني، وله أن يمنع المبيع عن البائع، حتى يأخذ الثمن منه، وطاب للبائع أي المشتري لا للمشترى، ولو ادّعى على آخر دراهم، فقضاها إياه، العبد المبيع وتدبر أو يكاتب أي للمشتري ما ربح أي الذي ما ربح في مبيع يتعين شخص أي شخص آخر أي الدراهم المدعي أي حل الثالث: أن يكون من غير بائعه، فلو باعه منه كان نقضاً للأول، ومنها: أن يهب المشتري المبيع ويسلمه بأن كان عبداً مثلاً، فوهبه لرجل، وكذلك لو تصدق به أو رهنه؛ لتعلق حق الغير بالمبيع، ومنها: أن يحرر، ومثل # التحرير توابعه من التدبير، والكتابة والاستيلاد. ما عداهما والحاصل: : أن تصرفات المشتري كلها نافذة فيه، ينقطع بها حق البائع في الاسترداد، سواء كان تصرفاً يقبل الفسخ أولا يقبله إلا الإجارة والنكاح؛ لأن الإجارة عقد ضعيف تفسخ بالعذر، وفساد الشراء عذر، والنكاح لا يمنع فسخ البيع؛ لأن له شبهاً بالإجارة بوروده على المنفعة، والبيع يرد على ملك الرقبة، والفسخ كذلك، فتعلق حق الزوج بالمنفعة، لا يمنع الفسخ على الرقبة، فيفسخ، ويرد على البائع، ويكون النكاح على حال، وفي من التصرفات ينقطع حق الاسترداد. (عيني، فتح، عناية) أو يبني بأن كان عقارا فبنى فيه، فهذه التصرفات تمنع الفسخ (عيني)) شروع فيما يمنع حق الاستراد من الأفعال الحسية، أي لو بنى المشتري في دار اشتراها شراء فاسداً أو اتخذها مسجداً، عليه قيمتها وينقطع حق الاسترداد عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا ينقطع حق الاسترداد، بل يقلع البناء ويرد الدار على صاحبها؛ لأن حق أضعف من حق البائع؛ لأنه يحتاج فيه إلى القضاء، ويبطل بالتأخير، ولا يورث، بخلاف حق البائع، حق ضعفه لا يبطل بالبناء، فحق البائع أولى وله أن البناء حصل بتسلط البائع، وهو مما يقصد به الدوام، فينقطع حقه في الاسترداد كالبيع بخلاف حق الشفيع؛ لأنه لم يوجد منه التسليط، ولهذا لا يسقط بيع المشتري وهبته فكذا ببنائه، وعلى هذا الخلاف الغرس، وعلى أصل الأئمة الثلاثة لا ينقطع حق الاسترداد، بل يسترد البائع بهذه التصرفات. (عيني، فتح)
Or he frees or builds, and he has the right to prevent the sold item from the seller until he takes the price from him, and it is permissible for the seller? (meaning the buyer) not for the purchased item, and if one claimed against another dirhams and he paid them to him, the sold slave and he arranges a tadbir or kitaba for him? meaning for the buyer? the profit? meaning that? the profit in a specific sold item? a specific person? meaning another person? meaning the dirhams? the claimant? meaning solution? the third: that it be from other than his seller, for if he sold it from him it would be a revocation of the first, and among them: that the buyer gifts the sold item and delivers it, such as if it were a slave, so he gifted him to a man, and likewise if he gave charity with it or pawned it; due to the attachment of the right of a third party to the sold item, and among them: that he frees, and like the tahrir its corollaries of tadbir, kitaba, and istilad. Other than them? And the conclusion: that all of the buyer's dispositions are effective in it, causing the seller's right to reclaim to be cut off, whether the disposition is subject to revocation or not, except ijarah and nikah; because ijarah is a weak contract that is dissolved by an excuse, and the invalidity of the purchase is an excuse, and nikah does not prevent the rescission of the sale; because it resembles ijarah in that it pertains to benefit, and the sale pertains to ownership of the corpus, and rescission likewise, so the husband's right attaches to the benefit and does not prevent rescission on the corpus, so it is rescinded and returned to the seller, and the marriage remains, and among the dispositions that cut off the right of reclaim. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inayah) Or builds if it were real estate and he built in it, these dispositions prevent rescission ('Ayni) Beginning of what prevents the right of reclaim from physical acts, i.e., if the buyer built in a house he purchased with a voidable purchase or made it a mosque, he owes its value and the right of reclaim is cut off according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the right of reclaim is not cut off, rather the building is removed and the house is returned to its owner; because the right (of the buyer) is weaker than the right of the seller; because it requires litigation, is invalidated by delay, and is not inherited, unlike the seller's right, the weakness of his right does not cause it to be invalidated by building, so the seller's right is more worthy, and because the building occurred with the seller's permission and it is intended for permanence, so his right of reclaim is cut off like a sale, unlike the right of the shafi'; because he did not grant permission, and therefore the buyer's sale and gift do not invalidate it, nor does his building. And upon this difference is also planting. And according to the principle of the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) the right of reclaim is not cut off, rather the seller reclaims despite these dispositions. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the effect of the buyer's physical actions (such as building or freeing a slave) on the seller's right to reclaim the sold item in a voidable sale. It is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that such actions terminate the seller's right to reclaim because they are irreversible and imply the buyer's ownership, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the seller's right remains and the buyer must restore the item, removing any additions. The passage also lists other dispositions like gift, charity, and pawn that similarly cut off the right. Exceptions are ijarah (lease) and nikah (marriage), which do not prevent rescission due to their nature pertaining to benefits rather than the corpus. The author concludes with a reference to the three Imams (including the Sahibayn) stating that the seller can reclaim despite these actions, but notes the difference with Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
التحرير (al-tahrir): Freeing a slave; one of the irreversible actions by the buyer that cuts off the seller's right to reclaim.
التدبير (al-tadbir): A contract in which a master declares that his slave will be free upon his death. It is covered by the same ruling as tahrir.
الكتابة (al-kitaba): A contract of manumission in which the slave pays a sum to the master over time. The buyer's action of writing a kitaba for the slave also prevents reclamation.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The status of a slave woman who bears her master's child; she becomes a umm walad and is free upon the master's death, and during his life she cannot be sold. This is also considered irreversible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens if a buyer builds on land purchased in a voidable sale?
A) The building is removed and the land returned to the seller.
B) The buyer must pay the value of the land and keeps the building; seller's right is cut off.
C) The sale is automatically valid.
D) The buyer must destroy the building immediately.

Key Takeaway
In a voidable sale, irreversible actions by the buyer (building, freeing, etc.) cut off the seller's right to reclaim under the mufta bihi view of Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn disagree.

Buyer's Right to Detain the Sold Item in a Voidable Sale
Rule on detention and priority in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the buyer's right to detain the sold item in a fasid sale and explain the analogy to pledge.

Core Idea
In a voidable sale, the buyer may detain the sold item like a pledge until the price is returned.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains that after rescinding a voidable sale (fasid), the buyer has the right to keep the sold item until the seller returns the price. This right is analogized to a mutual rescission in a valid sale and to the rules of pledge (rahn). The ruling also covers the scenario where the seller dies, giving the buyer priority over burial expenses and other creditors regarding that specific item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the buyer's right? It is the right to detain (habs) the sold item after the fasid sale is rescinded. The ruling is that this detention is valid and the buyer is entitled to it.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to two cases: (a) mutual rescission in a valid sale, where each party returns the consideration, and (b) a pledge (rahn), where the item is held as security for the debt.
The text explicitly says: 'li-anna al-mabī' maqbūl bi-hi, fa-yasīru maḥbūsan bi-hi ka-l-rahni'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The sold item is held in exchange for the price, just as a pledge is held as security for a debt. Additionally, in a mutual rescission of a valid sale, each party has the right to retain their consideration until the other receives theirs.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that a voidable sale does not grant any right to detain; the item must be returned immediately without conditions.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different views of the nature of fasid contracts.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi because the contract, though voidable, transfers possession and creates a right to compensation. The analogy to pledge is strong, and the buyer's possession is protected.
Authors of Fath al-Qadir and Ramz al-Haqa'iq confirm this as the most correct view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله أن يمنع المبيع إلخ: بعد الفسخ اعتبارا للفاسد بالجائز إذا تفاسخا بعد قبض العوضين، وعلى هذا الإجارة والرهن الفاسدان، وإن مات البائع أو المؤجر أو الراهن فالمشتري والمستأجر والمرتهن أحق بما في يده من العين ويقدم على تجهيزه وغرمائه، إلا أن الرهن مضمون بقدر الدين فما فضل فللغرماء. وقوله : "حتى" يأخذ الثمن منه"، أي من البائع؛ لأن المبيع مقابل به، فيصير محبوساً به كالرهن، فإن كان قائما أخذ عينه؛ لتعين الدراهم في البيع الفاسد وهو الأصح؛ لأنه بمنزلة الغصب، وإن كان مستهلكا أخذ مثله رمز الحقائق: ٤٤/٢] (فتح) وطاب للبائع إلخ: أي لو اشترى شيئاً يتعين بالتعيين بما لم يتعين كالدراهم والدنانير، وربح كل واحد منهما، حل للبائع ما ربح في الثمن، و لم يحل للمشتري ما ربح في المبيع؛ لأن العقد يتعلق بما يتعين فيتمكن الخبث فيه ولا يتعلق العقد الثاني بما لا يتعين، بل يجب مثله في الذمة، فلا يتمكن الخبث فيه فلا يجب التصدق به، وهذا في الخبث
And he has the right to prevent the sold item etc.: after rescission, treating the voidable (sale) like the permissible (sale) when they mutually rescind after receiving the two countervalues, and upon this (analogy) are the voidable lease and pledge. And if the seller, lessor, or pledgor dies, the buyer, lessee, or pledgee has a stronger right to the specific item in his hand and is preferred over (the seller's) burial expenses and other creditors, except that a pledge is secured up to the amount of the debt, so whatever is surplus goes to the creditors. And his statement: "until he takes the price from him", i.e., from the seller, because the sold item is in exchange for it, so it becomes detained by it like a pledge. If it (the price) is still in existence, he takes it in kind because dirhams are specific in a voidable sale, and this is the most correct (view) because it is like usurpation. And if it is consumed, he takes its equivalent. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 44/2) (Fath) And it is permissible for the seller etc.: i.e., if he bought something that becomes specified by specification with something that does not become specified, like dirhams and dinars, and each of them made a profit, it is permissible for the seller (to keep) the profit he made on the price, and it is not permissible for the buyer the profit he made on the sold item; because the contract attaches to what is specified, so corruption can occur in it, and the second contract does not attach to what is not specified, rather its like is due in the dhimma, so corruption does not occur in it, so it is not obligatory to give it in charity. And this applies to corruption.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explained by Fath al-Qadir, addresses two issues in a voidable sale: (1) the buyer's right to detain the sold item until he receives the price he paid, analogizing it to a pledge; if the price is still in existence (being specific dirhams/dinars), the buyer takes it in kind; if consumed, he takes its equivalent. This applies also to voidable leases and pledges. (2) The ruling on profit: in a fasid sale where the price is something non-fungible like dirhams (which are specific in the contract), the seller may keep the profit he earned from using the price (e.g., by trading it), because the price is a specific item and the buyer's right is to the specific item. However, the buyer's profit from the sold item is not permissible for him, because the sold item is tainted by the voidable contract and must be returned; any profit from it must be given in charity. The commentary also notes that if the death of a party occurs, the buyer is preferred over the seller's burial expenses and other creditors regarding the specific item in his possession.

Key Terms
الحبس (al-habs): The buyer's right to keep the sold item in his possession until the seller returns the price.
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fāsid): A sale that is defective in one of its essential conditions (like uncertainty in price) but still transfers possession; it is voidable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the seller dies after a fasid sale is rescinded, the buyer's right to the specific sold item is:
A) Subordinate to burial expenses and other creditors.
B) Equal to other creditors.
C) Preferred over burial expenses and other creditors.
D) Void because the sale is void.

Key Takeaway
In a voidable sale, the Hanafi school grants the buyer a right to detain the sold item as security for the price, with priority over the seller's other obligations.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Ruling on Profit in a Voidable Sale
Rule on profit distribution in fasid sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the seller may keep profit from the price but the buyer must give profit from the sold item in charity in a fasid sale where the price is specified.

Core Idea
Profit from the price is permissible for the seller; profit from the sold item must be given in charity.

The second part of the passage addresses a scenario where two parties in a fasid sale have each made a profit from the countervalues. The ruling depends on whether the countervalue is specific (mu'ayyan) or non-specific. The key is that khubth (taint) attaches only to specific items that are the direct subject of the voidable contract. Thus, the seller's profit from the price (which is specific) is permissible, but the buyer's profit from the sold item is tainted and must be given in charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the scenario
The scenario: A buyer purchases a specific commodity with specific dirhams (the price becomes specified in a fasid sale). Both parties make a profit from what they received. The ruling: The seller may keep the profit he earned from the price; the buyer must give any profit from the sold item in charity.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is based on the nature of the contract: The fasid contract attaches to the specific price, so taint (khubth) occurs there. The buyer's profit from the sold item is tainted because the contract is voidable and the sold item is subject to return. The seller's profit from the price is not tainted because the price, though specific, is not the subject of the voidable contract in the same way; the seller's right to the price is transferred.
The text says: 'li-anna al-‘aqd yata‘allaqu bi mā yata‘ayyanu fa-yatamakkinu al-khubth fīhi'.
3
Trace the distinction between specific and non-specific
In a fasid sale, dirhams become specific (mu'ayyan). The contract attaches to that specific pile, so any profit from the sold item (the buyer's profit) is tainted. The price, being specific, is considered like a specific object, but the seller's profit from it is not tainted because the seller's claim is on the specific price, and any profit made by the seller from that price is not derived from the voidable transaction directly.
4
Understand the concept of khubth
Khubth (spiritual corruption) is the taint that necessitates giving profit in charity. It attaches only when the contract is voidable and the item is specific (mu'ayyan). If the item is not specific (like fungible goods), the profit is not tainted because the contract does not attach to a specific individual item.
The text says: 'lā yatamakkinu al-khubth fīhi, fa-lā yajibu al-taṣadduq bihī'.
5
Apply the ruling to the parties
Seller: Profit from the price is halal (permissible) to keep. Buyer: Profit from the sold item must be given in charity (ṣadaqah). This is because the sold item is the direct subject of the voidable contract and is tainted.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explained by Fath al-Qadir, addresses two issues in a voidable sale: (1) the buyer's right to detain the sold item until he receives the price he paid, analogizing it to a pledge; if the price is still in existence (being specific dirhams/dinars), the buyer takes it in kind; if consumed, he takes its equivalent. However, the buyer's profit from the sold item is not permissible for him, because the sold item is tainted by the voidable contract and must be returned; any profit from it must be given in charity. The commentary also notes that if the death of a party occurs, the buyer is preferred over the seller's burial expenses and other creditors regarding the specific item in his possession.

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khubth): Spiritual corruption or impurity in the transaction that necessitates giving the profit in charity.
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification of the consideration (e.g., a specific pile of dirhams) in the contract, making the contract attach to that particular item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a buyer in a fasid sale sells the sold item and makes a profit, what must he do with that profit?
A) Keep it, because the sale is voidable, not void.
B) Give it in charity because the sold item is tainted.
C) Return it to the seller.
D) Split it with the seller.

Key Takeaway
In a fasid sale, the seller may keep profit from the price, but the buyer must give profit from the sold item in charity due to khubth attaching to the specific item.

Profit from a Debt That Lapses: Ownership and Its Taint
Ruling on profit from a debt that later proves false

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit from a debt that later proves false and explain the distinction between fasad al-milk and 'adam al-milk.

Core Idea
Profit from a later-disproven debt is halal because the initial receipt was based on valid ownership under fasad al-milk.

Imagine you sue someone for a debt, collect payment, invest it, and later both parties agree the debt never existed. Should you give away your profit? According to the Hanafi school, you may keep it. This ruling hinges on a subtle distinction between corrupted ownership (fasad al-milk) and nonexistent ownership ('adam al-milk). In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning and the qiyas analogy used by the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the passage to understand the case: defendant pays a claimed debt, plaintiff profits, then they agree there was no debt. The ruling: the profit is permissible for the plaintiff.
The ruling applies only when the initial payment was based on mutual acknowledgment of a debt.
2
Recognize the source of taint
The passage says the taint (khubth) comes from fasad al-milk (corruption of ownership), not from absolute non-ownership. Distinguish these two concepts.
Fasad al-milk means ownership is defective but still conveys lawful profit; 'adam al-milk means no ownership at all.
3
Apply the distinction to the case
At the time of receipt, the plaintiff owned the money because the debt was mutually acknowledged. Later agreement does not retroactively nullify that ownership; hence the profit is his.
4
Analyze the qiyas analogy
The passage uses an analogy: buying a slave with a female slave, freeing the slave, then the female is reclaimed. The freeing is valid because the slave was owned at the time. Similarly, the plaintiff's profit is valid because the money was owned at receipt.
The shared illa (effective cause) is ownership at the time of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصادقا أنه لا شيء عليه طاب له ربحه. المدعي والمدعى عليه المدعى عليه شيء عليه : أي على المدعى عليه من تلك الدراهم التي أداها إلى المدعي . (عيني) طاب له ربحه: أي لو ادعى شخص على شخص آخر ،مالا فقضى المدعى عليه المال إلى المدعي وتصرف المدعي القابض فيه وربح، ثم تصادقا أنه لا دین عليه، حل للمدعي ما ربح؛ لأن الخبث لفساد الملك؛ إذ الدين وجب بتصادقهما أولا فملكه، ثم ظهر بالتصادق أنه لا دين عليه، وبدل المستحق مملوك، ألا ترى أنه لو باع عبدا بجارية فأعتقه المشتري ثم استحقت الجارية لا يبطل العتق في العبد، ولولا أنه مملوك لبطل. (عيني، فتح)
They agree that he owes nothing, his profit becomes permissible for him. The claimant and the defendant. The defendant. Something on him: i.e., on the defendant from those dirhams that he paid to the claimant. (Ayni) His profit becomes permissible for him: i.e., if a person claims money from another, and the defendant pays the money to the claimant, and the claimant, having received it, uses it and makes a profit, then they agree that there was no debt, then it is permissible for the claimant (to keep) what he profited; because the taint is due to corruption of ownership; because the debt was established by their initial agreement, so he owned it, then it appeared by their mutual agreement that there was no debt, and the substitute for that which was due is owned. Do you not see that if he sold a slave for a female slave, and the buyer freed the slave, then the female slave is reclaimed (by a third party), the freeing of the slave is not invalidated; if it were not owned, it would be invalidated. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a scenario where a defendant pays a claimed debt, and later both parties agree that there was actually no debt. The plaintiff had earned profit from that money in the meantime. The ruling: the profit is permissible (halal) for the plaintiff. The reason given is that the taint (khubth) in such a situation is due to corruption of ownership (fasad al-milk), not due to absence of ownership. Initially, when the defendant paid, the plaintiff owned the money because the debt was mutually acknowledged. Later, when they agree there was no debt, the money becomes like a substitute for the rightful owner (the defendant) but is treated as owned; thus the profit from it is permissible. The analogy given: if someone buys a slave for a female slave, frees the slave, then the female slave is found to belong to someone else (i.e., the sale is void as to the female slave), the buyer's freeing of the slave remains valid because the slave was considered owned at the time of freeing. Similarly, the plaintiff's ownership of the money at the time of receiving it was valid, so the profit is his.

Key Terms
فساد الملك (fasad al-milk): Corruption of ownership; a situation where the ownership is defective but not absent. Here, the plaintiff's ownership is considered valid at the time of receipt.
بدل المستحق (badal al-mustahaqq): Substitute for what is rightfully due. Here, the money paid by the defendant becomes a substitute that is owned by the plaintiff initially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if a defendant pays a claimed debt and later both agree there was no debt, what must the plaintiff do with the profit he earned from that money?
A) Give the profit to charity
B) Keep the profit
C) Return the profit to the defendant
D) Destroy the profit

Key Takeaway
Profit from a debt that later proves false is halal because the initial ownership was valid under the concept of fasad al-milk.

The Prohibition of Najsh and Sawm in Sales
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on najsh and sawm, identify the hadith evidence, and apply the condition that distinguishes prohibited sawm from permissible auction.

Core Idea
Najsh and sawm are prohibited (makruh tahrimi) but the sale remains valid; deception is the root cause.

In this lesson, we examine two prohibited practices in sales: najsh (artificial bidding) and sawm (overbidding after a price is settled). The Hanafi school rules both as makruh tahrimi – the act is sinful but the sale itself is valid. We will explore the hadith evidence and the critical condition that determines when sawm becomes permissible as an auction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
First, define najsh (bidding without intent to buy) and sawm (offering a higher price after the seller has accepted the first buyer's offer). The ruling for both is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
The sale is valid but the person committing najsh or sawm earns sin.
2
Locate the hadith evidence
The prohibition is based on two hadith: one from Abu Huraira prohibiting najsh, and another from the Prophet prohibiting offering over a brother's offer (sawm). Both are narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad.
The hadiths use a general prohibition (nahy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In Hanafi usul, a clear prohibition (nahy) indicates makruh tahrimi unless there is evidence for a higher level (haram). Here, the prohibition is linked to deception and harm, so it is makruh tahrimi.
The sale itself is not nullified because the prohibition is not on the contract's essential elements.
4
Understand the condition for sawm
Sawm is only prohibited when the seller's heart is inclined to the first buyer's price. If the seller has not yet accepted or is not satisfied, then offering a higher price is permissible as an auction (bay' al-yazid).
The hadith's mention of 'brother' is for emphasis; the ruling includes non-Muslims as well.
5
Apply the ruling to modern scenarios
Recognize that shill bidding in online auctions corresponds to najsh and is prohibited. On the other hand, a live auction where no prior price is settled is permissible. The key is whether the seller has already committed to a buyer.
If in doubt, the condition of seller's satisfaction determines if sawm applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Najsh: (with two fatha or sukun on the jim) it is that you bid for a commodity at a price higher than its value while you do not intend to buy it, so that others see you and fall into it. (Muslim?) He (the author) attached it to the fasid (category) because the dislike in these places is prohibitively disliked, and he delayed it because it is less severe than (the other) in terms of invalidity of the contract; because the invalidity (in najsh) is due to a meaning not in the contract itself or its conditions of validity, so it is valid, and its dislike is understood when the bidder is seeking (the item) at its value; if he seeks it at a discount, there is no harm in increasing until it reaches its value. The reason for its dislike is what Abu Huraira reported: "... that it is upon her? that the town-dweller should not sell for the Bedouin, and that you should not engage in najsh", narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 44/2) And because that causes a person to fall into (buying) at more than the price, and it is deception, and deception is ugly and adjacent to this sale, so it is disliked. (Fath, 'Inayah). And offering over another's offer etc.: due to his statement (peace be upon him): "A man should not propose over his brother's proposal, nor should he offer over his brother's offer", narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. Even if the other is a dhimmi or a musta'min, and the mention of 'brother' in the hadith is not a condition but rather for emphasis. It is only disliked if the seller's heart inclines to sell at the price that the buyer named; if his heart does not incline and he is not satisfied with it, then there is no harm for someone else to buy it at a higher price, because this is 'sale of the highest bidder' which is permissible. Anas said: "He ('Umar) sold a cup and a blanket by auction", narrated by Ahmad and Tirmidhi. And because it is the sale of the poor, and there is a great need for it. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 45/2) (Fath). 'Over his brother's offer' means that the two parties are satisfied with the sale and the price is settled between them, and nothing remains but the contract, then (someone) increases (the price) and spoils his sale in order to buy (the item) for himself. (T, 'Ayni)

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses two prohibited practices in sales: najsh (artificial bidding) and sawm 'ala sawm ghayrihi (offering a higher price after a deal is practically settled). Both are declared makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), making the sale valid but sinful. The text first clarifies the distinction between fasad al-milk (corruption of ownership) and 'adam al-milk (absence of ownership), applied to profit from fasid sales. Then it defines najsh: bidding without intention to buy. The hadith from Abu Huraira prohibits it because it is deception. The ruling applies when the bidder is seeking the item at its fair value; if the bidder is trying to raise the price from a low starting point, it is allowed until it reaches market value. For sawm: it is offering a higher price after the two parties have agreed on the price and only the formal contract remains. The hadith forbids offering over a brother's offer, and this applies even to non-Muslims. However, if the seller has not yet accepted the first offer, then auction bidding (bay' al-yazid) is permissible, as practiced by 'Umar. The key is whether the seller's heart is inclined to the first offer; if not, no prohibition.

Key Terms
النجش (al-najsh): Bidding on an item with a high price without intending to buy, to lure others into buying at that price.
السوم على سوم الغير (al-sawm 'ala sawm al-ghayr): Offering a higher price for an item after the buyer and seller have already agreed on a price and are about to conclude the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Najsh and sawm are prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) in Hanafi fiqh, but the sale remains valid; the exception for auction (bay' al-yazid) applies when the seller has not yet accepted an offer.

Profit Halal When Debt Later Acknowledged Nonexistent
Ruling on profit with qiyas proof and khubth distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit when both parties later agree no debt existed and explain the qiyas proof using the slave emancipation example.

Core Idea
Profit is halal if ownership was valid at the time, even if the basis later disappears.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario: a plaintiff receives money under a debt claim, profits from it, and later both parties agree no debt ever existed. What happens to the profit? The Hanafi school rules it halal, relying on a qiyas (analogy) from a similar case in sale. The passage also distinguishes between two types of legal taint (khubth) that affect how profits and charity are handled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening statement: if both affirm no debt, the profit is halal for the plaintiff. Note that this is the Hanafi position.
The ruling is clear and unconditional.
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The author provides a qiyas: a slave sold for a female slave, the buyer frees the slave, then the female slave is reclaimed. The emancipation remains valid. What is the illa (effective cause)?
The proof shows that valid ownership at the time of action is not retroactively invalidated.
3
Extract the Illa
The illa is that the property (slave or money) was considered owned at the time of the action (emancipation or profit). The later revelation does not nullify that ownership.
This is the reasoning that links the original case to the new case.
4
Apply to the New Case
Apply the same illa to the plaintiff's profit: since the money was owned when received (based on the belief in a debt), the profit derived from it is also owned.
The plaintiff's profit is thus halal.
5
Distinguish the Two Types of Khubth
The fasl (section) contrasts 'taint due to corruption of ownership' (fasad al-milk) with 'taint due to lack of ownership' (adam al-milk). In the first, profit is halal; in the second (e.g., usurped property), charity is required for the profit.
The distinction depends on whether the property was owned at the time of the transaction.

(Ayni, Fath)

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if a plaintiff receives money from a defendant under a claim, later the two agree no debt existed, the profit the plaintiff made from that money is halal for him. The reasoning: the taint (khubth) was in the ownership (fasad al-milk), not in the property itself. At the time of payment, both believed a debt existed, so ownership was established; later it becomes clear there was no debt, but the compensation (badal) of what was rightfully owned is considered owned. A proof through analogy: if someone sells a slave for a female slave, the buyer frees the slave, then the female slave is reclaimed as usurped, the emancipation is not invalidated—showing the slave was validly owned. The section (fasl) then distinguishes the taint due to corruption of ownership versus lack of ownership (like usurped property).

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khubth): Taint or impermissible aspect in a transaction, whether due to corruption of ownership (fasad al-milk) or lack of ownership ('adam al-milk).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, profit earned from property that was validly owned at the time remains halal even if the basis for ownership later disappears; the taint is in the ownership, not the property itself.

Disliked Sales: Najsh and Bidding Over Another's Bid
Ruling that najsh and overbidding are makruh tahrimi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on najsh and bidding over another's bid, cite the hadith proofs, and explain why the sale remains valid despite the prohibition.

Core Idea
Najsh and bidding over a settled bid are prohibited (makruh tahrimi) but the sale itself is valid because the defect lies in the means, not the contract.

This lesson examines two sales practices that are legally valid yet morally blameworthy: najsh (artificially inflating the price) and al-sawm 'ala sawm ghayrihi (bidding over another's bid after the seller has inclined). The Hanafi school classifies these as makruh tahrimi, meaning the act is sinful but the sale contract itself is not void. Understanding this distinction is crucial for differentiating between contractual invalidity and ethical violations in Islamic commercial law.

Guided Path
1
Define the two prohibited practices
Identify what najsh and sawm 'ala sawm ghayrihi mean based on the text.
Najsh: bidding without intent to buy, to deceive. Sawm 'ala sawm: bidding after seller's inclination.
2
Identify the hukm level
Recognise that the text explicitly says 'karaha' meaning makruh tahrimi.
The term 'makruh tahrimi' is indicated by the phrase 'أي تحريما' in the text.
3
Examine the proofs
Locate the hadith cited for each prohibition and understand their authority.
Hadith in Bukhari and Muslim prohibit both practices.
4
Analyse the validity of the sale
Understand why the sale remains valid despite the prohibition.
The corruption is not in the core contract or its conditions; it is an external ethical violation.
5
Apply the condition: when is bidding allowed?
Distinguish between prohibited overbidding and permissible auction (bay' min yazid).
If the seller has not inclined to the first bid, it is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكره النجش والسوم على سوم غيره
أي تحريما
أي وكره تحريما
النجش [بفتحتين وبسكون الجيم، وهو أن تستام السلعة بأزيد من ثمنها وأنت لا تريد شراءها؛ ليراك الآخر فيقع فيه. (مس)] ألحقه بالفاسد؛ لكون الكراهة في هذه المواضع تحريمية، وأخره؛ لأنه أوفى حالا منه في فساد العقد؛ لأن الفساد فيه لمعنى لا في صلب العقد ولا في شرائط الصحة فكان صحيحا وكراهته محمول على ما إذا كان الطالب يطلبها بقيمتها فإن طلبها بما نقص لا بأس بأن يزيد إلى أن تبلغ قيمتها. ووجه كراهته ما روى أبو هريرة الله : " أنه عليها فهي أن يبيع الحاضر للبادي، وأن يتناجشوا"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد. رمز الحقائق: ٤٤/٢] ولأن ذلك سبب لإيقاع رجل فيه بأزيد من الثمن، وهو خداع، والخداع قبيح جاور هذا البيع، فكان مكروهاً. (فتح، عناية)
والسوم إلخ : لقوله عليه: "لا يخطب الرجل على خطبة أخيه، ولا يسوم على سوم غيره"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد، وإن كان الغير ذميا أو مستأمنا، وذكر الأخ في الحديث ليس قيدا بل لزيادة التنفير، وإنما يكره إذا جنح قلب البائع إلى البيع بالثمن الذي سماه المشتري، أما إذا لم يجنح قلبه، ولم يرض به، فلا بأس لغيره أن يشتريه بأزيد منه؛ لأن هذا "بيع من يزيد"، ولا بأس به، وقد قال أنس : "إنه علي باع قدحاً وحلساً بيع من يزيد"، رواه أحمد والترمذي، ولأنه بيع الفقراء، والحاجة ماسة إليه رمز الحقائق: ٤٥/٢] (فتح) على سوم غيره وهو أن يرضي المتعاقدان بالبيع فيستقر الثمن بينهما ولم يبق إلا العقد، فيزيد عليه، ويبطل بيعه لإرادة شرائه. (ط، عيني)
And disliked are najsh and bidding over another's bid. i.e. tahrimi. i.e. disliked prohibitively. Najsh (with two fatha and sukun on the jim): it is that you bid on an item at more than its price while you do not intend to buy it, so that another sees you and falls into it. (Muslim) He attached it to the fasid (corrupt) because the dislikedness in these places is prohibitive, and he delayed it because its state is better than corruption of contract; because the corruption in it is due to a meaning not in the core of the contract nor in conditions of validity, so it is valid, and its dislikedness is interpreted as when the seeker seeks it at its value; if he seeks it at less, there is no harm in increasing until it reaches its value. The basis of its dislikedness is what Abu Hurairah narrated: 'Indeed upon them is that the townsman should not sell for the bedouin, and that you should not practice najsh.' Reported by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 44/2) And because that is a cause of trapping a man at more than the price, and it is deception, and deception is evil adjacent to this sale, so it is disliked. (Fath, Inayah) And bidding over another's bid: due to his saying: 'A man should not propose over the proposal of his brother, nor bid over his bid.' Reported by Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad, even if the other is a dhimmi or musta'min. Mentioning 'brother' in the hadith is not a restriction but for added repulsion. It is disliked only if the seller's heart inclines to sell at the price which the buyer named; if his heart does not incline and he is not pleased, then there is no harm for another to buy it at a higher price, because this is 'sale by increase' and there is no harm in it, and Anas said: 'Indeed Ali sold a cup and a saddle blanket by sale of increase.' Reported by Ahmad and Tirmidhi, and because it is the sale of the poor, and need is pressing for it. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 45/2) (Fath) 'Bidding over another's bid' is that the two parties are satisfied with the sale, so the price is settled between them and nothing remains except the contract, then someone increases over it and invalidates his sale due to his desire to buy it. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage states two types of disliked sales: najsh (artificially inflating the price) and al-sawm 'ala sawm ghayrihi (bidding over another's bid). Both are makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Najsh is defined as bidding on an item at more than its price without intending to buy, to deceive another into buying at that inflated price. The sale itself is valid because the corruption is not in the core of the contract, but it is disliked due to deception. The proof is the hadith of Abu Hurairah prohibiting najsh. Bidding over another's bid is also prohibited based on the hadith 'Do not bid over the bid of your brother.' This applies when the seller has already inclined to sell at the first buyer's price; if the seller has not inclined, then it becomes a permissible auction (bay' min yazid). The commentary specifies that the prohibition applies even if the other party is a dhimmi or musta'min, and that 'brother' is not a restriction.

Key Terms
النَّجْشُ (al-najsh): Bidding on an item at a higher price without intending to buy, to deceive another into buying at that price.
السَّوْمُ عَلَى سَوْمِ غَيْرِهِ (al-sawm 'ala sawm ghayrihi): Bidding or offering a price on an item after another buyer has already negotiated a price and the seller inclines to accept, in order to outbid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on najsh (artificially inflating price)?
A) The sale is invalid (batil).
B) The sale is valid but the act is makruh tahrimi.
C) The act is permissible (ja'iz).
D) The sale is fasid.

Key Takeaway
Najsh and bidding over a settled bid are prohibited acts, but the resulting sale remains valid; the prohibition targets deception, not the contract itself.

Meeting Caravans: The Ruling of Talqi al-Jalb
Ruling that intercepting trade caravans before market is makruh tahrimi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on talqi al-jalb and identify the conditions under which it is considered makruh tahrimi.

Core Idea
Intercepting trade caravans before market is makruh tahrimi only when it harms townspeople or involves price concealment.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the ruling on meeting incoming caravans to buy their goods before they reach the market—a practice known as talqi al-jalb. The Hanafi school does not consider it always prohibited; rather, it is disliked (makruh tahrimi) under specific conditions. Understanding these conditions is essential to apply the rule correctly in real-world trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The passage begins by stating that meeting caravans (talqi al-jalb) is makruh tahrimi. Note that the ruling is not absolute but conditional.
The term is defined linguistically, but the legal focus is on the harm-based conditions.
2
Examine the conditions for dislikedness
The text lists two factors that make the act disliked: (a) if it harms the townspeople by depriving them of goods or driving up prices, and (b) if the buyer conceals the market price from the incoming seller. Either factor alone can trigger the ruling.
Harm to townspeople is the primary condition; price concealment is an independent ground.
3
Synthesize the summary rule
The passage provides a clear summary: if the buyer conceals the price, the act is always disliked regardless of harm to townspeople. If no concealment, it is disliked only when it causes harm. Otherwise, it is not disliked.
This summarizes the conditional logic in a binary fashion.
4
Note the proof
The ruling is based on a hadith from Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade meeting caravans for buying. This is a sahih hadith, and the Hanafi school uses it as evidence for the ruling.
The hadith is reported by Bukhari and Muslim, making it strong evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتلقي الجلب
أي وكره تحريما
وتلقى الجلب بفتح اللام من جلب الشيء إذا جاء به من بلد إلى بلد، وهو في الأصل يستعمل في الأنعام، وقد يتوسع فيه فيطلق على جلب الأقوات إلى البلدان ويحتمل أن يكون جمع الجالب كالخدم جمع الخادم، ويحتمل أن يكون بمعنى المجلوب كالنشر بمعنى المنشور، فالمجلوب إذا قرب من بلد تعلق به حق العامة فيكره أن يستقبله العامة البعض ويشتريه ويمنع وهذا إنما يكره إذا كان يضر بأهل البلد، وإن كان لا يضر بأهله فإنه لا يكره إلا إذا أسر السعر على الواردين واشترى منهم رخص من سعر المصر وهم غير عاملين به فحينئذ يكره، والحاصل أنه إذا لبس السعر كره مطلقا ضر بأهل البلد أم لا ، وإن لم يلبس وضر بأهل البلد كره وإلا لا وكراهة التلقي لقول ابن مسعود : إنه عليها نهى عن تلقى الجلب للبيع أي الشراء، رواه البخاري ومسلم. (عيني، فتح)
And meeting the caravans. i.e. it is makruh tahrimi. Meeting the caravans (talqi al-jalb): with fatha on the lam of jalb, from bringing something from one country to another. It is originally used for livestock, and may be extended to bringing provisions to towns. It may be plural of jalb (importer) like khadam plural of khadim, or it may mean the imported goods like nashr meaning published. When the imported goods approach a town, the right of the public attaches to them, so it is disliked that some of the public go out to meet them and buy and prevent (others). This is disliked only if it harms the people of the town; if it does not harm them, it is disliked only if one conceals the price from the importers and buys from them cheaply while they are unaware of the market price; then it is disliked. In summary, if one conceals the price, it is absolutely disliked whether it harms the town people or not; if one does not conceal and it harms them, it is disliked; otherwise no. The dislikedness of meeting (caravans) is due to the saying of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Indeed upon them: he forbade meeting the caravans for selling, i.e. buying.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage rules on 'talqi al-jalb' (meeting caravans) as makruh tahrimi. The term refers to going out to meet incoming traders before they reach the market to buy their goods, thereby depriving the local market. The ruling is conditional: it is disliked when it harms the townspeople, or when the buyer conceals the market price from the seller. The proof is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud that the Prophet forbade meeting caravans for buying. The commentary explains linguistic aspects and the harm-based reasoning.

Key Terms
تَلَقِّي الْجَلَبِ (talqi al-jalab): Going out to meet incoming traders (caravans) before they reach the town market to buy their goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is meeting caravans (talqi al-jalb) not disliked according to Hanafis?
A) When the buyer conceals the market price.
B) When it does not harm the townspeople and the buyer does not conceal the price.
C) When the seller is a dhimmi.
D) It is always disliked.

Key Takeaway
Talqi al-jalb is makruh tahrimi only when it harms the townspeople or involves price concealment; otherwise, it is permissible.

Auction (Bay' Min Yazid) is Permissible
Ruling with brief dalil that auction is not makruh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on auction (bay' min yazid) and cite the hadith evidence for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Auction is permissible and not disliked, as proven by the Prophet's own practice.

This short passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common concern: is the auction-style sale (bay' min yazid) considered disliked? The text clarifies that it is not only permissible but also has prophetic precedent. Understanding this ruling helps distinguish it from the prohibited practice of outbidding after the seller has inclined to a buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The passage begins with 'لا بيع من يزيد' – this is the term for auction. Recognize that 'bay' min yazid' literally means 'sale of increase' where buyers increase the price competitively.
The word 'لا' here negates dislike, not permissibility.
2
Understand the ruling
The commentator explains 'أي لا يكره' – meaning it is not disliked (makruh). Determine the precise legal level: here it is mubah (permissible) or possibly khilaf al-awla? The text confirms permissibility without any dislike.
No mention of it being mustahabb; just not disliked.
3
Locate the proof
The evidence is a hadith of Anas that the Prophet sold a cup and a saddle blanket by auction. This reveals that the Prophet himself practiced this type of sale, establishing it as a valid transaction.
The dalil is a single hadith (ahad), so its thubut is zanni, but the ruling is clear.
4
Distinguish from prohibitions
Note the difference between this auction and the prohibited 'sawm 'ala sawm gharihi' (bidding over another's bid when the seller has already inclined). In auction, the seller has not yet accepted any offer, so competition is open and permissible.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بيع من يزيد
أي لا يكره
لما روي عن أنس عنه أن رسول الله باع قدحاً وجلساً بيع من يزيد. (عناية)
No sale of increase. i.e. it is not disliked. Due to what was reported from Anas that the Messenger of Allah sold a cup and a saddle blanket by sale of increase. (Inayah)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage clarifies that a sale by increase (bay' min yazid), i.e., an auction where bidders increase the price competitively, is not disliked. The proof is that the Prophet himself conducted such a sale. This is contrasted with the earlier prohibition of bidding over another's bid when the seller has already inclined; auction is a different context where buyers openly compete.

Key Terms
بَيْعُ مَنْ يَزِيدُ (bay' man yazid): A sale where the seller offers an item and buyers compete by increasing the price; an auction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on an auction (bay' min yazid)?
A) It is makruh tahrimi.
B) It is permissible and not disliked.
C) It is haram.
D) It is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
Auction (bay' min yazid) is a permissible sale in the Hanafi school, supported by the Prophet's own practice, and is not to be confused with the prohibited outbidding after seller's inclination.

Separation of Family Members in Sale Prohibited
Ruling that separating family members in a sale is makruh tahrimi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on separating family members in a transaction and identify the evidence (dalil) used by the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Separating a mother from her child or siblings in a sale is makruh tahrimi — the Prophet cursed such a separation.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a social and ethical dimension of transactions: the prohibition of separating close family members. The ruling is derived from a hadith where the Prophet cursed those who separate a mother and child or siblings. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and its scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being regulated and the legal classification (hukm). The passage states that a Muslim seller must not separate family members in a transaction, and this is makruh tahrimi.
The act is 'al-tafriq' (separation) of close kin (mother-child, siblings) through a transaction.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
Find the proof cited. The author quotes a hadith from Abu Musa that the Prophet cursed those who separate a mother from her child or a brother from his brother.
The hadith is from Ibn Majah and al-Daraqutni, classified as ahad (single-narrator), but its meaning is supported by the general spirit of Islamic law.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
Explain how the hadith proves the ruling. The use of 'curse' in the hadith indicates a major sin, and in Hanafi usul, a curse implies tahrim (prohibition), hence the ruling is makruh tahrimi.
The ruling is not haram because the hadith's authenticity and indication are zanni; but being makruh tahrimi still means it is sinful and must be avoided.
4
Note the scope of the prohibition
Identify who is addressed and what transactions are included. The ruling applies only to Muslims; non-Muslims are not addressed. It applies to sale, gift, inheritance division, booty, and auction.
The text uses 'لا يفرق' (must not separate) and explains it covers various transfers.
5
Apply the ruling
Practice applying the ruling to a scenario. For example, a Muslim auctioneer who sells a mother and her child to different bidders commits makruh tahrimi.
The prohibition is not limited to the seller of the original item; any Muslim involved in the transaction that causes separation is responsible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يفرق البائع المسلم في البيع عن شرائه
أي وكره تحريما
ولا يفرق يعني المسلم، حراً كان أو مكاتباً أو مأذونا له في التجارة، بخلاف الكافر؛ لأن الكفار غير مخاطبين بالشرائع، عبر بالنفي مبالغة في المنع؛ إذ قد ورد عن أبي موسى قال: لعن رسول الله الله من فرق بين الوالدة وولدها، وبين الأخ وأخيه، رواه ابن ماجه والدار قطني. والمراد التفريق ببيع أو هبة أو قسمة ميراد راث أو غنيمة أو بيع من يزيد. (عناية)
And the Muslim seller must not separate in sale from his purchase. i.e. it is makruh tahrimi. 'He must not separate' meaning the Muslim, whether free, a mukatab, or authorized for trade, contrary to the disbeliever because disbelievers are not addressed by the shari'ah. He expressed it by negation to emphasize prohibition. It has been reported from Abu Musa that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah cursed the one who separates between a mother and her child, and between a brother and his brother.' Reported by Ibn Majah and al-Daraqutni. What is meant is separation by sale, gift, division of inheritance, booty, or sale of increase. (Inayah)
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage prohibits a Muslim seller from separating close family members when selling. The matn 'ولا يفرق البائع المسلم في البيع عن شرائه' is understood as a prohibition against separating a mother from her child or siblings. The proof is the hadith of Abu Musa that the Prophet cursed those who separate such family members. The prohibition applies to Muslims only, not to non-Muslims who are not addressed by Islamic law. The separation can occur through various transactions like sale, gift, inheritance division, or auction. The ruling is makruh tahrimi.

Key Terms
التَّفْرِيقُ (al-tafriq): Separation of close family members (mother-child, siblings) through a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only Muslim sellers.
C) Only non-Muslim sellers.
D) Only free Muslim sellers.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim must not separate mother and child or siblings through any transaction, as the Prophet cursed such acts.

Intercepting Trade Caravans (Talqi al-Jalab): Conditions and Ruling
Explains ruling, conditions, and textual evidence for intercepting trade caravans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when intercepting a trade caravan is makruh tahrimi in the Hanafi school and identify the conditions that trigger the prohibition.

Core Idea
Intercepting trade caravans is disliked when it harms townspeople or conceals market price.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on intercepting trade caravans (talqi al-jalab). The passage first defines the term, then explains the conditions under which the act becomes makruh, supported by a prophetic hadith. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between harm to the public and price concealment.

Guided Path
1
Define the term and its scope
Understand that 'al-jalab' refers to goods brought from one town to another, originally livestock but extended to any provisions. The act is going out to meet these goods before they enter the town.
2
Identify the default ruling and the public right
Recognize that upon approach to the town, a public right (haqq al-‘ammah) attaches to the goods. Interception that prevents townspeople from access is problematic.
3
Distinguish the conditions that change the ruling
Note two independent triggers for makruh: (1) harm to the townspeople (e.g., raising prices or reducing supply), and (2) price concealment (labasa al-si'r). If neither is present, interception is not disliked.
The author emphasizes that concealment alone makes it disliked absolutely, even without harm.
4
Connect to the prophetic evidence
The hadith of Ibn Mas'ud (narrated by Bukhari and Muslim) states the Prophet forbade intercepting caravans for sale. In Hanafi usul, the imperative of prohibition (nahy) indicates makruh tahrimi unless overridden by absence of harm or concealment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتلقي الجلب، بفتح اللام من جلب الشيء إذا جاء به من بلد إلى بلد، وهو في الأصل يستعمل في الأنعام، وقد يتوسع فيه فيطلق على جلب الأقوات إلى البلدان ويحتمل أن يكون جمع الجالب كالخدم جمع الخادم، ويحتمل أن يكون بمعنى المجلوب كالنشر بمعنى المنشور، فالمجلوب إذا قرب من بلد تعلق به حق العامة فيكره أن يستقبله العامة البعض ويشتريه ويمنع وهذا إنما يكره إذا كان يضر بأهل البلد، وإن كان لا يضر بأهله فإنه لا يكره إلا إذا أسر السعر على الواردين واشترى منهم رخص من سعر المصر وهم غير عاملين به فحينئذ يكره، والحاصل أنه إذا لبس السعر كره مطلقا ضر بأهل البلد أم لا ، وإن لم يلبس وضر بأهل البلد كره وإلا ،لا وكراهة التلقي لقول ابن مسعود : إنه عليها نهى عن تلقى الجلب للبيع أي الشراء، رواه البخاري ومسلم.
And intercepting the camel herd (al-jalab) – with fath of the lam from 'jalaba al-shay' if he brought it from one country to another. It is originally used for livestock, but may be extended to the import of provisions to towns. It could be the plural of 'jalib' like 'khudam' plural of 'khadim', or it could mean 'majlub' (the imported goods) like 'nashr' meaning 'manshur'. When the imported goods approach a town, the public right attaches to them, so it is disliked for some members of the public to go out to meet them and buy them and prevent others. This is only disliked if it harms the people of the town. If it does not harm them, it is not disliked unless he conceals the price from the importers and buys from them cheaply while they are unaware of the town's price, then it is disliked. The conclusion: if he conceals the price, it is disliked absolutely whether it harms the townspeople or not. If he does not conceal and it harms them, it is disliked; otherwise not. And the prohibition of intercepting is due to the saying of Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade intercepting caravans for sale (i.e., purchase). Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling for intercepting trade caravans (talqi al-jalab). It first defines the term: al-jalab refers to goods brought from one town to another, originally livestock but extended to provisions. The legal ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) under certain conditions. The key principle is the public right (haqq al-'ammah) that attaches to goods when they approach a town. If the interception harms the townspeople by raising prices or depriving them of supply, it is disliked. Even without harm, if the interceptor conceals the local market price and buys cheaply from ignorant sellers, it is also disliked. The absolute case is when the interceptor dresses (confuses) the price (labasa al-si'r), then it is disliked regardless of harm. The author supports this with the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud reported in Bukhari and Muslim that the Prophet forbade intercepting caravans for sale, meaning purchase. The Hanafi school follows this prohibition as makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked) when conditions are met.

Key Terms
تلقي الجلب (talqi al-jalab): Going out to meet trade caravans before they reach the town to purchase their goods, often to resell at a higher price.
جلب (jalab): Goods brought from one place to another; originally livestock, but extended to any imported provisions.
لبس السعر (labasa al-si'r): Concealing or confusing the market price from the seller, buying at a lower price while the seller is unaware of the town's prevailing price.
حق العامة (haqq al-'ammah): The right of the public to access goods brought into the town at fair market prices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
